HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Dip As Durables Top Estimates

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Government securities slipped yesterday, in response to an unexpectedly strong manufacturing report and the weight of a fresh supply of Treasury debt.
The 30-year Treasury bond, the benchmark for many consumer and business loans, fell 5/32, to 95 18/32, for a yield of 6.98 percent, up from 6.96 percent on Tuesday, when the Federal Reserve raised its Federal funds target rate, or the overnight loan rate banks charge each other, to 5.50 percent, from 5.25 percent. 
 For the second consecutive month, durable goods orders exceeded expectations, a sign that manufacturing activity is humming along. Durable goods orders were up 1.5 percent in February after a revised 4.1 percent rise in January. Analyst forecasts had ranged from no change at allto a gain of one-tenth of 1 percent.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said, "After appearing to falter in both November and December, new orders for durable goods rebounded with a vengeance in January and February, which noticeably diminishes the possibility of any imminent slowdown in the durable goods sector."
That, he added, "further verifies the upside risks" that prompted the Fed "to adopt a less accommodative monetary posture."
Mr. Fine noted that the rise in durable goods -- items intended to last three years or more -- came despite declines in both civilian aircraft and motor vehicle orders, reflecting in part gains in communications equipment and electronics components. And equally important was the strong 1.1 percent February jump in the unfilled orders backlog on the heels of a similar one in January. He said that provided a base for continued growth in the output of durable goods.
Dr. Sherry Cooper, chief economist at Nesbitt Burns Securities Inc., said: "More solid economic reports such as this one will surely build on fears of further Fed tightening in the months ahead. Indeed, it is unlikely that a mere 25-basis-point hike in the Federal funds rate will be enough to slow a fully employed U.S. economy, which is clearly expanding at an above-potential rate of growth."
In yesterday's auction, the Treasury sold more than $26 billion in new one-year bills and five-year notes, with the results as expected. The average rate for a one-year bill was 5.66 percent, up from 5.36 percent last month, and the highest since April 27, 1995, when the yield was 5.90 percent.
Traders said the five-year note auction went well, with 6.660 percent the awarded high yield, up from 6.359 percent last month, and the highest since June 26, 1996, when it was 6.674 percent. While the bid-to-cover ratio -- the number of bids compared with the size of the sale -- was about average at 2.54 to 1, noncompetitive bidding was better than expected. It gave some support to the market, but sellers are using the gains as selling opportunities.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auctions of a new five-year note and new one-year bill:

HEADLINE: U.S. Rebuffed in Global Proposal For Eavesdropping on the Internet

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

 BODY:
In a setback for the Clinton Administration that demonstrates the difficulty of setting global policies for the Internet, the leading industrial nations have declined to embrace a United States proposal to allow computer eavesdropping by the world's law enforcement agencies.
The United States proposal, backed by Britain and France, was an attempt to restrict the private use of increasingly advanced data-scrambling technology that can protect the privacy of electronic mail and other forms of computer communication. The equipment can make it difficult for law enforcement officials to crack a code when they suspect it is masking criminal or terrorist activities. 
 The proposal called for international endorsement of a system in which mathematical keys to computer-security codes would be held by escrow agents from whom law enforcement officials could obtain the keys once they have a court's wiretapping warrant.
But policy guidelines scheduled to be released in Paris today by the 29-nation Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development fail to endorse the United States proposal. And they leave such leeway for members to regulate data-scrambling technology -- or not -- that computer security experts say any uniform international policy remains elusive.
"The difficulty with the guidelines is that anybody can interpret parts of them in their own way," said Konstantine Papanikdaw, a policy analyst for information security at the European Commission in Brussels.
Indeed, the industrial world seems to be deeply divided on whether governments can ever legitimately eavesdrop on the electronic communication of their citizens. Because messages on the Internet are easy to intercept, a growing number of individuals and corporations are protecting the privacy of their communications and the security of their commercial transactions by scrambling such information.
Some O.E.C.D. nations, including Britain and France, have either outlawed or are in the process of tightly regulating the private use of data-scrambling systems. But other nations -- including Australia, Canada, Denmark and Finland -- have policies that protect individual privacy. Among other member nations, Japan had initially resisted the United States proposal but was said to be moving closer to it, while Germany remained deeply divided.
Most other countries, inside or outside the O.E.C.D., have yet to confront the data-scrambling issue. And even the United States has a somewhat contradictory national policy that permits citizens to use whatever data-scrambling software they wish within the nation's borders, but restricts the export of the most up-to-date computer-coding technology.
That seeming contradiction, however, did not prevent the Clinton Administration in recent months from waging a vigorous behind-the-scenes effort for its proposal. And hoping to resolve some of the policy conflicts, the Administration is now circulating draft legislation on Capitol Hill which would attempt to control even the domestic use of data-scrambling software and establish a key-escrow system for the United States.
While the O.E.C.D. has no authority to set international policy, its recommendations are frequently used by member nations in setting their own foreign and trade policies. And the privacy and law-enforcement aspects of the Internet are issues on which member governments have been desperate for guidance.
But even though most of the O.E.C.D. discussions involved law enforcement officials, who have been the main advocates for measures that would insure their ability to crack codes, European officials say that there was never much agreement on what to do.
And so the primary recommendation in the report, a copy of which was obtained by The New York Times, simply gives O.E.C.D. member nations the latitude to do as they see fit when it comes to data scrambling, which is formally known as cryptography.
"National cryptography policies may allow lawful access to plain text, or cryptographic keys, or encrypted data," the report says.
Privacy-rights advocates see the O.E.C.D. guidelines as a critical setback for the Clinton Administration. "The U.S. proposal to endorse lawful access to private keys was explicitly rejected by the O.E.C.D. member countries," said Marc Rotenberg of the Washington-based Electronic Privacy Information Center and a member of the O.E.C.D.'s advisory group. "The O.E.C.D. chose instead a policy based on voluntary, market-driven development of cryptography products."
And even supporters of the United States position acknowledged that guidelines were a disappointment.
"The United States probably had more success raising consciousness then getting language that could be treated as an endorsement for key recovery," said Stewart Baker, a former National Security Agency official who participated on the American delegation to the O.E.C.D.
Meanwhile, executives for the United States computer industry were critical of the O.E.C.D. for even leaving the door open for governments to set national policies on data scrambling.
"We think that markets, not governments, should be the primary determinants of technology solutions," said Jon Englund, a vice president at the Information Technology Association of America, a trade group.
Many experts question whether governments can ever hope to insure law enforcement access to electronic messages or to restrict the spread of super-strong coding software, because new, more powerful versions can always be developed and easily transmitted over the Internet in the blink of an eye.
And any international effort is almost certainly doomed if some countries refuse to go along with a common approach, because people looking for strong encryption can simply acquire it wherever the laws are lax.
In fact, the big German company Siemens A.G. recently introduced an encryption system that it advertises as being much more powerful than American companies can export under United States law.
Besides the United States, France and Britain both support a system for enabling law enforcement officials to obtain keys to data-scrambling codes. France has already passed a stringent law that requires participation in such a system, although the rules to carry out the law have not yet been worked out.
And in recent days, Britain has quietly circulated the most restrictive proposal of any nation, a domestic policy under which the Government would allow private use only of cryptography that was officially licensed, to make sure that the software uses code that law enforcement officials can crack.
Under such laws, of course, criminals and terrorists might logically choose to use unauthorized encryption software. But the mere fact that such use would be a crime may be a deterrent -- or give the police grounds to arrest anyone whose communications were indecipherable.
In Germany, encryption remains a deeply divisive issue. The Interior Ministry has supported the need for encryption restrictions of some sort, but the Justice Ministry and the Economics Ministry have both signaled their opposition. And German businesses have been outspoken opponents against any new restrictions on data scrambling.
Meanwhile, United States export restrictions have been a boon for Brokat Informationssysteme G.m.b.H., a two-year-old start-up company in Boblingen, Germany. Brokat supplies secure electronic transaction software for banks like Deutsche Bank and on-line services like America Online in Europe.
One of Brokat's hottest products is the Expresso Security Package, which essentially adds strong encryption to the World Wide Web browsers and Internet server software sold by two of the largest American software companies -- Microsoft and Netscape Communications.

HEADLINE: Huge Indonesia Gold Strike May Have Been Exaggerated

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: OTTAWA, March 26

 BODY:
In a stunning reversal of fortune, a tiny Canadian company that once said it had made the world's richest gold strike in the jungles of Indonesia conceded today that it had probably overestimated the size of its find.
The company, Bre-X Minerals of Calgary, Alberta, told its prospective American partner in developing the site, Freeport-McMoran Copper and Gold of New Orleans, that estimates ranging from 70 million ounces of gold to as much as 200 million ounces at the Busang area of Indonesia may have been inflated because of invalid geologic sampling. 
 Last month, Freeport entered an agreement to become a partner of Bre-X and to build a $400 million mining complex, contingent on the results of its own analysis. Today the company said that it had drilled seven test holes and found "insignificant amounts of gold."
The announcements represent yet another twist in a tale with high political intrigue, fierce corporate power struggles, a mysterious fire and the strange death a week ago of the Bre-X executive directly responsible for estimating the amount of gold at Busang.
The expected reduction of gold estimates is considered evidence by some analysts that big mining companies, which had jockeyed for position to develop the Indonesian find with Bre-X, may have been taken in by a seemingly irresistible pitch.
Bre-X was not available to comment today, but as recently as Monday it stood behind its estimates.
The interest in Bre-X and its apparent gold strike went beyond just companies like Freeport-McMoran, Barrick Gold and Placer Dome to stock market investors, who have sent the value of Bre-X shares on a dizzying rise since the company began exploring at the Busang site, in the Indonesian region of Borneo, in 1993.
At that time, Bre-X shares sold for 30 cents, and the estimated amount of gold was one million ounces. The company's only real asset was its claim to Busang. But as the amount of gold thought to be there rose sharply, so did the value of the shares. At the height, Bre-X sold for $250. It was then split 10 for 1 and the value rose and fell on revised estimates of gold and continuing turmoil in securing the drilling rights from the Indonesian Government.
Today, trading of Bre-X stock was suspended on both the Toronto Stock Exchange and in the United States, where it is traded on Nasdaq. The shares closed on Tuesday at $15.50 (Canadian) in Toronto, and are expected to sink whenever trading resumes. On Nasdaq Tuesday, Bre-X shares closed at $11.375 (United States).
Trading in Freeport-McMoran Copper and Gold was suspended today on the New York Stock Exchange for more than four hours. When it resumed, those shares fell $2.25, or 7.1 percent, to $29.625.
Freeport, whose stock listing is FCX, said in a statement today that the analysis of the core samples was not yet complete and that it would continue to study the site. But while the company was not immediately walking away from the project, it expressed clear doubts.
"FCX will elect to participate in the development of the Busang project only if development is, in FCX's opinion, economically feasible," the company said.
Trying to determine how much gold lies underground by drilling holes only a few inches wide is one of the riskiest parts of mineral exploration, and discrepancies often occur, experts said. Still, the possibility that what last month had been considered one of the greatest gold finds in history may now not be even economically feasible has left mining analysts shaking their heads in disbelief.
But the other companies involved in the contest to win a deal to develop the find denied they had been tricked. "We never discovered an evidence of wrongdoing," said Vincent Borg, a spokesman for Barrick Gold, the world's second-largest producer, which attempted a hostile takeover of Bre-X by aligning itself with members of the Indonesian ruling family before its efforts collapsed. "Having said that, we never completed our due diligence."
Hugh Leggatt, a spokesman for Placer Dome of Vancouver, which also struggled for a piece of Busang, said: "If we had had the opportunity to be involved, we would have insisted on our own due diligence. But we never got to visit the property."
After a struggle between the companies lasting months, Freeport and Bre-X in February worked out a deal. Freeport, which has 30 years' experience mining in Indonesia, was to control 15 percent of the Busang project and be sole operator of the mine, upon satisfactory completion of its own site examination.
Bre-X retained 45 percent of the project and the rest went to the Indonesian Government and local companies aligned with President Suharto and his associates.
Indications of Freeport's wariness became evident last week in an Indonesian business publication, which reported that after drilling its own test holes, Freeport discovered serious discrepancies with Bre-X findings from core samples taken over the last few years.
At the same time, the Bre-X geologist who had been one of the principal discoverers of the Indonesian field died in what seemed to be suspicious circumstances when he fell from a helicopter in an area near the Busang site.
Last week, trading in Bre-X stock was suspended for a time. Company officials vehemently denied there was any doubt about the authenticity of its gold strike and threatened to sue publications that reported unsubstantiated allegations.
Company officials also said the geologist who had died, Michael de Guzman, had taken his own life by jumping out of the helicopter because he had recently been told that he was suffering from an incurable disease. There were also reports from Indonesia that a fire in January destroyed much of his work.
"The company's board of directors has absolute confidence in the integrity and accuracy of assay results," Bre-X's chairman, David G. Walsh, said in a statement on Tuesday. "Unfortunately, when the first ounce of gold is poured at Busang, I am sure naysayers will complain about the color."
In apparent response to the doubts raised, Bre-X hired an independent Canadian mining consultant last week to scrutinize the company's findings. Today, that consultant, Strathcona Mineral Services, notified Bre-X that some of the existing samples were invalid, and recommended an extensive audit. Such an audit will take about a month, Bre-X officials said.
Despite all this, Bre-X continued to have its supporters. As late as Tuesday, a Toronto mining analyst sharply defended the company's findings. The analyst, Egizio Bianchini, of Nesbitt Burns of Toronto, attributed the discrepancies to a difference in the way Bre-X and Freeport handled core samples, rather than to any shortage of gold.
"The gold is there," he told a reporter from The Globe and Mail of Toronto.
Bre-X's own samples had been analyzed by Kilborn Pakar Rekayasa, a respected engineering division of the SNC-Lavalin Group of Montreal. In its latest resource calculation, dated Feb. 17, Kilborn estimated that there were at least 70.9 million ounces of gold at Busang. But the company did not do its own drilling.
A few days after Kilborn released that latest estimate, Bre-X officials said during a conference call to mining analysts that they were confident there would turn out to be around 200 million ounces of gold that could be easily extracted from the Indonesian jungle.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Wall Street Shifts Focus To Earnings

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
Now that the Federal Reserve has raised its short-term interest rate target, Wall Street is turning its focus again to corporate earnings.
As the end of the first quarter approaches, though, small clouds are gathering around an earnings outlook that was already less than robust. 
 The dollar, which has risen 8 percent this year against the German mark and 7 percent against the Japanese yen, has already begun to eat into the profits of American multinationals.
And there are other concerns, such as pricing competition and wage pressures at home, slower economic growth in the United States and abroad, and fears that many large companies can no longer use restructuring to increase profitability.
"Earnings in 1997 will probably be weaker than people expect," said Richard Bernstein, director of quantitative research at Merrill Lynch. "Keep in mind, we're big bulls at Merrill. But we're seeing a slowing in the growth rate of earnings."
Industry analysts at Merrill Lynch -- those who follow individual companies -- are projecting that earnings of the companies in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index will grow by about 17 percent this year. But Mr. Bernstein believes those estimates are "overly optimistic."
Even if the downgrades are not large, some fear that falling expectations would further disrupt a stock market that has been staggering in a trading range since the Dow industrials first reached the 7,000-point level in mid-February.
"There's a general malaise hanging over the market," said Charles Pradilla, a market strategist at Cowen & Company. " Right now stocks are overvalued relative to bonds, and the strong dollar is not benefiting anyone."
Of course, there are those, like Abby Joseph Cohen at Goldman, Sachs & Company, who disagree with the weakening earnings scenario. Yet even Ms. Cohen concedes that slower growth in Europe could reduce her 10 percent earnings growth forecast to 7 percent.
And while Byron R. Wien, the United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley, has not revised his forecast yet, he has begun to rethink his modest earnings growth expectation of about 7 percent because of worries about the strong dollar.
"It's a significant problem," said Mr. Wien, who has already forecast that the stock market will fall sharply later this year. "We're going to see some impact on first-quarter earnings. The companies that will be hit are the multinationals."
Earlier this year, I.B.M. said the stronger dollar was curbing its profits and just last week Eastman Kodak said that revenue in the first two months of this year was flat compared with last year, due in part to the strong dollar. Shares of both stocks plummeted on the news.
A strong dollar hurts profits two ways. It makes American exports more expensive abroad, which can reduce sales. And when those foreign sales are translated back into a stronger dollar at the end of a quarter, they are worth less.
Analysts of the technology sector, which has the largest foreign exposure, have also begun to worry about currency translations and weakening demand in Europe, two factors that have helped exacerbate an already ferocious drop in technology stocks this year.
In addition to the dollar, price competition -- as suggested by McDonald's recent decision to lower the price of selected items -- could also reduce earnings.
And if pricing competition does not eat up profits, strategists like Charles Clough at Merrill Lynch suggest that other factors will. Although real sales growth gained about 2 percent a year in the 1990's, he said that earnings had grown by about 16 percent a year, largely because of "restructuring" or downsizing. "But our belief is that that string is running out," he said.
 But Ms. Cohen of Goldman, Sachs contends that the gloomy predictions are overstated, partly because a new formulation is at work in corporate America.
More than the strong dollar, she worries about a European economy that is in a "funk," burdened by unemployment and weak domestic activity. But those problems are offset, she said, by the fact that American companies are spread across the globe, including in countries where they benefit from a favorable currency exchange.
But even more important than the fact that many large companies hedge against bad times, Ms. Cohen said, is the fact that American products sold abroad often have a "value added" component.
"If you're selling steel, chemicals or newsprint, then your customers have options," she said. "But much of what we produce is value-added," such as Intel's pentium processor, which has few competitors in Europe and Japan.
"If we were going to see a significant dollar effect, we should have seen it in the fourth quarter," she said. "I think the surprise was that the dog didn't bark."
There are two ways of tracking earnings on Wall Street and both of them are slipping. "Bottom up" projections are based on analysts' estimates for individual companies while "top down" forecasts are made by strategists and economists using economic and industry figures to estimate overall earnings growth.
Traditionally, the "bottom up" and "top down" projections vary widely early in the year, with industry analysts more optimistic than market strategists. This year is no different. Industry analysts are projecting, on average, a 13.6 percent growth in earnings of the companies in the S.& P. 500, down from 14.4 percent a month ago. Top-down equity strategists are projecting 6.6 percent, down from 6.7 percent a month ago, according to First Call, which tracks earnings projections.
 But as the year progresses, the two forecasts get closer, usually with the bottom-up analysts reducing their forecasts, said Charles Hill, director of research at First Call.
"The critical question is not whether the industry analysts lower their numbers, but will they lower them more than normal," Mr. Hill said. "But so far, no one is pushing the panic button."

This year there is a large disparity between the earnings growth projections of industry analysts and market strategists. Graph compares projections of both during January, February and March. (Source: First Call Inc.)

HEADLINE: Technology Stocks Soar After Slump

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Technology stocks rebounded strongly yesterday, sending the Nasdaq composite index to its biggest one-day gain in nearly six weeks and lifting the Dow Jones industrial average slightly after early losses.
Shares of Intel, surged nearly 6 percent, up 7 3/8, to 140 5/8. Cisco Systems, the most actively traded stock, rose 4 1/2, to 52 3/8, and Microsoft was up 315/16 , to 94 1/4. 
 The Dow rose 4.53 points, to 6,880.70, led by I.B.M., which rose 4 3/8, to 140 1/4, and was the biggest gainer in the index.
The Nasdaq 100 index -- which is heavily weighted with technology stocks -- surged 27.19 points, or 3.4 percent, to 834.38. It was the second-largest gain of the year for the Nasdaq 100, and the equivalent of a 234-point gain in the Dow Jones industrial average.
Along with the Nasdaq 100, the larger Nasdaq composite index also gained for the second consecutive session, up 21.02, or 1.7 percent, to 1,269.08, but was still well shy of its high of 1,388.06 on Jan. 22.
Yesterday's performance was the strongest for the Nasdaq since its Jan. 3 rally, when the Nasdaq 100 gained 4 percent. But since then, the Nasdaq, in general, and technology stocks, in particular, have been in their most serious downturn since last summer.
Networking stocks -- those that allow computers to talk to one another and log onto the Internet -- have been particularly hard hit this year, with some stocks falling by as much as 60 percent. But yesterday, two networking issues, in addition to Cisco, made strong gains. Shares of U. S. Robotics rose 3 3/8, to 59 7/8, and 3Com gained 2 1/8, to 35 1/2.
Technology stocks have been beaten down in recent weeks amid questions over whether profits would justify their high prices.
But the Federal Reserve's interest-rate increase on Tuesday made the stocks of banks and big cyclical companies, so called because they ride the ups and downs of the economy, less attractive. Suddenly technology issues looked relatively cheap.
"Money managers are seeking out stocks that have been depressed," said Dan Ascani, president and research director at Global Market Strategists Inc. in Gainesville, Ga., adding that they still had a considerable amount of cash to invest before the first quarter ends on Monday.
Though computer stocks rose sharply, those of banks, for which profits are harder to come by when interest rates are going up, fell yesterday. Citicorp fell 2 1/8, to 113 5/8; Chase Manhattan dropped 1 7/8, to 99 7/8, and BankAmerica lost 2 3/4, to 109 1/4.
And basic-industry stocks that are vulnerable to an economic downturn were also losers. Du Pont was the biggest drag on the Dow, dropping 2 3/32, to 109 7/8.
Advancing issues outnumbered decliners by a 13-to-12 margin on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume totaled 506.7 million shares as of 4 P.M., up from 487.5 million in the previous session.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.43 points, to 790.50. The New York Stock Exchange's composite index fell 0.04, to 415.37, and the American Stock Exchange composite index fell 3.09, to 583.07.
Stocks were lower early in the session after the Commerce Department reported an unexpectedly strong 1.5 percent increase in orders to factories for durable goods last month. The strong orders reinforced suspicions that Tuesday's increase in interest rates by the Fed would be the first of several intended to slow economic growth.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Orders for Durables Jump, A Sign of Stronger Growth
Showing unexpected strength, orders for durable goods jumped sharply again in February, suggesting that the economy was gaining momentum as it began its seventh year of expansion.
The increase, 1.5 percent, after January's 4.1 percent surge, lent after-the-fact support to the Federal Reserve's decision on Tuesday to raise short-term interest rates by one-quarter percentage point, to 5.5 percent, to try to avert inflationary pressures. [Page D3.] 

Prices of Treasury securities slipped in response to the unexpectedly strong economic report and the weight of a fresh supply of Treasury debt. The yield on the benchmark 30-year bond rose to 6.98 percent from 6.96 percent. [D5.]
 
Technology Stocks in Rebound
Technology stocks rebounded strongly, sending the Nasdaq composite index to its biggest one-day gain in nearly six weeks -- 21.02 points, to 1,269.08 -- and lifting the Dow Jones industrial average 4.53 points, to 6,880.70. I.B.M. led the Dow, while on the Nasdaq, Intel and Microsoft surged. [D6.]
 
Plan on Data-Scrambling Rejected
The leading industrial nations have declined to embrace a United States proposal for computer-privacy technology that would allow eavesdropping by the world's law-enforcement agencies. The decision is a setback for the Clinton Administration, which had attempted to limit the private use of such data-scrambling equipment, and demonstrates the difficulty of setting global policies for the Internet. [A1.]
 
Hearst to Create TV Company
In a deal that furthers the consolidation of the television broadcasting business, Hearst said that it would buy Argyle Television, a San Antonio-based owner of six stations, for $320 million. Hearst said it would combine its six stations with Argyle's and create a public company, Hearst-Argyle Television. [D2.]
 
Plea of No Contest in Fraud
John G. Bennett Jr., the man accused of cheating universities and charities of more than $100 million through his New Era Philanthropy, pleaded no contest to Federal charges of fraud and money laundering. [D17.]
 
Swiss Companies to Plead Guilty
Two Swiss chemical companies, F. Hoffmann-La Roche and Jungbunzlauer, have agreed to plead guilty and pay a combined $25 million in criminal fines for participating in the citric acid price-fixing scheme that also involved Archer Daniels Midland, the Justice Department said. [D8.]
 
Food Giants Pick Ad Agencies
Domino's Pizza, Long John Silver's Restaurants and Quaker Oats -- which together have advertising accounts with billings estimated at nearly $200 million -- all changed agencies. The highest billings involved the national account of Domino's, for which it chose Campbell Mithun Esty in Minneapolis to handle the creative duties and J. Walter Thompson USA to handle media.
Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D5.]
 
F.T.C. Staff Cites Joe Camel
The F.T.C. staff wants the commission to lodge a complaint of unfair advertising against R. J. Reynolds Tobacco, charging that its Joe Camel advertisements are intended to lure children into smoking. [B14.]
 
Sanctions Against D. H. Blair
The D. H. Blair brokerage firm was censured and fined $250,000 for failing to supervise brokers who had prompted complaints because of their high-pressure sales tactics, the New York Stock Exchange said. [D6.]
 
Costs Hurt Deutsche Bank Earnings
Deutsche Bank said that it set aside 1.2 billion marks, or $710 million, last year to reimburse customers and pay potential fines arising from the irregularities by a fund manager at a subsidiary. The bank said the costs had reduced its 1996 profit. [D16.]
 
47th Street Photo Is Closed
47th Street Photo, a landmark name in New York electronics retailing for 30 years and a pioneer discounter, has closed, laid off its work force and may not reopen. [B1.]

HEADLINE: Criticizing Giuliani, Council Proposes a New Budget Strategy

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
The City Council's leadership staked out its first bargaining position yesterday in negotiations over the next budget, asserting that Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's spending plan lacked imagination and underestimated the growth in tax revenues from Wall Street by $516 million.
The Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, said at a news conference that the city should spend $120 million of that windfall on parks, libraries, cultural institutions and other programs, while squirreling away $225 million in a new rainy day fund. Mr. Vallone also called for $45 million in tax cuts for small businesses that create high-technology jobs in industries like software. 
 That reduction would be in addition to $123 million in tax cuts that the Speaker proposed earlier this year.
The Council also proposed that the city begin paying for repairs for water pipes that break between the street and a homeowner's water meter. Until now, homeowners have borne the cost of such work. The Council said the city should assess all water users an extra fee, which would be less than $2 per user annually, to cover the additional expense.
Last year, about 550 such breaks occurred, costing homeowners an average of $3,000 each for repairs, officials said.
Besides those initiatives, the Council leadership did not take issue with much of Mr. Giuliani's preliminary $34 billion budget, including hundreds of millions in cuts at city agencies. And the Council made few proposals to address criticism that the city is not using enough of the taxes from strong profits on Wall Street to solve its long-term budget problems.
The city faces projected deficits in 2000 and 2001 of more than $3 billion annually. The 1998 fiscal year begins on July 1.
Mr. Vallone, a Queens Democrat, said at a news conference that the Council leadership's plans would invigorate the city. He maintained that the proposal to cut taxes for small business involved in high technology showed that the Council was looking toward the future. The Mayor's budgetary priorities, he said, were rooted in the past.
"There is no strategy, no vision in that budget to propel us into the next century," Mr. Vallone said.
Mr. Giuliani defended his fiscal management, saying that in the first three years of his tenure, the number of private sector jobs in the city had jumped by more than 120,000. He said he would examine the Council's initiatives, but he complained about what he said were partisan jibes by Mr. Vallone.
The Republican administration and the Democratic majority on the Council will continue to spar over fiscal policy until May, when Mr. Giuliani presents his final, or executive, budget. If the past is any guide, the Council leadership will extract a few changes from the Mayor before approving the budget in June.

HEADLINE: Standard & Poor's Threatens to Move Jobs to New Jersey

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
The Standard & Poor's Corporation, a fixture of New York City's financial industry, is threatening to move 2,000 jobs to a new waterfront office tower in New Jersey if the Giuliani administration does not grant the company special tax breaks and other incentives to remain in lower Manhattan, according to real estate executives and city officials.
Standard & Poor's, a division of the McGraw-Hill Companies, is seeking to consolidate its bond rating agency and financial information services under a single roof. The company is considering moving to either Jersey City or a 900,000-square-foot space at 55 Water Street, a bulky, concrete tower on the East River near Wall Street. 
 Standard & Poor's now occupies space at 25, 26 and 65 Broadway.
Standard & Poor's is only the latest corporation to engage in the high-stakes game of threatening corporate relocation to try to gain tax and other financial concessions as states compete for jobs and landlords compete for tenants.
Like Dillon, Read; the American International Group; PaineWebber, and ING Capital, S.&P. has looked into abandoning the city for less expensive offices in New Jersey or Connecticut -- all while pressing New York City for tax breaks. Standard & Poor's has talked to the developers of Newport City, a vast complex on the waterfront in Jersey City, about building a new tower for its operations, and possibly, for some units of McGraw-Hill.
"This is not a situation where McGraw-Hill is threatening to move," one person involved in the negotiations said. "They have an important business, Standard & Poor's, that has a space problem downtown. The solution is either 55 Water or move to New Jersey."
McGraw-Hill declined to discuss whether its Standard & Poor's division would move to 55 Water Street or to New Jersey. "We do have some leases expiring in 1998 and 1999, so we are reviewing our space allocations," said Neal Allen, a spokesman for McGraw-Hill.
Buoyed by the robust financial markets, revenues at McGraw-Hill's financial services division, which includes Standard & Poor's, rose 8 percent last year from 1995, while operating income advanced 13.5 percent, to $262.1 million. A spokesman for the city's Economic Development Corporation, which negotiates with corporations threatening to leave New York, would not comment on "negotiations that may or may not be taking place."
But there is little doubt that Standard & Poor's is awaiting word from City Hall before it makes a final decision, sometime in April, according to brokers active in the downtown office market.
"Standard & Poor's is one of several major transactions in lower Manhattan that appears close to reality," said Joseph F. Harkins, a director of Cushman & Wakefield, a commercial real estate broker. "Fifty-five Water Street is one of only a few candidates that could accommodate them."
As the city's economy has rebounded from a recession in the early 1990's, city officials have tried to take a tougher stand in negotiating what they call "corporate retention packages." In calculating whether a company is bluffing about a move out of New York and how attractive a deal to offer, city officials take into account that the office vacancy rate on the New Jersey waterfront and in Stamford, Conn., has dropped considerably in recent years, leaving few options other than costly new construction.
Nevertheless, Gov. Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey pushed a series of bills through the Legislature in 1996 that were designed to entice companies from New York by offering a range of incentives including a rebate of up to 80 percent of the state income tax paid by employees.
But determining the sincerity of a corporation's intentions, in Manhattan or in New Jersey, is often difficult for any landlord or public official. Richard Lefrak, president of the Lefrak Organization, confirmed that Standard & Poor's had looked into building a new office building at his Newport City project in Jersey City. "They and everybody else in the market in lower Manhattan is considering a building at Newport," Mr. Lefrak said. "I can't tell you what's on their mind. A lot of people come out, kick the tires and then go back to New York to negotiate a deal. But New Jersey does have a very powerful incentive program."
So does New York. In 1995, the Giuliani administration created a general series of tax breaks and zoning changes for lower Manhattan aimed at resuscitating a seriously ailing real estate market. Nearly a quarter of all office floors below Chambers Street sat empty, a victim of layoffs and the recession of the early 1990's. City officials had hoped that the downtown plan would eliminate the need to negotiate a tailor-made deal for each company threatening to flee to the suburbs.
But over the last two decades, it has become the norm for a corporation to threaten to leave the city whenever its current leases expire. Some companies have left, while others got millions of dollars in tax breaks to stay in Manhattan.
With its high vacancy rate, real estate brokers and city officials acknowledge, 55 Water, the largest privately owned office building in New York, has served as a "stalking horse" for companies seeking concessions from the city or a landlord.
But real estate brokers now say that Chubb LifeAmerica, Reliance, IBJ Schroder and Standard & Poor's are seriously negotiating to lease space at 55 Water because there are few alternative locations for large blocks of space, the rental rates are low relative to midtown and because the landlord has refurbished the building.

HEADLINE: NAMING OF ENVOYS SLOWS TO A CRAWL

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 26

 BODY:
A leading candidate for ambassador to France has withdrawn, tired of waiting for the call from the White House. There is no American ambassador in important countries, like Russia, Japan and Germany. And many top jobs under Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright await appointees.
Two months into President Clinton's second term, the selection of the senior diplomatic corps has bogged down, a victim of White House indecision caused in part by the furor over campaign financing. 
 With the Administration under scrutiny for the favors that it may or may not have granted donors to last year's Presidential campaign, White House lawyers are scrutinizing the backgrounds of potential nominees for anything that might drag Mr. Clinton into a new controversy.
The awarding of ambassadorships has always been a game of spoils, with choice posts often going to top contributors, but now politics has further intruded, sowing confusion and anxiety. And it has left some prospective appointees hanging, with jobs, businesses and reputations on the line as Mr. Clinton makes up his mind and the lawyers make sure that his decisions create no new headaches.
Now the process appears to have claimed its first victim: Frank G. Wisner, the Ambassador to India and one of the nation's most respected career diplomats, has withdrawn from consideration to be ambassador to France, a senior Administration official said today.
Mr. Wisner, along with Felix G. Rohatyn, the investment banker from New York, was considered a top contender to replace Pamela Harriman, who died on Feb. 5.
But he informed President Clinton and Secretary Albright last week that he would leave the Foreign Service and take a job with American International Group, the insurance company, the official said. (The official added that it would be wrong to assume that Mr. Wisner's withdrawal would guarantee the post for Mr. Rohatyn.)
The official, who spoke on condition of anonymity, said Mr. Wisner had personal reasons to step down now. But a close friend of his said today that Mr. Wisner simply could not wait any longer for Mr. Clinton's decision. Mr. Wisner, who recently returned to New Delhi after a visit to the United States, could not be reached for comment tonight.
The embassy in Paris is only one of 14 with no ambassadors, and dozens of others are expected to open when the current ambassadors rotate out this summer.
"The system has lost common sense," said one person under consideration for an appointment, who, despite having clearance to see some of the country's top secrets, still faces an arduous background check by the White House. "Everybody is so afraid of scandal."
Richard N. Haass, the director of foreign policy studies at the Brookings Institution, said the delays in appointing people to the senior diplomatic posts -- not to mention the hurdle of Senate confirmation -- could hamper Mr. Clinton's foreign policy initiatives.
"By the time you get people named, vetted and through the confirmation process, you'll be lucky to get people into place by the summer, many by the fall," he said. In that case "you've lost most of the year, which traditionally is the best year for foreign policy in a second term."
For her part, Ms. Albright said delays in appointments would not affect the State Department's work. She noted that many top officials, like Deputy Secretary of State Strobe Talbott, are staying on, and that even vacant embassies have capable chiefs of mission serving in lieu of ambassadors.
"Obviously it would be better if we had everybody in place," Ms. Albright said in a telephone interview the other day. "But we're doing fine now."
Some officials play down any delay by noting that prolonged jockeying for foreign policy posts is not new to Mr. Clinton's Administration.
But in the two months since Ms. Albright became Secretary, the President has announced only two members of her team: Thomas R. Pickering, another respected career diplomat who retired from the Foreign Service last year, as Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, and Stuart E. Eizenstat, who was domestic policy adviser to President Carter and is now an Under Secretary of Commerce, as Under Secretary of State for Economic Affairs.
But at least a dozen more of Ms. Albright's choices were sent to the White House for approval more than a month ago and remain in the vetting process, White House and State Department officials said.
Among them, the officials said, is Ms. Albright's choice to be Under Secretary of State for Management, Bonnie R. Cohen, who is now serving as an Assistant Secretary at the Interior Department.
Ms. Albright has also tapped a number of Assistant Secretaries, including Susan Rice, a National Security Council director, for African Affairs; Stanley O. Roth, a former National Security Council director, for East Asian Affairs; K. F. Inderfurth, the deputy representative at the United Nations, for South Asian Affairs, and Marc Grossman, Ambassador to Turkey, for Canadian and European Affairs.
In other important posts, she has selected David R. Andrews, a lawyer who was previously deputy general counsel at Health and Human Services, to be her legal adviser, and James P. Rubin, to serve as the Assistant Secretary of State for Public Affairs.
Decisions appear to have been made on a number of ambassadorships as well, including James F. Collins, the State Department's special adviser for the former nations of the Soviet Union, to Russia, and John Kornblum, the Assistant Secretary for Canadian and European Affairs, to Germany.
None of these appointees appear to be in danger, but in Washington's hyperpartisan mood these days, the White House is taking nothing for granted.
"People are very careful right now in ways that they haven't been in the past," a White House official said.

INTERNATIONAL A3-15
 
Campaign Furor Slows Hiring of Top Diplomats
Frank G. Wisner, one of the nation's most respected career diplomats, withdrew from consideration to be Ambassador to France. A friend said he was tired of waiting for the White House to make its choice. The Paris job is one of many top diplomatic posts that have gone unfilled as the White House has reacted to accusations of favoritism toward donors by scrutinizing its nominees closely for any controversy. A1
 
U.S. Expels Belarus Diplomat
The United States expelled a Belarus diplomat from Washington, retaliating for the "unwarranted" ouster of an American diplomat who was monitoring an anti-Government march in Minsk. A9
 
Yeltsin Defends Finland Summit
President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia defended his conciliatory stance on NATO expansion and said President Clinton had promised at the Helsinki summit that Russia would become a member of the G-7 -- something that Washington is likely to dispute. His Government prepared for a nationwide strike that unions and his opponents have called for today. A5
 
Riots Spread in West Bank
Riots spread to the West Bank town of Ramallah as Palestinian youths and Israeli soldiers clashed for the seventh consecutive day. Dennis Ross, the special United States envoy to the Middle East, met the Palestinian leader, Yasir Arafat, in Morocco and was expected to arrive in Jerusalem today. A6
 
Top German Rejects Turkey
Klaus Kinkel, the Foreign Minister of Germany, told Turkish leaders in Ankara that although Turkey "belongs to Europe," the country's record on everything from human rights to the economy made its entry into the European Union impossible for the foreseeable future. Membership in the Union is Turkey's foremost foreign policy goal, and some fear that rejection will fan Islamist sentiment. A13
 
Few Heed Haiti Strike
Stores stayed shut in downtown Port-au-Prince, the Haitian capital, and the windows of some cars were broken in a shantytown. But most Haitians ignored a call by opposition groups for a general strike against the Government. Protesters want Prime Minister Rosny Smarth to resign and the Government to halt a program of economic measures supported by the United States and the International Monetary Fund. (AP)
 
Malaysia Irked by Singapore
Malaysia suspended business and political ties with neighboring Singapore in the latest diplomatic dispute between the two countries. The quarrel started when Singapore's leader, Lee Kuan Yew, made disparaging remarks about a Malaysian state where a rival politician fled after accusing Mr. Lee of engineering his defeat in a January parliamentary vote. A Singapore Government spokesman refused to comment. (AP)

Apparent Mass Suicide Found in California
At least 39 young men were found dead at an estate north of San Diego, the apparent victims of a mass suicide. No note or signs of trauma were found, and the men were lying on their backs with their hands at their sides but not in any particular pattern, the authorities said. Neighbors said the house had been used for meetings of a religious cult. A1

The new ratings system for television shows may actually attract children to violent or sexually explicit programs, a study found. A researcher called it the "forbidden fruit syndrome." But the study did not explicitly examine the TV system. A22

HEADLINE: Los Angeles Mayor, Profile Low, Rides High in Election Polls

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, March 26

 BODY:
Two weeks ago, in the sole face-to-face encounter of their desultory contest to be Mayor of Los Angeles, State Senator Tom Hayden repeatedly derided his popular incumbent opponent, Richard J. Riordan, as "an invisible man" and "an invisible Mayor," unfit to be "the leader of a global city."
"Newsweek magazine said the Mayor of San Francisco, New York, Laredo mattered," Mr. Hayden, the 1960's radical, said in the mid-day campaign debate. (It was carried live on only one cable station, in Santa Monica.) "The Mayor of Los Angeles did not make the list in Newsweek. We need to have a mayor who matters, who represents neighborhoods against downtown special interests." 
 Indeed, two weeks away from probable re-election, Mr. Riordan stands as something of a paradox. He is a moderate Republican with broad bipartisan support who has presided over an economic rebound that would be the envy of many an aging metropolis, yet his public profile is much lower than those of big-city counterparts like Rudolph W. Giuliani in New York and Richard M. Daley of Chicago.
"It's not my personal style," he said in a relaxed interview in his City Hall office this week, recalling with a bit of amused envy a particularly vivid encounter he witnessed between Mr. Daley and Chicago reporters. "I'd like it to be. It would make it more fun, I think."
There are reasons for Mr. Riordan's comparative obscurity. Under its charter, Los Angeles has a weaker mayoralty than most Eastern cities, and the county is responsible for many of the biggest programs, from welfare to physical planning, that New York City, for example, oversees itself. The greater city is broken up by independent enclaves: Beverly Hills, Santa Monica, West Hollywood. And there is no tradition of colorful mayors like La Guardia, Curley, Daley or Koch.
But a big part of the equation is the phlegmatic Mr. Riordan himself. He built a $100 million fortune as what he calls a "transactional lawyer" and an investor in corporate buyouts before winning the mayoralty in his first run for elective office four years ago, and whatever his protests to the contrary, he seems content to be just who he is, a distinctly unpolitical politician.
"I wonder why a lot of people want a job as mayor or something who are totally incapable of doing the job," Mr. Riordan said, in what appeared to be a dig at his opponent. "And I think too often they figure the title defines them rather than what they do. And you know, to me, I just decided I'm going to be myself. You go into a room and people are cheering and you just figure, 'O.K., Dick, everybody in that room is just as important to God as you are.' "
A devout Roman Catholic, Mr. Riordan has given millions of dollars to educational charities, built a chapel in the backyard of his estate in the Brentwood section and works as mayor for $1 a year. At 66, he is an avid bicyclist and skater. A no-nonsense administrator who awoke the morning of the 1994 earthquake, put on a sweat suit and drove himself to City Hall, he presides over a monthly book group that reads works by Andre Gide, Graham Greene and Alvin Toffler.
"The Mayor does not talk about himself much," said Kevin Starr, the state librarian, author of a multi-volume social history of the state and a longtime friend. "If anything, he's almost oblique and diffident about talking about himself and the exercise of power and government. It's not the way he works. He works as a negotiator, a mediator, a lawyer."
The Mayor's critics contend that is part of the problem. They paint Mr. Riordan as too cozy with his developer friends, blame him for costly mistakes and delays in construction of a new subway system, and accuse him of working behind the scenes to successfully block the renewal of the contract of the city's police chief, Willie Williams -- all without adequate explanation to the public.
Like his fellow Princeton graduate, Jimmy Stewart, Mr. Riordan is a shy and stammering public presence, a stop-and-start speaker who routinely steps on his lines and freely acknowledged in an interview that he had lost his train of thought as a sentence trailed off unfinished.
But it is a style that most Angelenos seem to like, and the Mayor enjoys a 20-percentage-point lead in the popularity polls over his Democratic challenger, Mr. Hayden, although neither's party affiliation will be on the April 8 primary ballot. Mr. Riordin is favored to win a majority and avoid a runoff with the second-place finisher in the field of five.
On Tuesday, Mr. Riordan's longtime friend Senator Dianne Feinstein became the latest in a long line of Democrats, prominent liberals, Hispanics, blacks and Hollywood figures to endorse him.
"Frankly, there have not been any direct areas that potential opponents could zero in on, and it's clear that his current opponent is having a hard time," said Joe Hicks, the former head of the Los Angeles branch of the Southern Christian Leadership Conference who now runs the Multicultural Collaborative, a private civil rights group. He praised the Mayor's decision to double the budget of the city's human rights agency, even as he said Mr. Riordan needed to speak out more on civil rights in the most diverse county in the nation.
"There needs to be stronger leadership from the Mayor in that regard, but it is clear to me that his heart is in the right place," Mr. Hicks said. "Hayden finds it hard stirring up antipathy because Riordan has not really angered people by doing bone-headed kinds of things."
But in one sense, Mr. Riordan has paid for his low profile: While Los Angeles has roughly double the job-growth rate of New York (1.8 percent last year, compared with eight-tenths of 1 percent for New York), and its unemployment rate is about 7.5 percent compared with nearly 10 percent for New York, Los Angeles draws less national attention.
The Mayor led a successful fight to consolidate the city and county offices responsible for issuing film permits, streamlining the process and prompting an increase in filming on location. In the last three years, 60,000 new entertainment industry jobs have been created in Los Angeles County.
Still, Joel Kotkin, a fellow at the Pepperdine Institute for Public Policy in Malibu and an expert on the Los Angeles economy, said: "Riordan has been a very poor politician relative to the City Council. He has not been able to make strong personal relationships with other politicians, he's not a good communicator."
Mr. Riordan amiably challenges such criticism; to suggestions that he might speak out more often, he asks: "Where? To whom? I talk to everybody all around the city, two or three times a week." Indeed he does, in informal settings. But he has adopted the front-runner's standard strategy of ducking more debates with Mr. Hayden, who has taken to carrying around a cardboard cutout of the Mayor and mocking him as "a dollar-a-year man."
The Mayor takes it in stride. "I said he could come up with a better line, like, 'He's being paid what he's worth!' " Mr. Riordan said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Two weeks away from probable re-election, Mayor Richard J. Riordan of Los Angeles is still distinctly unpolitical. He talked yesterday at a meeting of the Los Angeles Metropolitan Transit Authority. (Jim Wilson/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: In Plan to Cut L.I. Utility Rates, Biggest Savings Come Early

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 26

 BODY:
The state's plan to lower Long Island's electric rates delivers the greatest savings to consumers in the first few years, beginning as Gov. George E. Pataki campaigns for re-election next year. To do so, it raises the cost to consumers by more than $1 billion during the next 30 years.
The two Long Island Power Authority officials who set up the financing, a Republican and a Democrat, insisted that politics did not enter into their decision, and that the Pataki administration did not push for a particular approach. But they acknowledged that at the very least, the timing of the deep rate reductions, including one-time rebate checks to Long Islanders during an election year, is fortunate for Mr. Pataki. 
 "I expect someone will suggest a connection, but it's not true," said Frank G. Zarb, the chairman of the power authority, a Republican appointed by Mr. Pataki. "This is as much a Democratic plan as a Republican plan." Aides to the Governor had referred questions about the financing to Mr. Zarb.
The accord announced last week calls for the authority to sell $7 billion in bonds to pay for a LIPA takeover of much of the Long Island Lighting Company's debts and assets. By refinancing Lilco's debts at lower interest rates, the authority would be able to save money and lower the Island's electric rates, the highest in the continental United States. The deal still needs the approval of state and Federal regulators.
Over the long run, officials say, the takeover will lower rates an average of 16 percent, and the pending merger of Lilco and the Brooklyn Union Gas Company will save 2 percent more.
But to give deep rate cuts right away, up to 23 percent, the plan calls for borrowing more than is needed for the takeover, and it postpones repayment for a few years, strategies that increase the long-term cost of borrowing by well over $1 billion. Compared with spreading the savings evenly over 30 years, the accord gives consumers an extra 5 percent savings in the first three years, but the savings are 2.4 percent less than they would have been for most of the next three decades.
"We're trying to get Long Island competitive again right away, to jump-start things," said Richard M. Kessel, a Democrat named to the power authority board by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, who worked closely with Mr. Zarb in designing the takeover. "And I believe that all of the ratepayers are entitled to something up front for all the years of overpayment."
In Nassau County and the Rockaways, Queens, each residential and business customer will receive a $232 rebate check next year. In Suffolk County, every customer will receive a $101 rebate check.
But consumer advocates attacked the plan as financial gimmickry. "This is the type of proposal that would be suggested by the Mayor on 'The Simpsons,' and then would be wildly cheered by the ignorant townspeople," said Larry Shapiro, senior staff attorney at the New York Public Interest Research Group.
On the largest bond issue of $4.3 billion, the power authority would pay interest but no principal in the first three years. On another issue of $462.5 million, it would pay neither principal nor interest for the first five years. Both strategies inflate the long-term interest costs.
The $462.5 million bond sale is intended only to pay for a one-time customer rebate and rate reduction during the next five years, not to finance the buyout. That bond sale would be repaid only by Suffolk customers.
In other words, the power authority would borrow money, give it to consumers, and then charge consumers slightly higher rates for 25 years to repay the debt. Whether it is better for the ratepayer to get the savings right away at a long-term cost, is, to some extent, a matter of opinion. It is akin to lottery winners having a choice between a lump-sum payment or annual payments for many years. If the balance between the two is right, it might be a wash, but that depends in part on future economic conditions that no one can predict.
The rebate checks would go out next year, in the thick of the gubernatorial race. It was Mr. Kessel, the Democrat, who insisted on rebates, he and Mr. Zarb said.
"Ratepayers move, they die," he said. "We wanted to give money to the same people who've been overpaying all these years."
Nassau County ratepayers will get larger rebates than those in Suffolk County, as part of a tax dispute settlement between Suffolk and Lilco. The alternative to rebates, officials said, would have been to give Nassau much lower electric rates than Suffolk, which they said would have hurt Suffolk's economic competitiveness.
Mr. Zarb argued that making the deepest rate cuts right away would put pressure on Lilco and the state to find ways to keep rates low, like developing solar technology. "We need to have a lot of heat and pressure to keep it going," he said.

HEADLINE: Queens Home Yields Cache Of Chemicals

BYLINE: By DAVID M. HERSZENHORN 

 BODY:
The police last night arrested a Queens man after discovering dozens of containers of hazardous materials at his home yesterday afternoon. One canister was marked "Sarin Gas," but officials said they did not know if it actually contained the powerful nerve gas used in a terrorist attack in the Tokyo subway in 1995.
As a precaution, more than 200 people were evacuated from the area around the house at 241-05 52d Avenue, in the Douglaston section. 
 Officials said the canister would be tested by the Department of Defense sometime today. The police reported that in the house, they had also found more 200 gallons of flammable liquids including gasoline, lacquers, jet fuel, ether and nitro-methane.
Based on those findings, the owner of the house, Lester E. Deily, 52, was arrested at the 111th Precinct station house on charges of reckless endangerment, the police said. Officials said that during questioning he had told the police that the canister did not contain any sarin gas.
Neighbors described Mr. Deily as a lifelong chemistry buff and a loner who lived in the same house where he had been raised by his grandparents.
At a news conference at the scene early this morning, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani said Mr. Deily had told investigators that the canister contained only oxygen and that the label on it was a joke. But the Mayor said officials would take no chances.
"At this point, in order to be safe, we have to treat it as if it is sarin gas," Mr. Giuliani said.
"That's why all these precautions are being taken, because we don't know whether we can believe this person or we can't."
One law enforcement official, speaking on the condition of anonymity, said that two other containers in the house were marked "radioactive" but that tests had revealed no signs of radioactivity.
Mr. Giuliani said that Mr. Deily had ordered $69,000 worth of chemicals and scientific equipment from the Hewlett-Packard company but had not paid his bill, and that the police were led to Mr. Deily by two Hewlett-Packard employees who went to his home yesterday afternoon and became suspicious when they saw a number of chemical containers.
By midafternoon, police, Fire Department and environmental emergency workers had surrounded the house and had started to search through the dozens of containers, none larger than five gallons.
"The house has been described as an overpacked -- for those of you who remember the phrase -- Collier's mansion," said Chief of Department Louis R. Anemone, referring to a famous house that was found crammed with newspapers.
At about 7:30 P.M., the police found a .83-liter silver canister in the basement marked with a homemade label that read "sarin gas." Next to it was a canister marked "compressed air." At that point, Mr. Giuliani said, the search was called off.
Mr. Giuliani said that Mr. Deily had been convicted of a harassment charge in New Jersey five years ago for making threatening telephone calls.
Neighbors said Mr. Deily was a car enthusiast who had been restoring an old Mercedes and Jaguar. Investigators said they believed that Mr. Deily was using some of the fuel found at his home in that hobby.
But officials last night said last night that they were most concerned over the possible presence of sarin, the degenerative nerve gas that killed 12 people and injured thousands when it was released by an apocalyptic cult in the Tokyo subway in 1995.
Police officials said they were not qualified to test the canister for the nerve gas and last night were awaiting a special airtight container from the Department of Defense officials to transport the canister to a Police Department firing range in the Bronx.
At 11:15 P.M., officials began evacuating residents from about 100 homes in the leafy neighborhood near Douglaston Parkway.
Deputy Inspector Michael Collins, another police spokesman, described the metal canister as approximately one-liter in size and affixed with a homemade label saying "sarin" that appeared to have been printed on a computer.
Inside the three-story house, investigators found tools, chemicals, generators and extension cords, Sergeant Cory Cuneo, a department spokesman, said. Investigators found the house to be extremely neat, he said. Late last night, emergency workers were preparing a police robot to conduct a further search of the house by remote control.
Lee Anne Rothstein, 30, an unemployed teacher who often walks her dog around the quiet leafy neighborhood, said that Mr. Deily was a recluse who inherited his house from his grandparents and rarely left it.
"He was quiet and kind of eccentric," she said. "I don't think he held a job."
"He was constantly throwing out garbage in big black plastic trash bags wrapped in duct tape," she added. "It would sit in the front of the driveway for weeks."
Another neighbor, Hank Brehl, 51, said Mr. Deily had moved to the Douglaston with his grandparents at the age of 10 and that he and Mr. Deily had gone to Public School 98, Junior High School 67 and Bayside High School together.
"He was always into chemistry sets," Mr. Brehl said. "He was always making little mixtures of gun powder."

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY DAILY BRIEFING;
Man Steals a Bus

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

DATELINE: WINSLOW

 BODY:
An unemployed Egg Harbor man stole a New Jersey Transit bus from a garage in Orange yesterday, made a few passenger stops, then headed toward Atlantic County before he was chased and arrested, the authorities said.
"He actually picked up two people and he drove them to their destination, dropped them off and then came down the Garden State Parkway," said Steve Coleman, a spokesman for N.J. Transit. 
The suspect, David Middleton, 34, drove out of the garage unnoticed just after 5 A.M. amid the 139 other buses that depart starting at 4:30 A.M., Mr. Coleman said.
He was eventually pulled over after a New Jersey Transit mechanic spotted the bus on the parkway, where buses are not allowed.
Mr. Middleton was charged with receiving stolen property and eluding the police and is being held on $25,000 bail, Detective Frederico said. Officials were checking whether he had ever worked as a bus driver, Mr. Coleman said.

HEADLINE: With Questionable Future, 47th St. Photo Closes Doors

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
A landmark name in New York electronics retailing for 30 years, 47th Street Photo, has closed down, laid off its work force and may not reopen.
"We're re-evaluating our position," said Stuart Held, the president of Tut Services Inc., which has been doing business as 47th Street Photo since 1995 under a licensing agreement. Mr. Held said he closed the Manhattan store and its mail-order sales department "several weeks ago," when a number of issues, most notably whether his company would be able to continue using the 47th Street name, became uncertain. He said he expected a resolution of the issue, with a final closing, or another phoenixlike re-emergence of the once thriving company, within the next few weeks. 
 "Our condition right now is a wait-and-see attitude," Mr. Held said in an interview. "It has to do with whether we can make money in the business, and the raising of funds."
Beginning in 1967, 47th Street Photo helped pioneer the idea of discount consumer electronics retailing in New York. Through the 1970's and 1980's, as chains like Crazy Eddie and Nobody Beats the Wiz exploded onto the scene, 47th Street Photo maintained its own distinctive New York flavor. It was noisy, hectic -- and because of its largely Orthodox Jewish work force -- always closed on Saturdays.
The company stumbled, along with many other consumer electronics chains, during the economic downturn of the early 1990's, when the razor-thin margins of the business got squeezed to the brink by a falloff in demand. At its peak, the old company had five locations and more than 300 employees. When the current company closed earlier this month, only 39 workers were left and one location, which was not even on 47th Street, but on West 45th Street. The 47th Street store was closed in the early 1990's.
The company's original owners filed for protection under Chapter 11 of the Federal bankruptcy law in 1992 and are not involved in the company's current incarnation.
Mr. Held, a former employee of the old company, and his brother Ira, backed by an Israeli manufacturer, the Tutnauer Company, licensed the rights to the 47th Street name and its West 45th Street lease for $2 million in 1995.
A lawyer for the creditors committee in the bankruptcy case, Ted A. Berkowitz, said the central issue now was whether Tut Services was still able to make its payments on the licensing agreement. If the company defaults, he said, the name would revert back to the bankruptcy trustee and would probably be licensed to another store operator. "I think the name still has value," Mr. Berkowitz said.
When the Held brothers took over the 47th Street name, they announced an aggressive comeback plan, including expansion into new locations, a public stock offering and a push toward retailing on the Internet. Mr. Held said that if the company did reopen, he still hoped to sell stock perhaps later this year.
Hopes are also still alive, he said, for a new 47th Street Photo at a mall now under construction near Fortunoff's store in Westbury, L.I.
Retailing analysts said consumer electronics -- one of the most cyclical of industries because so much of its fate is tied to innovations, constantly revolutionized technology and consumer fads -- suffered nearly everywhere in the country in 1996.
"Things tapered off because consumers had all the consumer electronics products they needed and there were no exciting new products," said John Hughes, a retailing analyst at Piper Jaffray Inc.
A question for 47th Street Photo's future, whether it survives under the current management or not, is what effect its closing might have customer faith. The company has maintained a "satisfactory" rating with the Better Business Bureau since its ownership change. But the current bureau report also notes that the only two complaints received since 1995 came this year.
A longtime customer, H. C. Peterson of San Francisco, said he was concerned about not being able to get through to even ask a question. Mr. Peterson said that he ordered a $666 camera lens from 47th Street Photo in early February and that his credit card shows that amount charged, but still he has no lens.
When he called several weeks ago, he was told by a salesman that the manufacturer was experiencing delays. More recently, Mr. Peterson said, every fax and phone number has resulted in a dead end.
"It didn't strike me that a renovation should make you shut down all your phone lines," Mr. Peterson said.

HEADLINE: U.S. of Future: Grayer and More Hispanic

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 26

 BODY:
A new report by the Census Bureau documents explosive growth in the nation's Hispanic population and projects that by the year 2005, Hispanic Americans will surpass blacks as the country's largest minority.
And by the year 2050, the report says, they will outnumber the nation's total of blacks, Asian-Americans and American Indians combined, and will account for one-fourth of the population, up from 10.7 percent now. 
 The long-term rise in the number of Hispanic people, who have the nation's highest fertility and immigration rates, will coincide with a decline in that of non-Hispanic whites, says the report, which projects that by 2028, the number of white Americans who die will exceed those being born.
The report, "Demographic State of the Nation," gives a picture of a generally healthy current national economy, with an increase in median household income, a significant drop in the number of people living in poverty and a rising level of education. But there are still significant differences among the races and between the sexes, and some discouraging circumstances for children.
In 1995, the base year for most of the statistics cited in the study, children under 18 still accounted for a vast segment of those living in poverty, about 40 percent, even though they made up only about 25 percent of the total population.
There are also many more children under 18 living with just one parent than there were a generation ago. In 1970, 12 percent of all children lived with one parent; by 1995, 27 percent did. The Census Bureau cited rising divorce rates and a growing tendency to have children first, then marry.
Of the single parents in 1995, 35 percent had never married, 38 percent were divorced, 23 percent were separated and 4 percent were widowed.
In 1995, 21 percent of white children lived with one parent, as did 33 percent of Hispanic children and 56 percent of black children. Those rates are up substantially from 1970, when 8.7 percent of white childrenand 31.8 percent of black children lived with one parent. Such figures for Hispanic children were not kept until 1980, when 20.5 percent of them lived in one-parent households.
The report also reinforces earlier projections of a graying of America. It forecasts that people over 85 will become the fastest-growing age group among the population by the middle of the next century, carrying sweeping implications for the health care industry and Social Security.
In 1995, about four million people were over 85 and made up 1.4 percent of the population. By 2050, the report says, there will be 18 million people over 85, who will account for 4.6 percent of the population. By then, people over 65 will be 20 percent of the population.
The nation's population as a whole is projected to increase 50 percent by 2050, to 394 million, from 263 million in 1995. But the average annual rate of population growth is expected to decline, to 0.63 percent for the period 2040-50 from 1.05 percent for the period 1990-95. This is largely because the baby boomers, the huge postwar bulge of people born from 1946 to 1964, are beginning to move beyond their reproductive years.
Starting in 2020, the report says, more Hispanic Americans will be added to the population each year than blacks, Asian-Americans and American Indians combined. And starting in 2019, the bureau says, the Hispanic American population, relatively youthful, will have the nation's lowest death rate.
In 1995, 46 percent of the 23 million foreign-born people in the United States were of Hispanic origin; nearly seven million had immigrated from Mexico, the country that had exported the largest number of people to America. (The Philippines was second.)
About 800,000 people immigrate legally every year. Immigration of whites will continue to bolster the overall number of white Americans, but by 2034 they will be dying at such a greater rate than their rate of birth or immigration that their numbers will start diminishing, according to Colby Perkins, a statistician in the Census Bureau's population division.
In 1996, non-Hispanic whites made up 73.1 percent of the population; that proportion is expected to fall to 52.8 percent by 2050. Asian-Americans' share of the population will more than double, the report says, to 8.2 percent in 2050 from 3.5 percent in 1996, and blacks will hold about steady, to 13.6 percent then from 12 percent in 1996.
For the first time in six years, there was an increase in real median household income in 1995. The rise from 1994 was 2.7 percent, to $34,076 from $33,178.
But the median earnings of individual women working full time year-round actually declined 1.5 percent after adjusting for inflation, to $22,497 in 1995 from $22,834 in 1994, while men held roughly steady at $31,496.
And women still earn only 71 cents for every dollar that men earn for the same work.
There was a significant decline in the rate and number of those living below the poverty level, which in 1995 was $7,400 for an individual. In 1994, 14.5 percent (38.1 million people) fell below the poverty level, compared with 13.8 percent (36.4 million people) in 1995. Notwithstanding the decline, the new rate is above the 1989 level of 13.1 percent.
On the other hand, 82 percent of adults over 25 years old in 1995 had completed high school, and 23 percent had earned a bachelor's degree or more. Both figures are record highs.
