HEADLINE: Another Blow to Hartford: Whalers to Leave, Rejecting Arena Offer

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, March 26

 BODY:
The Hartford Whalers, a hockey team whose losing record is matched only by its financial woes, rejected the state's offer of a new $147.5 million arena today and announced that they would leave Connecticut at the end of the season.
The team had not inspired a widespread following, but the decision was still a blow to Gov. John G. Rowland, who had made keeping them part of his efforts to revitalize this struggling city. For two years, Mr. Rowland, a Republican, was involved in intense negotiations with Peter Karmanos, the Whalers' owner, and this month he offered to build the team an arena in downtown Hartford. 
 "In essence, we would build the facility and hand the Whalers the keys," Mr. Rowland said at a news conference this morning. "I believe I presented the Whalers with the best offer I could reasonably expect the people of Connecticut and the legislature to support. Mr. Karmanos obviously believes that there's a better deal out there."
Mr. Karmanos said later that the team had needed a heroic deal to stay in Hartford, adding: "The problem here is that we're in a Bermuda Triangle of markets, stuck between New York and Boston, and you need to do something special to survive."
He said the team could not afford the $2.5 million rent the state wanted to charge at the new arena, and that it needed the state to pay the $45 million in losses that it faced during the next three years until the new arena opened, a request that Mr. Rowland refused. Mr. Karmanos also complained about the state's proposal to charge a 10 percent tax on ticket revenues to help pay for the construction.
Other hockey teams have also moved in search of better profits. In the last five years, the Winnipeg Jets moved to Phoenix, the Quebec Nordiques to Denver and the Minnesota North Stars to Dallas, leaving behind thousands of angry fans and city officials who protested that the franchises were demanding too much taxpayer money.
Mr. Karmanos said the team had not found a new home, but suggested that other municipalities were lining up for the chance to get his team. He mentioned Columbus, Ohio; Nashville, Minneapolis-St. Paul, Las Vegas, Nev., and Raleigh-Durham, N.C., as possibilities.
Any franchise shift requires league approval, but league officials said today that they would work with the team to help it find a new home.
There is little question that the move hurts Hartford and leaves Mr. Rowland scrambling to find other ways to help this city of 120,000. The Whalers were one of the few attractions that drew people into downtown. On nights and weekends when there is no hockey game, the streets around the Civic Center Coliseum, where the team plays, are often deserted.
Lisa Kruse, an assistant manager at Coach's Sports Bar and Grill, a downtown restaurant, said the bar fills up on nights the Whalers are playing.
"From 5 to 7:30, you can't breathe," she said. "The Whale brings people in. They have loyal fans that now won't have any reason to come into Hartford."
When the Whaler's goalie, Sean Burke, entered Coach's after a recent (and rare) victory, "he got a standing ovation," she said.
"The place was crazy," she said. "It was one of those goose-pimple moments."
About 97 percent of Whalers fans live outside the city, according to a report by the Hartford Downtown Council, and the games accounted for 16 percent of the visits to the city for arts and entertainment events. The Whalers pumped $73 million a year into the city's economy, the report said.
The team's fortunes appear to have mirrored the city's.
When the Whalers were brought here in 1975 from Boston, the team was owned by a group of local companies, including Aetna Life and Casualty and The Hartford Courant. It was boom time in Hartford, skyscrapers were rising, the city still had many local banks and insurance companies, and the Whalers were a glamorous team, with Gordie Howe and his two sons on the roster.
But since 1990, downsizing and mergers have made Hartford one of the fastest-shrinking cities in the country. Last year, it led the nation in job losses.
As part of the economic downturn, the companies that once viewed the Whalers as a worthy civic project had disappeared or could no longer afford an increasingly unprofitable hockey team. In 1994, Mr. Karmanos, an entrepreneur from Detroit who heads a $2 billion computer software company, bought it.
During the next three years, the team continued to lose about $15 million a year, Mr. Karmanos said, and he said that only a stellar arena could change the team's fortunes. The coliseum in the Civic Center seats 14,660 for hockey, making it one of the smallest arenas in the league, and placing the team among the worst in total attendance.
The team's fortunes were not helped by its lackluster performance. It has not made the playoffs since 1992, and on Tuesday night it lost its sixth game in a row.
A poll by The Hartford Courant in February showed that 7 out of 10 state residents did not want the state to spend $160 million to build the new arena.
The Whalers are not even close to having the same place in the hearts of people here that, say, the Yankees have in New York. The threat that the team might leave has hovered over Hartford for several years, and a dozen random interviews today with people here elicted responses, like "Let 'em go," and "Who cares?"
Mr. Rowland received little criticism today. Even politicians who routinely find fault with him said that he could not have offered a better deal without compromising the state budget.
Mr. Rowland described the package as "generous but fair," and said it would have given the team all revenue from concessions and parking for all arena events except University of Connecticut basketball games. The state would have paid for construction with bonds that would be repaid with the tax revenues and rent.
Mr. Rowland estimated that the deal would have provided the Whalers annual revenue of at least $50 million, a figure Mr. Karmanos disputed.
The Whalers have agreed to pay the state $20.5 million to allow them to leave one year before the expiration of the contract that Mr. Karmanos signed with the state when he bought the team in 1994.
Many were aghast that Mr. Karmanos could turn down such a deal, and saw it as a sign of the arrogance of today's professional sports team owners.
Although Mr. Karmanos insisted that he wanted to stay in Hartford, Mayor Mike Peters expressed doubts about that today and accused Mr. Karmanos of blackmail.
"Every time the state made a positive move to close the gap, the gap seemed to get bigger on the Karmanos side," Mayor Peters, a season ticket holder, said. "Obviously, we're very disappointed."
He added that he would not attend another game.

HEADLINE: Metro Business;
Women-Owned Businesses

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO 

 BODY:
The number of businesses owned by women in New York City and the lower Hudson Valley has jumped by 68 percent since 1987, outpacing the 48 percent increase in the number of all firms for the same period, according to a study released today by the National Foundation for Women Business Owners, a nonprofit research group.
The study of women-owned firms, which covered the 50 largest metropolitan areas in the country, found that New York City ranked first in the numbers of such businesses, with Los Angeles and Chicago second and third, respectively. 
 The 248,700 companies owned by women in New York City, as well as Westchester, Putnam and Rockland Counties, now represent 37 percent of all businesses there. With 754,000 people on the payrolls, those businesses employ 20 percent of all workers in the area while generating 20 percent of all business sales.
 LISA W. FODERARO

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls in Light Trading, Following Stocks and Bonds

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The dollar fell broadly yesterday, following United States stock and bond markets lower.
The selloff came in light trading, which exaggerated price movements ahead of a long Easter holiday weekend. Financial markets in the United States and most markets in Europe are closed today in observance of Good Friday. Most markets in Europe will also be closed on Monday. 
 "It's a follow-through of yesterday's technical selloff, thin holiday trade, and some long-dollar people getting out," said Russ Lascala, head of spot trading at Citibank. "It all seems to be the same scenario: Stocks and bonds are down; the dollar is getting hit."
The dollar was dragged down as stocks and bond fell sharply. The Dow Jones industrial average closed down 140.11 points, or 2 percent. The price of the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond fell more than a point.
In trading in New York late yesterday, the dollar fell to 123.48 yen, from 124.12 yen on Wednesday, and to 1.6736 marks from 1.6898 marks. The pound traded at $1.6315, up from $1.6287 and the highest level against the dollar in a month.
The dollar starting slipping in Asian trading after Deputy Treasury Secretary Lawrence H. Summers in an interview on the Charlie Rose television program expressed concern over Japan's trade surplus with the United States.
His remarks came ahead of a visit to Japan by Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin on April 4 for talks in which the nagging trade tensions are expected to feature prominently.
The dollar was helped somewhat by comments Mr. Rubin made in an interview in The Financial Times of London yesterday. Mr. Rubin reiterated that "a strong dollar is in the country's interests."
The dollar fell even as new economic data -- on consumer sentiment and home resales -- pointed to possible further jumps in interest rates. Higher rates typically bolster a currency. On Tuesday, the Fed raised rates by a quarter-point, the first rise in more than two years.
In other currency trading, the mark weakened against the Italian lira after the Cabinet approved a mini-budget to rein in the deficit and earn Italy a place among the first countries signing up for the European single currency.

HEADLINE: Fed Vote on Rates Was 10-0 in February

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 27

 BODY:
Federal Reserve policy makers were unanimous in holding interest rates steady at their meeting on Feb. 4-5, minutes of the session showed today.
The minutes were released just two days after the Fed's March meeting, in which policy makers raised the Federal funds target rate a quarter-point, to 5.50 percent -- its first increase in more than two years. 
 At the February meeting, however, the policy-making group, the Federal Open Market Committee, voted 10 to 0 to leave the target for the Federal funds rate -- the overnight bank lending rate -- at 5.25 percent. Two outgoing Fed governors, Janet Yellen and Lawrence B. Lindsey, did not attend the meeting.
"Most members thought that inflation likely would remain contained for some period ahead and that any strengthening in inflation pressures probably would be gradual, allowing the committee to respond in a timely manner," minutes from the meeting showed.
Still, the panel agreed that it would be "desirable" to raise rates even "before any sign of actual higher inflation were to become evident."
Several F.O.M.C. members warned an interest rate increase "was not generally anticipated in financial markets, and a move at this time could have exaggerated repercussions."
That preceded repeated warnings from the Fed's chairman, Alan Greenspan, of the possibility of a "pre-emptive" strike against inflation.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Yield Rises Above 7% on Strong Economic Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The yield on the Treasury's benchmark 30-year bond climbed above 7 percent yesterday as strong consumer sentiment and vibrant housing data heightened investor concern that the Fed would again raise short-term interest rates to contain inflation.
The 30-year bond tumbled 1 8/32, to a price of 94 12/32, pushing its yield up to 7.08 percent, from 6.98 percent on Wednesday. The yield is at its highest level since it reached 7.11 percent on last Sept. 11. 
 The bond market is closed today for Good Friday, making it a three-day Easter weekend, and Monday is the end of the first quarter. So some investors were taking no chances and sold to limit their exposure to any negative news.
Traders said the market started to price in potentially strong economic data next week that could have inflationary implications. For example, the monthly employment report is due, and it is likely to show the tight labor market continuing.
Anthony Chan, vice president and chief economist at the Banc One Investment Advisors Corporation, Columbus, Ohio, said: "The market was able to generate little comfort from the firmer tone of today's economic statistics that were released. And since Alan Greenspan was not privy to the outcome of today's numbers, markets are going to surmise that the Fed chairman must be flexing his trigger finger for possible future action if the tone of today's figures persists much longer."
Data released yesterday showed that home resales in February rose 9 percent. Dr. Sherry Cooper, chief economist at Nesbitt Burns Securities Inc., said the activity suggested that interest rates were nowhere near high enough to dampen activity.
The weekly unemployment claims for the week ended March 14 fell 4,000, suggesting that the market could encounter another daunting employment report next week.
Adding to traders' worries, the University of Michigan said its index of consumer sentiment for March rose to 100, from 99.7 in February, suggesting that consumers are more upbeat about their finance and economic prospects.
Jack Regan, chief derivative strategist at Josephthal, Lyon & Ross, said, "The significant element of bond trading today was not just the piercing of the 7 percent yield level, but the inability of the market to come back through it." He added that it suggested that "people were lightening long positions ahead of the quarter-end period."
If data come in as expected next week, showing continuing strength in economy, Mr. Regan added, the yield on the 30-year bond could easily reach 7.25 percent, especially if the employment report confirms the continued strength of the economy.
In the municipal market, the City of Orlando, Fla., said it planned to offer the market's first issue of inflation-protected securities through Goldman, Sachs & Company.
Dubbed "Muni C.P.I.'s", these securities have a final maturity in 2015 and will track changes in the Consumer Price Index very much as Treasury Inflation-Protection Securities do, but unlike the Treasury securities, income from the Muni C.P.I.'s will be exempt from Federal income taxes. The Treasury sold its first inflation-linked debt in January, and corporate borrowers have already followed suit with their own inflation-indexed bonds.
Underwriters said Orlando was selling $40 million of these securities to pay for its waste-water system.

HEADLINE: Strong Data: Home Resales Up 9%, Jobless Claims Down

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 27

 BODY:
Home resales jumped 9 percent in February, bolstered by unusually mild weather and continued job creation, the National Association of Realtors said today.
In a separate report, the Labor Department said that fewer Americans applied for state unemployment benefits last week. 
 The 9 percent rise in home resales brought them to an annual rate of 4.26 million, the second-highest level on record. Analysts surveyed by Bloomberg News had expected a 1 percent gain, to a 3.98 million rate. During January, resales fell a revised 1 percent, to a rate of 3.91 million.
The figures represent actual closings, which lag behind the signing of a contract by a few months.
Housing has defied economists' predictions of a slowdown for almost a year. And although some economists continue to say that much pent-up demand has been exhausted and the growth of the nation's home-buying population is slowing, others contend that as long as the economy continues to add jobs and Americans' incomes rise, the housing market will remain strong.
Referring to the announcement on Tuesday that Federal Reserve policy makers had raised the interest rate on overnight bank loans by a quarter of a percentage point, David Lereah, chief economist at the Mortgage Bankers' Association in Washington, said: "It's going to take the Fed's rate hike, and further increases, to meaningfully inhibit housing activity. Right now this economy is generating hundreds of thousands of new jobs, consumers are very confident, wages are up. I just don't expect housing to slow in the next few months."
Today's report showed that resales rose in the Midwest and West and fell in the Northeast and South.
In its report, the Labor Department said that first-time jobless claims unexpectedly dropped by 4,000 last week from the prior week, to a seasonally adjusted 310,000. The four-week average for jobless claims, a less volatile measure of employment conditions, fell to 311,250, from 312,750 the previous week.
Claims have remained between 309,000 and 316,000 for the last month.

HEADLINE: Dow Tumbles 140.11 as More Strong Economic Reports Jolt Stocks

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
Stocks were rocked yesterday after reports showed that the economy could be accelerating too fast, raising fears that inflation could derail the seven-year-long bull market.
Falling bond prices and a selloff in futures of the Standard & Poor's 500 index set off a wave of blue-chip selling late in the afternoon, in what at one point looked like a panic. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average fell as many as 216 points in the last half-hour of trading before recovering to end the day down 140.11 points, or 2.04 percent, at 6,740.59. The plunge came after bond prices fell more than a point, pushing the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond -- a benchmark for corporate and consumer interest rates -- above the psychologically important level of 7 percent. Bond prices and their yields move in opposite directions.
"The economy is hotter than people thought, and the bond market is recognizing that," said David Shulman, chief equity strategist at Salomon Brothers. "Right now, there's more of an inflationary risk than people previously thought."
If a strong pickup in inflation is indeed on the horizon, Federal Reserve policy makers -- who voted earlier this week to raise short-term interest rates a quarter-point -- may feel compelled to push rates higher. Such a move to slow the economy, analysts fear, would increase lending costs, cutting into corporate profits -- and stalling the bull market.
The new economic data that jolted the stock and bond markets yesterday included reports that home resales jumped 9 percent in February and that the number of first-time claims for unemployment benefits dropped unexpectedly. And a study conducted by the University of Michigan showed consumer confidence picking up. These signs of growth, coming just days after the Federal Reserve voted to raise rates, were troubling for the bond market. The yield on the 30-year bond rose to 7.08 percent, from 6.98 percent on Wednesday -- its highest level since Sept. 11.
Many analysts say that investors begin to see bonds as more attractive than stocks when bond yields rise above 7 percent.
"It's tough to beat 7 percent when the stock market is, as many of us believe, overvalued," said Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at First Albany.
For the Dow, yesterday was the eighth-largest point loss in history, but in percentage terms, it did not even rank in the top 25. And the Dow fell 160.48 points less than two weeks ago, on March 13, after the the yield on the 30-year bond shot up to 6.95 percent from 6.88 percent.
And yesterday's tumble came on relatively light trading. About 476.8 million shares were traded on the New York Stock Exchange heading into a holiday weekend. The market will be closed today in observance of Good Friday.
Still, it was the lowest close for the Dow since Jan. 24, and about 345 points, or about 5 percent, below its record close of 7,085.16 on March 11.
At one point, late in the day, a wave of program selling and a futures selloff pushed the market down and the drop looked as if it might accelerate into one of the biggest point declines in Dow history. At 3:40 P.M., the Dow was down 216 points -- or 3.1 percent. The only time the Dow has ever lost more than 200 points was on October 19, 1987. That day the Dow -- which is the most widely followed index -- plunged 508 points, or 22.61 percent.
According to Birinyi Associates, a market research firm in Greenwich, Conn., there were seven large program trades yesterday -- block- trade selloffs that took more than 193 points off the Dow. The largest, which came at 3:27 P.M., trimmed about 72 points.
On stock market monitors around the world, the final hour of the Dow looked like a giant "V," first plunging and then bouncing back 76 points. The one "buy" program recorded yesterday by Birinyi Associates was a last hour gain of 55 points on the Dow.
The carnage was widespread, but banking stocks and shares in financial services companies were particularly hard hit. It is believed that higher interest rates would squeeze margins at banks while also lowering their underlying asset value.
While the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks lost 16.62 points, or 2.1 percent, to 773.88, the S.& P. Financials Index fell more than 3 percent. Among the hardest hit was Chase Manhattan, down 3 1/2, to 96 3/8; Citicorp, down 2 5/8, to 111, and BankAmerica, down 3 1/4, to 106.
Brokerage houses -- among the chief beneficiaries of a bull market -- were also down. Merrill Lynch fell 2 1/4, to 87 1/2, and Morgan Stanley lost 2 points, to 60 3/8.
Of the Dow's 30 component stocks, 27 lost ground yesterday, with 12 stocks falling at least 2 points. Boeing, I.B.M. and Procter & Gamble led the decline. Boeing lost 3 1/2, to 101 3/4; I.B.M. was down 3 1/4, to 137, and Procter & Gamble fell 3 3/8, 117 5/8. The Dow, however, is still up 4.5 percent on the year.
Michael Metz, the chief investment officer at Oppenheimer & Company, who has long been bearish on the market, said that yesterday's decline was tied to the fallout in the bond market.
"The fundamental background of the bond market gets more and more tentative every day," he said. "The best news for the stock market is a slow-growing economy. A boom is lethal, as is a recession."
While several analysts said that yesterday's selloff was troubling, few were willing to pronounce the onset of a bear market. Still, the volatility has become increasingly troubling for analysts, and they note that young investors -- those who have not yet experienced a bear market -- may see the huge declines as more ominous.
"This is very, very disheartening," Mr. Johnson at First Albany said. 'This is one of the most volatile periods I can recall. And that volatility is pretty intimidating. I'm managing this, but not managing it well. I can't imagine how young investors feel."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Home Resales Jump 9%, Exceeding Expectations
Home resales jumped 9 percent in February, to an annual rate of 4.26 million, the second-highest level on record, the National Association of Realtors said. Sales exceeded expectations and were bolstered by unusually mild weather and continued job creation. 
 In a separate report, the Labor Department said that new claims for jobless benefits unexpectedly dropped last week. [Page D15.]
 
The 30-year bond yield topped 7 percent, as the vibrant housing data heightened investor concern that the Fed would again raise short-term interest rates to contain inflation. The yield, at 7.08 percent, up from 6.98, was the highest in more than six months. [D14.]
 
Investors Slowing Fund Purchases
Investors have slowed purchases of equity mutual funds this month, amid warnings about an overpriced stock market. Fidelity Investments expects no net cash inflows this month, while T. Rowe Price and the Vanguard Group see declining inflows. [D4.]
 
Morgan Stanley Earnings Higher
Morgan Stanley Group said that its quarterly earnings rose 15.8 percent, in large part because of strength in the firm's securities and asset management businesses. [D2.]
 
Trouble at Columbia/HCA
Law enforcement officials and Government agencies are investigating business practices at the nation's largest health care company, Columbia/HCA. The scrutiny came to light last week in a Federal criminal investigation of Columbia operations in El Paso, but a yearlong examination by The New York Times has found that the company faces problems on several fronts. [A1.]
 
On the Way to New Semiconductors
Scientists at Cornell said they had found a way to overcome a significant obstacle to making new semiconductor materials. The development could be a step toward creation of many new types of semiconductors for computer chips, sensors, lasers, switches, data storage devices and other uses. [D4.]

Big Japanese Bank May Be Rescued
Nippon Credit Bank, one of Japan's largest banks, plans a major restructuring to avert a financial crisis and might receive assistance from the Government and other banks, Japanese newspapers reported. [D3.]
 
Bank Investment in Brazil
HSBC said that it had bought the banking business of Banco Bamerindus do Brasil for about $1 billion. [D3.]
 
Oracle Chief Muses Over Apple
Larry Ellison, the chairman of Oracle, who has speculated publicly about taking over Apple Computer in the past, did so again. And Oracle has taken the unusual step of setting up an Internet polling booth where customers, employees, competitors and others can vote on whether they think he should make a bid. [D4.]
 
Door Opens to Younger Car Renters
New York State's highest court ruled that car rental companies may not refuse to rent to young adults. While companies, many of which had set minimum ages of 21 to 25, will now have to rent to younger adults in the state, rates may be set higher for them. [B1.]
 
National Strike in Russia
Nearly two million people were estimated to have taken part in a national strike in Russia, as workers demonstrated against the nation's deepening privation. But because many of the factories rarely make anything, the effect on production is expected to be minor. [A3.]

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL A3-10
 
Canadians Link Hezbollah To U.S. Barracks Bombing
Canadian prosecutors asserted that the bombing of an American barracks in Saudi Arabia last year was carried out by a militant Shiite group with links to Hezbollah, the pro-Iranian group based in Lebanon that is also friendly with Syria. The prosecutors accused a Saudi man arrested in Canada last week of driving a car used in the attack and said he belonged to a group called Saudi Hezbollah. Iran denied any connection to the bombing. A1 

Russian Workers Strike
Russian workers staged the biggest strike against the Government since the Soviet Union dissolved. More than a million people protested the deepening hardship that has convulsed the nation since President Boris N. Yeltsin introduced economic reforms. Rallies in Moscow and St. Petersburg each drew nearly 100,000 people, and protests were held in more than 1,200 other cities. A3
 
Albright Considers Mideast Trip
President Clinton's Middle East envoy, Dennis B. Ross, met with Yasir Arafat in Morocco and then flew to Israel for talks with Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu. The key issues for both Israelis and Palestinians, one official said, are security and terrorism. Madeleine K. Albright may make her first trip to the region as Secretary of State if it can bring both sides back to the table. A8
 
British Elections Face Scandal
The British election campaign opened with scandal as some high-profile enemies of the Tory party of Prime Minister John Major vowed to pursue vendettas until they bring down his Government. The morning papers reported an affair between a 46-year-old Tory member of Government and advocate of family values and a 17-year-old nightclub hostess. The Conservatives' enemies are promising much more to come. A10
 
An explosion outside a Northern Ireland police station raised concerns that the I.R.A. might conduct a bombing spree before the British elections. The blast in Coalisland, a mainly Catholic town 30 miles west of Belfast, followed twin explosions at Wilmslow, an important rail junction 160 miles from London. (AP)
 
Serbs May Have Chemical Arms
Documents released by researchers for a human rights group suggested that the Serb-dominated Yugoslav Army might still possess chemical weapons and might be tempted to use them if war resumes in the Balkans. Researchers also released a NATO report that suggests Serbs may have conducted research on biological weapons. The Yugoslav Embassy in Washington had no comment on the report, saying it had not yet seen it. A5
 
Dalai Lama Upsets China
The Dalai Lama conferred with Taiwan's President in the first meeting of its kind. The Dalai Lama called it a "new beginning" in relations between the island and the Tibetan government-in-exile. Beijing called it "a joint attempt to split China." (AP)
 
NATIONAL A12-24
 
Comet Apparently Inspired Mass Suicide in California
The 39 men and women who committed mass suicide were members of a cult known as Heaven's Gate, the authorities said. They left behind detailed videotapes describing their intentions and appeared to believe that the Hale-Bopp Comet now streaking across the sky was their ticket to heaven. A1
 
The cult earned notoriety in California 22 years ago before disappearing into isolation to teach its members that the Earth was corrupt, that civilization was doomed and that only the disciplined few could be saved, rescued by a UFO. A1
 
Its founder, Marshall Herff Applewhite, lived an apparently unremarkable life in Houston as a music professor who sang in the choir at his Episcopal church. But sometime between 1972 and 1975, he was transformed into a budding cult leader who believed in aliens and Armageddon. The coroner's office confirmed that he was among the dead. A1
 
Neighbors in the cult's exclusive community recalled its members as taciturn, unemotional, seemingly unisexual and above all, unnaturally pale. A20
 
Ray Innocent, King Son Says
James Earl Ray told the youngest son of the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. that he did not assassinate his father. The son, Dexter Scott King, told Mr. Ray that the King family was convinced of his innocence. A22
 
New Advice on Mammograms
Women in their 40's should have breast X-rays every year or two to help detect cancer, the National Cancer Institute said, reversing its previous advice. The institute also advised women who bear their first child after age 30 or who have a genetic mutation that predisposes them to breast cancer to seek guidance on how often to have mammograms and when to begin. A16
 
Gene Linked to Cancer Is Found
Independent teams of researchers found a cellular control gene that is rendered ineffective in advanced forms of some common tumors. They said the finding could apply to hundreds of thousands of cases a year and could, among other things, help tell which patients require aggressive therapy. A14
 
Simpson Told to Yield Trophy
The judge in the wrongful death case against O. J. Simpson ordered him to turn over a long list of belongings that includes his Heisman Trophy and golf clubs to help pay damages to the family of Ronald L. Goldman. Mr. Simpson has seven days to surrender the items to the Los Angeles County Sheriff's Department, which may then sell them. A12
 
Navy Fighter Gains Ground
Defense Secretary William S. Cohen approved initial production of the Navy's F-18 E and F fighter, giving the plane a major edge in an intense battle among the military services over the future of tactical aircraft. There is not enough money to pay for each service's planned planes. (AP)
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-8
 
Car Rental Companies Can't Bar Young Drivers
Car rental companies may not refuse to rent to young adults, New York State's highest court ruled. But the court said they could charge higher prices to renters between 18 and 25 to offset the higher accident risk they pose. The major rental companies said they would comply. B1
 
Irish Bomber Wins U.S. Ruling
An immigration judge refused the Government's request to deport a former Irish Republican Army bomber who had served 12 years in prison. The judge, Phillip T. Williams, said Brian Desmond Pearson's 1975 attack on a Northern Ireland police barracks was a "political offense," not a terrorist act. B1
 
No Nerve Gas in Canister
A canister labeled "sarin" that was confiscated in Queens did not contain the lethal nerve gas, officials said. But containers at the site held everything from jet fuel to ether, nitro-methane, lacquers, sulfuric acid and gasoline, they said. The owner, Lester E. Deily, is charged with reckless endangerment. B3
 
Giuliani and McCall Spar . . .
Mayor Giuliani barred auditors from State Comptroller H. Carl McCall's office from evaluating city programs. Mr. Giuliani's aides said he believed Mr. McCall, a Democrat, had begun several audits solely to embarrass the Republican Mayor. Mr. McCall's aides denied it and said they would sue to get the information they need. B3
 
. . . as Do Pataki and Ross
Governor Pataki's staff tried to evict some of Lieutenant Governor Betsy McCaughey Ross's aides from their State Capitol office space. Mr. Pataki's spokeswoman described the space as "an empty office," but the Lieutenant Governor said six of her aides used it when she was in Albany. Mr. Pataki and Ms. McCaughey Ross have feuded for months. B6
 
U.N. in Uproar Over Parking
United Nations diplomats complained about the city's plan to crack down on their parking. Some demanded that the General Assembly take the matter up. The Giuliani administration plans to confiscate the diplomatic license plates of cars with unpaid tickets. B2

Dante Expert Named Top Poet
Robert Pinsky was named the nation's next poet laureate consultant in poetry. A professor of graduate writing at Boston University, he propelled Dante onto the best-seller lists with his acclaimed 1994 verse translation of the "Inferno." The $35,000-a-year job has few official duties; Mr. Pinsky said he might ask a broad spectrum of Americans to read and record their favorite poems for the Library of Congress. C3

Paul Friedman was named executive producer of ABC's "World News Tonight," the position he held from 1988 to 1993. He was passed over three weeks ago to succeed Roone Arledge as chief of ABC News. D18

Economic Reports Hurt Stocks
Reports that showed the economy might be accelerating too fast rocked the stock market. The Dow Jones industrial average fell as many as 216 points in what at one point looked like a panic, before recovering to end the day down 140.11 points, or 2.04 percent, at 6,740.59. Bonds also fell. D1
 
Health Company Is Investigated
Law enforcement officials and several Government agencies are investigating the nation's largest health care company, the Columbia/HCA Healthcare Corporation. Among the topics of the investigation are whether the company's hospitals inflate their Medicare billings and whether Columbia doctors illegally send patients to outside medical facilities in which they have a financial interest. Quaker Oats closed the books on its disastrous acquisition of the Snapple drink business. Quaker said it would sell Snapple to the Triarc Companies for $300 million, $1.4 billion less than it paid for Snapple in 1994. D1

SECTION: Section A; Page 29; Column 2; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 705 words

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
The Artful Dodger

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
In New Delhi last week, the Prime Minister of India was saying that China and his country were creating "C.B.M.'s together."
That turned out to mean confidence-building measures -- such as a visit to India by the President of China and talk about moving Chinese and Indian troops back some kilometers from eye-to-eye border posts. So, he said then, he had every confidence in China's peaceful intentions toward its neighbor India. 
 For a moment Prime Minister H. D. Deve Gowda and I stared at each other in silence. I could not say in his own office what I thought -- that he was deliberately evading India-China reality and history.
And I knew he was in powerful evasion company. While I was in New Delhi, Al Gore was talking pieties, even political untruth, in Beijing. He talked, so help us America and India, about a "significant advance in the process of democracy" in China. That was an insult to all democracies, and to all Chinese who suffered torture and death fighting for even a sip of democracy.
But I did say to Mr. Deve Gowda that he was the second Indian Prime Minister I had heard tell foreigners and his country about his confidence in China.
Jawaharlal Nehru, India's first Prime Minister, talked, often, about confidence-building measures like "panchshila" -- the five principles of non-interference India and Communist China had signed, the C.B.M. of the 50's and early 60's.
Nehru talked of the importance of another visit to India -- by Zhou Enlai, then Prime Minister of China. They traveled India together, passing under banners proclaiming "Hindi-Chini Bhai Bhai" -- Indians and Chinese are brothers.
Nehru, too, spoke of his confidence in China's peaceful intentions -- almost up to the very hour Chinese troops came through the Himalayan passes in 1962, smashing an Indian Army that had been left unwarned, unprepared, underarmed and shivering in the cold by Nehru and his nasty, America-hating Defense Minister, V. K. Krishna Menon.
What I did not say was that I had seen Indian officers weep in their sense of betrayal and that I hoped no Indian soldier would ever so denounce the name of H. D. Deve Gowda or any who followed him.
He did not reply to my little dissertation on history. But Indian defense specialists privately do not pretend. They know China is expanding its nuclear arsenal and sells Pakistan nuclear technology. They know the U.S. exacts no price and looks away.
Sign a test-ban treaty, as America pleads? While America covers up for China and helps its military grow? No Indian prime minister would be fool enough to do that.
China also still occupies 18 percent of one Indian state, Jammu and Kashmir, and refuses to recognize the boundaries of others on India-China high borders.
And since 1962 it has been China's voice that is heard everywhere, no longer India's. Now it is the Chinese dictatorship the American Vice President visits and to which the President plans a pilgrimage, not the Indian democracy.
The American China lobby has suddenly discovered that profits might also exist in India. But it advises India not to "seek" an enemy in China. Indian friends tell me that is insolent condescension to their intelligence and memory, both of which are capable of reaching back to 1962.
China does not even have to threaten attack now. India's weaknesses, inherited or inflicted by its leaderships, and the West's political and economic submission to China are enough to keep Indians and most of Asia quiet about the China they fear.
India's policies, successes and mistakes are her own. But when American leaders talk moral and political claptrap about Chinese democracy, Asians hear far more clearly than Washington intended. They hear Washington tell them that if they should ever stand up to China economically or politically, let alone militarily, don't count on us, bhais.
The Indian Prime Minister is a religious Hindu. Mr. Gore and Mr. Clinton are religious Christians. When Mr. Gore returns he and Mr. Clinton will go to churches of their choice to worship as they were taught. There they will pray aloud for Chinese Christians trying to do the same and beaten and arrested for it by order of Mr. Gore's hosts -- won't they?

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Brooklyn Rabbi Is Accused Of Stealing Grant Money

BYLINE: By JOSEPH P. FRIED 

 BODY:
A corruption case involving a politically connected Brooklyn social-services group widened yesterday when a former top official was charged in the theft of hundreds of thousands of dollars of Government funds that had been awarded to the group.
The defendant, Rabbi Elimelech Naiman, was director of employment and training of the Council of Jewish Organizations of Borough Park, an influential organization that for years has been courted by politicians who saw the council as a key to winning Orthodox Jewish votes in Brooklyn. Last December, the group's director of operations, Paul Chernick, was charged with the same crime. 
 An indictment unsealed yesterday in Federal District Court in Brooklyn lumps the men together as defendants in the case and repeats accusations in previous court papers that a small amount of the allegedly stolen money was diverted to the council's chief political benefactor, Assemblyman Dov Hikind. But Mr. Hikind, a Brooklyn Democrat, was not charged with any crimes, and he has long maintained that he has done nothing wrong.
Officials in the United States Attorney's office in Brooklyn, which is prosecuting the case, declined to comment on whether they believed that Mr. Hikind's alleged receipt of the money -- totaling about $10,000 for payment of various expenses for him and his relatives -- was illegal. One of the prosecutors in the case, Elisa Liang, an assistant United States attorney, said only that "the investigation is continuing."
In fact, her office has never named Mr. Hikind as the "public official" it says received the money, though details in court papers that the office has previously filed make clear the official is Mr. Hikind, and defense lawyers in the case acknowledge it.
A spokeswoman for Mr. Hikind, Chani Katzen, said the legislator had no comment about the case yesterday, except to say, "My heart goes out to Rabbi Naiman and his family." The rabbi, a 45-year-old Borough Park resident, has been a political adviser and fund-raiser for the Assemblyman.
Rabbi Naiman pleaded not guilty in the Federal Court in Brooklyn and was released on $500,000 bail by Magistrate Judge A. Simon Chrein. His lawyer, Benjamin Brafman, said later, "I cannot find any evidence whatsoever of any crime that was committed by Rabbi Naiman." Mr. Chernick's lawyer, Lawrence S. Goldman, said after his client was first indicted in January that Mr. Chernick "expects to be vindicated" at a trial.
In charging both men, yesterday's indictment does not specify how much each is accused of stealing and illegally "misapplying" from the Federal grants that the group -- widely known as COJO -- has received to carry out programs in job training and economic development, and for the elderly and immigrants. But Federal investigators have said in previous court papers that they believed that more than $600,000 in Government funds had been illegally diverted.
The indictment charges that Mr. Chernick and Rabbi Naiman used the stolen funds to pay for "personal expenses" and those of other COJO employees and of people who did not work for the group. The expenses it charges were paid for Mr. Hikind and his family include school tuition for his daughter and niece, a trip he and his family took to Israel and a car rental and a computer for his office.
If convicted of the most serious charge, the defendants face up to 10 years in prison.
COJO has also received state and city funds to finance what once totaled $5 million a year in programs but which today is far less because of the criminal investigation, Mr. Brafman said. In December, the city said it was rescinding nearly $1 million in contracts with the group.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; National Desk 

LENGTH: 2188 words

HEADLINE: DEATH IN A CULT: THE BELIEFS;
On the Furthest Fringes of Millennialism

BYLINE: By GUSTAV NIEBUHR 

 BODY:
The mass suicide in a wealthy Southern California enclave casts a brutal light on a millennialist sect that earned notoriety on the West Coast 22 years ago, before disappearing into isolation to teach its members that the Earth was corrupt, that civilization was doomed and that only the disciplined few could be saved, rescued by a U.F.O.
The police in San Diego said the 39 people dead in the suicide were members of a group called Heaven's Gate, which maintained an elaborate Internet site under that name. Documents on that site indicate that this group was the same as a U.F.O.-obsessed organization founded in 1975 by a couple from Houston: Marshall Herff Applewhite, who was among the dead found on Wednesday, and Bonnie Lu Nettles, whose charismatic preachings were described in an article in The New York Times Magazine in February 1976. 
 In a group whose members shed their birth names, Mr. Applewhite was identified on the Internet site simply as Do, as in the musical tone, while Ms. Nettles, who apparently died more than a decade ago, was Ti.
The group's most recent posting on its Internet site declared that the approach this week of the Hale-Bopp comet provided "the 'marker' we've been waiting for -- the time for the arrival of the spacecraft from the Level Above Human to take us home to 'Their World' -- in the literal Heavens." [Excerpts from the group's writings, page A20.]
In its documents, the group described a worldview on the furthest fringes of millennialism, with disconnected elements of Christianity interpreted through a thick lens of science fiction. In essence, its teachings boiled down to a belief in exalted purpose for a few, combined with an exceptionally grim view of life on an Earth where evil was in control -- a heady mixture of profound hope amid utter isolation.
Heaven's Gate left no shortage of clues as to its negative thinking about the value of this-worldly life. The group subscribed to a gnostic religious view of the soul as a separate and superior being, temporarily inhabiting a physical form.
Bodies, wrote Do in 1995, were merely "the temporary container for the soul." A soul, he added, could evolve to a higher level of being, at which point it would receive a new physical form to house it.
"The final act of metamorphosis or separation from the human kingdom," he wrote, "is the 'disconnect' or separation from the human physical container or body in order to be released from the human environment."
In another document posted on its Internet site, Heaven's Gate declared itself "against suicide" -- but with such nuance as to leave ample opening for it.
Yes, the group said, its members expected to exit Earth in their "physical vehicles (bodies)" when a spaceship arrived to take them to the "Next Level." But should the forces of the world turn violently against them, it added, the group would be "mentally prepared" for whatever came its way. The document asked readers to consider the example of the Jews at Masada who killed themselves rather than submit to Roman legions in A.D. 73.
Furthermore, the group said its understanding of suicide was not at all conventional: "The true meaning of 'suicide' is to turn against the Next Level when it is being offered." In the group's thinking, a spaceship, thought to be following in Hale-Bopp's wake, would be offering just such an opportunity.
 
Brief Taste of Notoriety, Then a Long Silence
The group came together 22 years ago under the charismatic preaching of Mr. Applewhite and his companion, Ms. Nettles, a former nurse. It enjoyed a short-lived burst of notoriety, before the couple took it underground in 1976. After existing in deep seclusion in various Southwestern cities, the group surfaced again briefly three years ago, when members sought out recruits with a series of public lectures.
In the group's documents, Mr. Applewhite and Ms. Nettles are described as representatives of an extraterrestrial plane called the Kingdom of Heaven, come to Earth "to offer the way leading to membership" there for those who could "overcome" their attachment to money, sex and family life.
Such total separation, the group preached, was necessary because Earth's human structures -- governmental, economic and, especially, religious -- were under the control of demonic forces: "Luciferians" and evil "space aliens," in the group's parlance.
The urgent belief in a coming end to a hopelessly corrupt world has long been a recurring part of American religious culture. In one of its best-known episodes, followers of the Bible student William Miller, expecting Christ's return in 1843, gathered on hillsides, anticipating transport to Heaven.
But as the 20th century wanes, the millennialist impulse has taken an increasingly dark cast among some smaller and socially isolated groups. More than 75 Branch Davidians, anticipating a coming end of the world, died in a fire during a siege of their compound near Waco, Tex., by Federal agents in 1993. And in the last three years, more than 70 members of a sect called the Order of the Solar Temple have committed group suicides in Canada and Europe in the belief they could transcend a corrupt world and reach a higher state.
Mr. Applewhite and Ms. Nettles told an interviewer from The Times Magazine in 1976 that they had come to believe they were beings from outer space, incarnate in human bodies, with a mission to teach others about the possibility of reaching a new stage of existence.
In time, they began calling themselves "the Two," a reference to the "two witnesses" of Christ foretold in the Bible's Book of Revelation, whose dense allegory has long attracted all manner of religious believers seeking prophetic knowledge of the future. (It was Revelation that David Koresh, the Davidians' leader, insisted he was in the process of decoding as Federal agents besieged that group's compound.)
According to the Bible, the two witnesses are prophets who will be slain by a beast from the bottomless pit, then be resurrected and ascend to Heaven.
In 1975, Mr. Applewhite and Ms. Nettles, who then used the names Bo and Peep, toured the West Coast, holding forth on college campuses and in private homes, declaring that a spacecraft would arrive to take away a select few. Who was ready, they asked, to "walk out the door" of their lives, to join the movement and reach the Evolutionary Level Above Human?
In the coastal village of Waldport, Ore., 20 people who heard them lecture pulled up stakes and left town with them. Elsewhere, people abandoned spouses, children and businesses to follow.
The message that Mr. Applewhite and Ms. Nettles preached was well suited to a nation traumatized by the loss of the war in Vietnam, the resignation of President Richard M. Nixon and years of social upheaval and violence. They were both anti-establishment and puritanical, calling for total separation from society, simple living with shared resources and adherence to a moral code that eschewed drink, drugs and sex.
At one point, it was estimated that the couple had attracted between 200 and 1,000 followers, who roamed the country in free-spirited pairs and small groups, spreading the word.
But in 1976, as numerous followers became disillusioned and drifted away, the couple took the remnants of their group underground. Those who went with them severed contact with loved ones, creating a mystery that lingered in the hurt suffered by the families they left behind.
Sociologists who originally tracked the group estimated that it had shrunk considerably by this time, with far fewer than 100 members.
 
Group Published Book To Tell Its History
The years that followed are known mainly through the group's documents. In a 200-page book that the members of Heaven's Gate self-published last year, one of them wrote that they had spent 17 years undergoing a type of re-education, "a 'metaphoric' classroom experience of changing over their consciousness and behavior," evolving to reach a stage in which they could enter the higher realm that Mr. Applewhite and Ms. Nettles preached.
In this and other documents, the group elaborated on its theology, filling out a skeletal Christian framework with the flesh of a wholly new set of beliefs.
The beliefs were these:
Two thousand years ago, the beings of the Kingdom Level Above Human appointed an "Older Member" to send to Earth a "Representative" (Jesus) to teach people how to enter the "true" Kingdom of God. But humans inspired by demonic forces killed this individual, also called "the Captain," and transformed his teachings into "watered-down Country Club religion."
Then, according to the documents, a new chance was offered to humanity in the 1970's, when the Kingdom Level dispatched a second team of two Older Members to take up human bodies (or "vehicles") and resume the teachings.
The documents also make clear that the group's members took peculiar new names, another sign of their total break with the outside world. The book, for example, contains an overview written by "Jwnody -- a student."
Much of the essential beliefs of Heaven's Gate are spelled out in a lengthy, first-person statement published on the group's Web site and updated two months ago. Apparently written in 1995 by Mr. Applewhite (under the title "An E.T. Presently Incarnate"), it reveals that Ms. Nettles died in 1985 (or, as he wrote, "separated from her borrowed human container and returned to the Next Level").
The document also says the group's followers arrived on Earth in "staged" spacecraft crashes and were temporarily disembodied before taking human form in bodies especially designated for that purpose by "other crews from the Level Above Human."
The document is full of foreboding about the state of the world, warning that the Government, the wealthy and "moral" leaders are controlled by evil space aliens, who have also used all religions to deceive humans about God. It warns, too, of a coming apocalypse that will destroy civilization; gang wars and ethnic cleansing are offered as proof that the process has begun. Later there will be a "restoration period" in which another civilization will be born.
The document reveals too the group's rigidly authoritarian code. "The only way an individual can grow in the Next Level is to learn to be dependent on his Older Member as that source of unlimited growth and knowledge," it says. "So, any younger member in good standing forever remains totally dependent upon (and looks to) his Older Member for all things."
Five years ago, Heaven's Gate put aside its seclusion and took its message public. In its book, the group said it undertook a series of "satellite television broadcasts" in 1992. Then, the next year, it took out an advertisement in USA Today, with a brief description of its philosophy. The ad's title: " 'U.F.O. Cult' Resurfaces With Final Offer."
The ad was also reprinted, the group said in its book, in "alternative newspapers" and in publications overseas, allowing Heaven's Gate to enter into correspondence with a far-flung audience.
In 1994, the book said, the group "sold all our worldly possessions except for a few cars and changes of clothing, and set out cross-country holding free public meetings" in various cities. Press reports from that time suggest that this met with mixed results.
Five members who gave a lecture on the group's philosophy at the University of Illinois at Chicago in July 1994 drew only 40 people, some of whom left when a videotape of Mr. Applewhite was shown, according to The Chicago Tribune. The newspaper reported that members of the group referred to their bodies as "vessels" that contained souls from a super-human level.
 
Membership Drive Adds 'Crew Members'
 But the group's outreach accomplished two ends: membership was "nearly doubled" (the group's book did not give numbers), and the group concluded that those who joined were not part of the "public in general" but rather lost "crew members," beings from the higher level. "It was quite evident to them and to us that we were of the same family, of the same mind," the book states.
As happy as this experience might sound, it was also the last positive interaction that Heaven's Gate would experience in trying to function publicly. The group, its book said, spent the next year in seclusion, undergoing new training, "an accelerated version of the 'metamorphic classroom.' "
In September and October 1995, the group published major statements on its Internet site. One was titled, "Undercover 'Jesus' Surfaces Before Departure"; the second was Mr. Applewhite's "E.T." statement.
The documents drew a mixed response, but the prevailing reaction struck the group as "ridicule, hostility or both."
Ominously, given what was to take place near San Diego this week, Heaven's Gate read this response as final, an indication that its mission was over, that the "weeds" of humanity had taken over Earth's "garden," and that civilization was finished.
"This was the signal," the group wrote in its book, "for us to begin our preparations to return 'home.' "

HEADLINE: Harlem on Her Mind;
Preacher's Daughter Takes Wall St. Sensibility Uptown

BYLINE: By ELISABETH BUMILLER

 BODY:
It's morning again in Harlem and Deborah C. Wright, the president of the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone, and therefore the neighborhood's de facto finance minister, begins another day deciding who will get money and who will not.
This does not always make her popular, and people sometimes scream at her in public meetings. She maintains a complicated alliance with Representative Charles B. Rangel, Harlem's longtime power broker, whose lieutenants get mad at her, too. No one, however, has accused her of measuring words. 
 Consider the following:
At 8:30 A.M., she tells her staff that a struggling Harlem doll-making company wants loans and grants of almost $7 million because its officers say "culture problems" with its Spanish-speaking workers are causing a drop in production. "They have no air-conditioning and no heat," Ms. Wright says dryly. "That's a big culture problem."
At 11 A.M., she meets with a downtown developer who wants to renovate a Harlem theater and add a recording studio. Ms. Wright perks up. "We're into this big time," she tells him, adding that applying for a loan is "not all that bloody."
At 12:30 P.M., she listens as representatives for Harlem's small grocers, losers to the big Pathmark supermarket expected to break ground this summer, ask her to finance business training aimed at making the grocers more "bankable," in the words of their consultant, Ralph Declet.
"Making them legal, to start," Ms. Wright shoots back, referring to those grocers who run cash businesses off the books, and triggering a wan smile from Mr. Declet.
"That's one way to put it," he says.
When people need things these days in Harlem, they go to Ms. Wright, a blunt, 39-year-old Harvard Business School graduate with an impeccable family pedigree, friends on Park Avenue and powerful backing from Wall Street, Albany and City Hall.
Empowerment zones try to help economically distressed areas through job training, assistance in home buying and tax and other incentives for businesses. Ms. Wright's job is to distribute $550 million in government tax credits and loans over the next decade, most aimed at jump-starting new business and spurring investment in Harlem.
She has already committed $11.2 million for Harlem U.S.A., the planned shopping complex on 125th Street, which will include the Gap and the Disney Store, and $350,000 for Minton's, a restaurant and jazz club, to be developed in part by the actor Robert De Niro and the restaurateur Drew Nieporent. Not surprisingly, she has set off neighborhood criticism that she is turning Harlem over to outside white businesses.
"Why can't the average shopper in Harlem have a range of options that looks like any other neighborhood?" Ms. Wright said. She pointed out that the third partner in Minton's is Melba Wilson, an African-American and a lifelong Harlem resident.
An economic revitalization program is hardly a new idea in Harlem -- hundreds of millions in government money has poured into the area since President Lyndon B. Johnson's war on poverty -- but putting a woman like Ms. Wright in charge of one is. Part of a new generation of black professionals who went to Ivy League colleges and worked at the elite financial institutions of New York, Ms. Wright is not, she said, another traditional social reformer or a politician handing out favors to friends, but a financier who says she holds Harlem to the standards of Wall Street.
Ms. Wright worked at First Boston during the investment bank's white-hot 1980's ("I wanted to be the first black female partner on Wall Street," she said, although she stayed for only three years), went to the New York City Housing Authority board under Mayor David N. Dinkins, then was appointed, as a Democrat, to be Commissioner of Housing under Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, whom she likes.
In her typical head-on style, she says she lives in Greenwich Village and not Harlem -- a lot of people would prefer it if she lived there -- because there are not yet enough amenities and she wouldn't be able to get a salad at Dean & Deluca.
"That's important to me," she said.
But there is another side to Ms. Wright. It is essential to know that she is the daughter, granddaughter, great-granddaughter and sister of Baptist ministers, and that her aunt is Marian Wright Edelman, the founder of the Children's Defense Fund and a mentor who disapproved of her niece's Wall Street years. "In the family values, that was sort of a no-no," said Ms. Edelman, who is thrilled that her niece has come home to the family business of public service.
 
Not a Job, But a Mission
Ms. Wright is not entirely sure she is home to stay, but says the family pressures on her were powerful, and talks now of the mission of her work. "This isn't a job," she said, talking for several hours over lunch the other day in midtown Manhattan. "It really is an emotional undertaking. It's such a wound in the psyche of the African-American public to see this place that was such a mecca be on its back, and knowing that the answers are really not brain surgery."
She mentions the busloads of Japanese and European tourists who venture up to the home of American jazz but return empty-handed because there is nothing to buy. "It's sickening," Ms. Wright said. She wants to develop tourism as part of the Harlem mix, along with national chains, local businesses and job training programs. "I'm not naive about how steep the hurdles will be," she said.
Despite the recent optimism about a Harlem rebirth, the area's most basic statistics remain grim. Of people 25 years and older, 47,000 are high school graduates, but 52,000 are not. The unemployment rate is nearly 19 percent.
An officer of the most ambitious government program would find the task overwhelming, but Ms. Wright is leading an organization created as a legislative afterthought in President Clinton's 1993 budget, then saved in committee by Mr. Rangel, who says he never believed in empowerment zones but wanted the money for his district. He continues to call empowerment zones (there are nine around the country) "grains of sand on the beach."
Dealing with Mr. Rangel is a challenging part of Ms. Wright's job. The old guard and the new guard praise each other extravagantly in public, and need each other to survive, but there is a power struggle in private over Mr. Rangel's role. So far the empowerment zone has approved $15.2 million for 12 projects expected to create 1,300 jobs; associates of Ms. Wright say Mr. Rangel's people often call her, angry that they have not been consulted or insisting she give a hearing to friends in the community.
"I don't have one political contract in there," Mr. Rangel said. "There'll never be my fingerprints on anything. I don't want to interfere with what she does."
Mr. Rangel said that "Debbie Wright is there because I put her there." That is somewhat true. Ms. Wright is also the only candidate on whom the Mayor, Gov. George E. Pataki and Mr. Rangel could agree.
Ms. Wright, when pressed on how much she holds Mr. Rangel and the old guard responsible for the failures of the Harlem Urban Development Corporation -- an organization that Mr. Rangel helped found, but that was shut down two years ago by Mr. Pataki, who cited mismanagement and waste -- is restrained.
"I just think they didn't have the skills to get the job done," she said.
 
Road to Harlem Began in the South
Debbie Wright's road to Harlem began in the segregated Deep South, in the small town of Bennetsville, S.C., where her grandfather built the Shiloh Baptist Church and preached until he died. Ms. Wright's father, the Rev. Harry S. Wright, now minister of the Cornerstone Baptist Church in Crown Heights, took over Shiloh and the leadership of Bennetsville's black community. In the 1960's, with his three young children in tow, he helped lead the way to integration. His most vivid memory is arriving with them at the all-white public swimming school.
"I just took the kids by the hand and walked on up there, and slowly made our presence felt," Mr. Wright remembered.
Ms. Wright and her brother and sister were the only blacks at the white elementary school. "It was intense," she said. She made no friends at the school -- "I felt pretty much just ignored," she said -- and moved to Dallas a few years later.
By the time she was in junior high school, in the early 1970's, Ms. Wright once again found herself on the front lines of the racial divide, this time in the first busloads of black students to integrate the Dallas public schools. "That seventh grade year was probably the worst," Ms. Wright said.
But in the kind of melding of black and white worlds that later marked her life in New York, Ms. Wright says she and the other leaders of the school, the smart students, pulled together. "We bonded in the student government," she said. She had even broader ideas her senior year, when she rejected the safe choice of Spelman, the black women's college in Atlanta (and the alma mater of her aunt and her two sisters) for Radcliffe.
"My father tried to convince me not to go, because he thought I was going to be disillusioned," Ms. Wright said. "And the truth is, I was."
Partly, she said, her feelings came from growing up in the South, "where there's so much emphasis on how racist the South is, and you actually think there's a place where it isn't the case."
"And you think of this great intellectual institution," she said, "and you get there, and it's the same old stuff. I know I'm being oversensitive, but you just had a constant feeling of being unwanted."
But she went on to Harvard Business School. "It's the ticket," she said. By 1984 she was one of 11 students who graduated with a joint degree in law and business, a sizzling credential that landed her a job as an associate in corporate finance at First Boston, where she did not distinguish herself.
"It was a really bewildering time, because I had never been in a position where I didn't achieve what I set out to," she said. "I was not connected in any way, not to the work, not to the people."
She left in 1987, when she was making $135,000 a year, and took a job for $100,000 less at the New York City Partnership, a business advocacy group, as director of marketing for a Harlem building project. "All I wanted was a job where I actually wanted to wake up in the morning," Ms. Wright said.
In 1992 she was named by Mayor Dinkins to the board of the New York City Housing Authority, the manager of the city's public housing, and in 1994 stayed on in the new administration, after "a lot of soul-searching," as Commissioner of the Department of Housing, Preservation and Development.
Richard D. Parsons, the president of Time Warner and the chairman of the empowerment zone, persuaded her to take her present job. She has committed herself to it for at least three years, knowing its successes and failures will be hers as well. She is eager for the talk to end and the groundbreaking to start.
"Otherwise," she said, "I'm just the latest Johnny-come-lately promising that things will be better."

HEADLINE: GOLF: Seeking Fairness on the Fairways;
Female Golfers Are Challenging Country Club Rules

BYLINE: By MARCIA CHAMBERS 

DATELINE: HARTFORD

 BODY:
For 10 years, Christina Watkins has been a full-fledged member of Wee Burn Country Club in Darien, one of the most exclusive country clubs in Connecticut. She put up with the all-male grill and restricted weekend tee times. She didn't get angry until she received an assessment raising her dues 6 percent.
"I do not question the board of directors' decision to raise Wee Burn's membership fees," she wrote to the board last year. "What I question is that as a full golf member I am denied the same access to club facilities as my male counterparts." 
 Her quest to get Wee Burn to change its policies began in January 1996. It has led her to the Connecticut Legislature in Hartford, where on Monday she and four other female golfers will testify before the 41-member joint Senate-Assembly Judiciary Committee about discrimination at their clubs. Each woman tried to bring about changes from within her club. Each failed.
In the last decade, a quiet revolution has taken place in scores of golf and country clubs throughout the nation. From Winged Foot in New York to Olympic in San Francisco, the leaders of the nation's country clubs have grappled with issues of discrimination, a subject they never dreamed would reach inside their gates.
Until recently, race rather than gender was the nemesis of private golf clubs. Marginal progress has been made on that front. Augusta National, home of the Masters tournament, which begins April 10, has 2 black members among roughly 250. In Connecticut, the New Haven Country Club will accept its first black member this spring. But Augusta, whose membership includes some of the top Fortune 500 executives in the country, has yet to admit a female member. As its former chairman, the late Hord Hardin put it: "We love our women. We just don't want any fussin' with 'em."
But today, it is the female golfers who are leading the charge against the clubs' policies. Women, typically only a few in each club and particularly those who work during the week, want the same rights as their spouses or single male members: to vote, to sit on the board, to be served lunch in the grill, to play golf on weekend mornings and certain midweek afternoons set aside for men only.
Because the clubs, and their male leaders, have been unresponsive to women's relatively narrow requests -- they're talking about tee times and grill access, not membership and board positions -- that women are pressing the issue.
Watkins, a construction entrepreneur who was raised in the country-club world, pays $6,000 in annual dues to belong to Wee Burn, a club that had a $42,500 initiation fee in 1996.
She thought that the inequity was so obvious the board would vote to change it. Wee Burn did make two changes: it increased women's weekend tee times by one hour, from 11:30 A.M. to 10:30 A.M., and it created a corner of another room for women and couples to eat lunch. The club kept the men's grill sacrosanct during the day. "All they did was to throw me a bone," Watkins said. "They didn't address the issues."
Such changes have also been resisted in the rest of the tristate area, home to a large contingent of the nation's corporate elite and the golf and country clubs they belong to. A bill aimed at eliminating discriminatory practices in private country clubs was first introduced in the New York State legislature in 1980; it passed the Assembly in 1981, 1982 and 1988. But it never found a Senate sponsor.
In 1991, Norma Grill, a Nassau County tax attorney and a golfer at Cold Spring Country Club on Long Island, mounted a new campaign that centered on overcoming opposition from senators who were members of private country clubs. The bill became law in 1994; it requires country clubs with 100 or more members to provide equal access, if the clubs conduct outside business such as renting out their facilities.
Last month, New Jersey's Assembly approved legislation that would eliminate disparate tee times and access to facilities for women holding their own club memberships. The New Jersey bill is now headed to the Senate, where one of the golfers leading that campaign, Hazel Frank Gluck, said, "it may well face a tough fight" to get out of committee.
The New Jersey Governor, Christine Todd Whitman, "opposes discrimination of any sort in any setting and is amazed that country clubs or other social clubs of their own accord would sanction discrimination in this day and age," her press secretary, Pete McDonough, said.
"The Governor believes men and women who pay equal dues to the same organization should all be treated alike."
The New Jersey legislation was first introduced in 1995 by Assemblyman John V. Kelly, a Republican from Nutley. He sponsored the bill after several female constituents complained about policies at their clubs. Two of New Jersey's most politically powerful female golfers testified before the state's Judiciary Committee last fall in support of the bill: Judy Shaw, Whitman's former chief of state, and Gluck, her former campaign co-chairwoman and now one of the state's most powerful lobbyists.
Again, the New Jersey bill is hardly radical in scope. It does not apply to a club's membership selection process. It does not apply to spouses, who are considered guests at many clubs. The bill says only that single women who own a membership in their name and who pay dues at their private club are entitled to the same rights as male members.
"Working women want to take their clients out to the course, same as the guys do," Gluck said this week. "If the woman has paid full freight she should have full access to the course. These are club rules left over from a bygone time."
In Connecticut last summer, Watkins approached Representative Ellen Scalettar, a Democrat from Woodbridge, the vice chairwoman of the Assembly's Judiciary Committee. Last month, Scalettar invited the Connecticut Attorney General, Richard Blumenthal, and various female country-club members as well as club officials to a meeting in Hartford to explore the issues. Afterward, Scalettar decided to hold a public hearing, the one on Monday. Blumenthal is scheduled to testify in support of a proposed bill that will require golf and country clubs holding liquor licenses to provide "equal access for all adults in all membership categories at all times."
Blumenthal said in an interview yesterday that the current forms of discrimination in a number of the state's country clubs "offend human dignity and deny business opportunities" to women.
Watkins also consulted a Westport attorney, Douglas Shrader. Earlier this month, he served Wee Burn with notice that Watkins had decided to sue. The lawsuit seeks to eliminate preferential tee times for men and to allow women into the men's grill.
The private country club was once treated as an oasis, protected from public scrutiny by first amendment rights of freedom of association. But not anymore.
Legislators in some states have done what was unthinkable two decades ago. After examining the inner workings of private country club life, they are searching for ways to intervene. Does the club receive property-tax incentives? Are there business tax write-offs or other economic benefits derived from corporate golfing events, social receptions or other outside events that give the club a public character? Do liquor licensing requirements bar discrimination?
In recent years, Florida, Kansas, Maine, Michigan and Montana have enacted legislation redefining private clubs as public accommodations if they fit a variety of conditions. Maryland and Minnesota bar discrimination in country-club policies as long as the club accepts the state's significant property tax deductions under its open spaces laws.
Although weekend tee times for women is the most visible issue, the deeper political problem concerns membership. Many clubs have bylaws restricting membership to men, thus excluding women from serving on the club's governing body. In the last decade, many clubs have changed their membership structure to enable either spouse to hold the membership.
That's what New Haven Country Club does. The couple may choose who will hold the membership; the membership holder gets to play golf early on weekend mornings. But when clubs permit either spouse to hold the membership, typically the husband do, leaving the wives in the same predicament. Currently, there is no woman holding the primary membership at New Haven County Club.
Darlene Iglhaut and her husband, Lud, members of New Haven Country Club for 13 years, recently resigned because the club failed to change its attitudes and policies toward women, especially spouses. "The issue has become a strong moral issue for us which has resulted in forfeiting a somewhat large initiation fee," said Iglhaut of the $10,000 she paid to join New Haven. Iglhaut, who served as chairwoman for the women's golf association for two years, said the club routinely rents out the course, holds a state liquor license and may benefit from a reduced property tax bill for its land.
"The question is: Do these advantages place a private club in the position of denying to female members the rights they should be entitled to?"

CORRECTION-DATE: April 2, 1997, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article on Friday about female golfers seeking equal rights at private country clubs, misstated the women's primary goals. They are concerned about membership policy and positions on boards, not just tee times and access to grill rooms.
