HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Deregulation Plan Outlined By Japanese

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: TOKYO, March 28

 BODY:
The Japanese Government today released a proposal for deregulation that is intended to lay the foundation for the liberalization of the nation's financial markets.
The package, based on a proposal earlier this month by the ruling Liberal Democratic Party, includes measures to lower the barriers separating banks, brokerage firms and insurance companies and to cut the tax and fixed commissions imposed on securities trades. 
 Analysts said the proposal might fall short of expectations.
The package may pay "too much attention to reconciling the colliding interests" of financial companies, Koyo Ozeki, senior director of the IBCA credit-rating agency, said. "The key is whether the Government can overcome its hesitation by June and achieve the really sweeping reform it has advocated."
The release of the planned measures is to be followed by the announcement of a detailed plan by June.
Finance Minister Hiroshi Mitsuzuka said, "We want to do whatever we can do ahead of 2001." Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto, who wants to bring the financial markets in Tokyo up to the level of those in New York and London, has said he wants reforms in place before then.
The measures would make it easier for brokerage firms and investors to trade financial products like stock options and new types of corporate bonds, the Finance Minister said.
Also under the proposals, the securities trading tax would be changed and possibly eliminated. Cash transactions through brokerage accounts that are similar to transactions through bank accounts would be permitted. Banks would be allowed to sell investment trust services, similar to mutual funds, at teller windows.
Leasing and credit card companies would be allowed to sell bonds and commercial paper to finance their lending.
Regulations covering the management of employee pension funds would be eliminated. Currently, more than 50 percent of the funds must be invested in Government bonds, less than 30 percent in assets denominated in foreign currencies, less than 30 percent in stocks and less than 20 percent in real estate.
Also under the plan, the sale of options on individual stocks would be permitted.
The Government also plans deregulation measures for other parts of the economy, including real estate, housing development and telecommunications.

HEADLINE: Lane Dwinell, 90, New Hampshire Governor

 BODY:
Lane Dwinell, a two-term Republican Governor of New Hampshire in the 1950's and later a member of the Eisenhower and Nixon Administrations, died on Thursday at his home in Hanover, N.H. He was 90.
The cause was heart failure, a relative said. 
 Mr. Dwinell, Governor from 1955 to 1959, was known for his shrewd application of accounting and budgeting principles to state government and for using some of the savings he achieved to pay raises for state employees, seeking to reduce what he called an excessive and costly turnover of state employees.
After being a businessman in Lebanon, N.H., for many years, he got his start in politics by being elected to the town budget committee, then worked his way up to president of the State Senate and House Speaker before becoming Governor.
In 1959, he was nominated by President Dwight D. Eisenhower as Assistant Secretary of State for Administration and served until 1961. He returned to the family-owned clothing-manufacturing business, Carter & Churchill. In 1969, he was appointed by President Richard M. Nixon to lead the Agency for International Development and served until 1971.
Mr. Dwinell graduated from Dartmouth College.
His wife, the former Elizabeth Cushman, died in October.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Obituary (Obit); Biography

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (96%);

HEADLINE: India and Pakistan Seek Cure For Ills Born of an Old Anger

BYLINE: By JOHN F. BURNS 

DATELINE: NEW DELHI, March 28

 BODY:
More than three years after high-level talks between India and Pakistan foundered over the perennial problem of Kashmir, the two countries resumed the dialogue today, with hopes for a breakthrough toward a more neighborly relationship as high as they have been in many years.
In four days of talks, the Foreign Secretaries of the two nations, each holding the top nonpolitical job in their ministries, are to discuss issues ranging from Kashmir to nuclear weapons, from trade and water supplies to cultural exchanges. 
 If the two sides can avoid the rancor over Kashmir that has impeded numerous efforts to improve their relations since the subcontinent was partitioned in 1947, officials on both sides say, there is a strong possibility of an early summit meeting between the two Prime Ministers, H. D. Deve Gowda of India and Nawaz Sharif of Pakistan. The tentative plan is for the men to meet in early May when six South Asian countries gather in the Maldive Islands for an annual conference on regional cooperation.
The two Prime Ministers both head new governments: Mr. Deve Gowda has been in office for nine months; Mr. Sharif won a landslide election eight weeks ago. Both have said their priority is better economic performance, not wasting energy on old confrontations, and both have said they favor breaking the deadlock this year, when India and Pakistan celebrate the 50th anniversary of their independence.
But the two leaders' upbeat mood is not fully shared by officials who prepared the current talks, largely because of concern that no way will be found to neutralize the old bitterness over Kashmir. Adminstration of the state has been divided since 1949, when the United Nations established a cease-fire line after two years of fighting between the Muslim majority and the Hindu rulers.
The dispute over the divided territory was at the heart of the last round of top-level talks between the two countries, again at the Foreign Secretary level, which began in 1989 and collapsed in 1994, after seven rounds, with increasing acrimony over the Pakistan-backed insurgency against Indian rule.
The tensions were apparent even today as the two Foreign Secretaries, Salman Haider of India and Shamshed Ahmed of Pakistan, prepared for the opening two hours of talks, with Indian officials saying progress depends on setting the Kashmir issue aside and Pakistan insisting Kashmir is the "core issue."
Still, officials acknowledge that India and Pakistan have a pressing need to find new ways of dealing with each other. Mr. Deve Gowda and Mr. Sharif have said in effect that India and Pakistan have missed out on the benefits that have flowed from political breakthroughs elsewhere in the world, notably with the end of the cold war and the collapse of apartheid.
Both countries are being nudged toward agreement by the United States, which is using enhanced diplomatic leverage born of coupling Washington's long friendship with Pakistan with a relationship with India that has greatly improved since the end of the cold war.
But the immediate spur for the talks came from Mr. Sharif, who surprised many in Pakistan by saying publicly during his election campaign what many politicians had long acknowledged privately: that hostility toward India had burdened Pakistan with unacceptably high military costs, impeded natural trade and distorted the country's development, and that it was time to be more pragmatic.
"We are nearly 50 years late, and we can't afford any further delay," Mr. Sharif said.
The Indian Foreign Minister, Inder K. Gujral, has pressed hard for a more harmonious relationship between India and its neighbors. Recent months have seen India sign major water-sharing agreements with Bangladesh and Nepal, and Mr. Gujral has said he sees no reason why similar progress should not be made with Pakistan.
But Kashmir is the toughest issue of all. India, which controls two-thirds of the territory, has never resolved the tensions that breed when a predominantly Hindu nation rules over a population that is more than 70 percent Muslim. On the other side, Pakistan has backed the insurgency by Muslim rebels, in which at least 30,000 people have been killed since 1989.

HEADLINE: Japanese Bank Reports Big Loss in U.S.

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: TOKYO, March 28

 BODY:
Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi Ltd. said its American unit incurred a loss of $50 million from positions in such financial instruments as interest rate swaps.
 The loss, which was announced on Thursday, followed a review of the unit's computer portfolio appraisal model, which had overestimated the market value of the positions, the bank said. After having unwound those positions, the unit recorded an after-tax loss of $83 million, which included $33 million in costs. 
 The bank said that the authorities in the United States and Japan had been informed of the loss but that no laws, regulations or internal rules had been broken.
 Commenting on the loss, Walter Altherr, senior financial analyst at Jardine Fleming Securities, said: "It's not an enormous amount of money. It may be big for the U.S. unit, but I don't think it's big for the group."
The loss will not cause "the slightest" problem for the bank's financial health, he said.
On the Tokyo Stock Exchange today, Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi fell 4.4 percent, declining 90 yen, or 73 cents, to 1,960 yen, or $15.85. The stock was hurt by Thursday's loss announcement and by concern that the bank, like other major Japanese banks, will be asked to shoulder part of the burden of a financial bailout plan for the Nippon Credit Bank.

HEADLINE: Dollar Rises in Quiet Trading After Broad Drop on Thursday

BYLINE: By Dow Jones 

 BODY:
The dollar rose in a quiet session yesterday as traders bought the currency after its broad decline on Thursday.
Trading was thin on the Good Friday holiday. Many European markets were closed. In the United States, financial markets were closed, and although banks in New York were open, many New York traders stayed out of the market. 
 Yesterday afternoon in New York, the dollar stood at 1.6790 marks, up from 1.6736 marks the previous day, and at 124.05 yen, up from 123.48 yen.
The only significant activity yesterday was by Japanese investors who bought marks for yen. "There's been some interest to invest outside of Japan to pick up yield," said John McCarthy, senior vice president at ING Capital Markets in New York.
Because interest rates in Germany are higher than those in Japan, investments denominated in marks carry higher yields than those denominated in yen.
The mark reached an intraday high of 73.92 yen and ended the day at 73.88 yen. The last time it traded at those levels was on Feb. 14, when the mark reached 74.01 yen.
 Analysts expect the dollar to move significantly against the yen once the new Japanese fiscal year begins on Tuesday. Some believe a wave of new investments in dollar-denominated assets will be made from Japan. Others are not so sure.
 "People seem to think the Japanese will come in, but they may be expecting too much too soon," said Seth Garrett of Credit Suisse First Boston in New York.

LENGTH: 839 words

HEADLINE: Drop Reported In Companies' Fourth-Quarter Profit Margins

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 28

 BODY:
Reversing course, profit margins of American companies declined in the final months of 1996 as compensation costs took a slightly bigger bite, the Government said today.
Although profitability fell only modestly -- to 10.4 percent of total output from 10.7 percent in the third quarter -- it was the first decline in a year and focused attention on a warning by Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, who has been cautioning that investors in stocks seem to have been betting on an expanding profitability that could probably not be achieved. 
 The sag in profits comes at a time when the economy is still showing little sign of slackening from a rapid pace, which was an important factor in the Fed's decision this week to raise short-term interest rates by a quarter-point.
Growth in the October-December period was at a 3.8 percent annual rate, the Commerce Department's report also disclosed, barely changed from the 3.9 percent the agency estimated at the end of February. The price index for gross domestic purchases ran at 2.6 percent instead of 2.5 percent earlier.
Recent strong reports on housing, big-ticket manufactured goods and initial claims for unemployment benefits have sent economists scampering to raise forecasts for the current quarter, many of them to rates of 3.5 to 4 percent, more than a point faster than the consensus estimate of 2.4 percent on March 10.
A second Commerce Department tally showed today that for the first time in more than a decade, sales of new homes surpassed an 800,000 annual pace for two consecutive months. An 811,000 rate for February was just seven-tenths of 1 percent below that for January.
Investors have become increasingly worried about accelerating growth and accompanying inflation, and on Thursday, the stock and bond markets tumbled. The markets were closed today, but the latest profit and other figures seemed likely to aggravate current jitters.
Calculations based on the profits data showed that the overall margin for nonfinancial corporations narrowed in the final 1996 period after three consecutive quarterly expansions. Compensation costs, said Kenneth A. Petrick, a department specialist, rose in the autumn after holding steady over the summer.
The result seemed to bear out the concern expressed by Mr. Greenspan. Late last month, for example, he expressed it this way before the Senate Banking Committee:
"The long-term earnings projections of financial analysts have been marked up noticeably over the last year and seem to imply very high earnings growth and continued rising profit margins at a time when such margins are already up appreciably from their depressed levels of five years ago."
He added that while it was possible that margins could fatten further if pay and benefits could be held to their current pace and productivity improved, even small disappointments "could have outsized impacts" on the stock market.
Indeed, the profit margin for businesses other than banks and other financial institutions hit 10.5 percent for all of 1996, today's report also showed, up from 9.9 percent for the two preceding years and the highest since 1978. The low for the latest 20 years was 6.4 percent in 1982.
The fourth-quarter decline, however, suggested that profit margins might have begun to narrow.
"Profit rates have probably hit their peak for the current business cycle," said Dean Baker, economist at the Economic Policy Institute, a liberal-oriented research organization here. He referred specifically to what he said was an unprecedented expansion of American profit margins since 1988 involving a "dramatic" shifting of income from wages and investments to profits.
Another analyst predicting that current profit levels will not hold up is David B. Bostian Jr., chief economist at Herzog, Heine, Geduld. "The deflation of corporate profits will accelerate," he said in a commentary this week, pointing to the Federal Reserve's interest-rate increase that he said would lead to recession.
And while not predicting an economic downturn, Paul C. Taylor of America's Community Bankers said he expected "some slowing" of corporate profits, reflecting, among other things, the strength of the dollar, which is making it harder for American exporters to sell abroad. He also pointed to rising inventories of unsold homes that have been largely masked by strong sales.
As for the stock market, Mr. Taylor declared it vulnerable "to any slight reduction in profit margins."
The Commerce Department, which reports profits by four different measures, said total profits from production, including financial companies, fell 1.1 percent in the fourth quarter after rising eight-tenths of 1 percent in the third quarter.
But Mr. Petrick said the decline would have become a 1.7 percent advance had it not been for special assessment on savings institutions to recapitalize their insurance fund.
The small downward revision to gross domestic product, the department said, reflected lower personal consumption spending.

SECTION: Section 1; Page 18; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 593 words

HEADLINE: Paralyzed, Again, in Albany

 BODY:
For the 13th year in a row, New York State will start a new fiscal year next week without a budget and with a Governor and Legislature once again paralyzed by arguments over money. But the spectacle seems sorrier than ever, since the two sides cannot even agree on how far apart they are. An even sadder reality is that there is enough money available to finance a sensible compromise between Republicans and Democrats, if only there were leadership instead of posturing.
Gov. George Pataki started the budget cycle off on a confrontational note by proposing another round of unnecessary cuts in payments to hospitals, nursing homes, community health centers and home care for the elderly poor. Once again he advocated raising tuition at the State and City University systems and cutting tuition assistance for those who desperately need it. His school aid and property tax cut formulas are almost brazen in the way they discriminate against New York City. These cutbacks are unconscionable at a time when tax revenues are rolling in from Wall Street and an expanding economy elsewhere. 
 Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, the top Democrat in Albany, obviously suspects that Mr. Pataki wants to push through tough budget cuts this year so he can have a surplus handy to pay for popular programs when he runs for re-election in 1998. To head off that possibility, Mr. Silver's budget goes to the other extreme. Passed in part by the Assembly last week, it would add $2.25 billion in spending and tax cuts by the Democrats' estimate. Republicans say the Democratic proposals will actually add $4 billion.
At this point in the annual Albany budget game, the two sides are at least supposed to agree on the cost of what they want to do, but partisanship has fuzzed even the numbers.
Then there is the problem of holding the budget hostage to each side's pet concerns. Last year Mr. Pataki successfully demanded that the Democrats accept reform of the workers' compensation system before he would agree to a budget. This year the Democrats are naming their price, threatening to resist any budget negotiations until Republicans agree to drop their threat to remove rent controls from New York City apartments. The law governing rents expires June 15, and many fear that there will be no budget until then.
The first priority for Mr. Pataki, Mr. Silver and other legislative leaders is to separate rent, welfare reform and other emotional issues from the budget itself. Second, they should direct their aides to reach a sensible compromise on the amount of revenue available for the budget. That compromise should be a cautious one, reflecting the realistic concern that the current stock market boom will not go on forever. Both sides should drop their destructive habit of enacting multi-year tax cuts and spending programs that cause deficits to balloon in the years ahead.
Finally, Mr. Pataki should accept the idea that the budget cannot be balanced with cuts that fall disproportionately on the poor, as they now do, and that his tax cut proposals should not penalize New York City. The Governor says he wants to increase the city's portion of school aid, but the budget for next year goes in the opposite direction. Only about 9 percent of Mr. Pataki's $1.7 billion proposed cut in the property tax would benefit New York City homeowners.
The Governor says that he is ready to compromise on his ideas, but that the Democrats are being unreasonable. All sides could use a dose of reality. There is no reason they could not get to it right away.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

LENGTH: 1222 words

HEADLINE: Gephardt Takes to the Road, and Speculation on 2000 Follows

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY 

DATELINE: CONCORD, N.H., March 28

 BODY:
Perched on a windowsill in the cramped private office of State Representative Peter Hoe Burling are pictures of a beaming Bill Clinton and his heir apparent, Vice President Al Gore. And smiling back from a wall across the room is a photograph of Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House Democratic leader.
In a state that takes special pride in its first-in-the-nation and frequently make-or-break Presidential primary, Mr. Burling, leader of the Democratic minority in the New Hampshire House, insists that any symbolism in the pictorial face-off between Mr. Gore and Mr. Gephardt, potential rivals for the Democratic Presidential nomination in 2000, is entirely unintentional. 
 "Nobody in this state is thinking about that right now," Mr. Burling said. "We are all aware that it is out there, but we have other business on our minds right now. But we know in a year's time, there will be a lot more attention."
Even now, there is hardly a move that Mr. Gephardt makes or barely a comment he utters that does not stir immediate conjecture about his intentions, raising what some of his senior aides refer to simply as "the question." And he brought speculation to a boil this week when his search for 1998 Democratic Congressional candidates carried him first to Iowa, whose Presidential campaign caucuses are held even before the New Hampshire primary, and then here.
"I think we are leaping to judgments here that are inappropriate," Mr. Gephardt said in Iowa on Tuesday when "the question" arose barely a minute into a news conference at the Statehouse in Des Moines. "I'm here for one reason and one reason only, and that is to encourage people to run for the United States House of Representatives. We've got some very, very important issues in front of all of us right now, and the last thing we need to be doing is talking about political races way out in the future."
But to Curt Olson, a high school administrator who spent the better part of an afternoon listening to Mr. Gephardt expound his views on education at a round-table discussion in Indianola, just south of Des Moines, there was a familiar ring.
"It seems we are getting started pretty early this time with the Presidential election visits," Mr. Olson said afterward.
"I like him -- he seems honest and sincere," Mr. Olson said, and then volunteered, "I'd vote for him."
The speculation about Mr. Gephardt is fueled largely by his unsuccessful campaign for the 1988 Democratic Presidential nomination. It was presumed that he would try again in 1992, but he chose early on, like a number of other Democratic prospects, to sit out the race rather than fight for a chance to take on a then popular incumbent, George Bush.
In recent weeks, he has rekindled the talk by staking out some positions that are at odds with those of the Clinton-Gore team. He was among the Democrats who rebuked the Administration for certifying Mexico as an ally in the war on drugs. And it was his opposition to readjusting the Government's chief inflation measure, the Consumer Price Index, that scuttled a potential budget deal between President Clinton and Congressional Republicans.
Further, nearly on the eve of Mr. Gore's trip to China this week to discuss trade and other issues, Mr. Gephardt introduced legislation that would require Congressional approval, and not just the President's, for China to join the World Trade Organization.
And earlier this month, as the storm over campaign financing continued to swirl around Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore, he moved to shelter himself from it, announcing that he had returned $22,000 in contributions that he had received from some figures who are a focus of the investigation, including John Huang, the former Democratic Party fund-raiser at the center of the controversy.
"It shouldn't be a surprise to Clinton and Gore, but it is breathtaking how aggressive Gephardt has become in the last few weeks," said Thomas M. Mann, director of government studies at the Brookings Institution in Washington. "He is staking out some positions and has made it clear that if Clinton gets anything done in the second term, it's not going to be because of the assistance of his good friend, the minority leader of the House of Representatives."
Since last year's elections, which left Democrats 11 seats shy of regaining control of the House, Mr. Gephardt has visited 14 states trying to recruit candidates who can help the party retake that control and elevate him to Speaker.
Those cross-country travels have also kept him in the public eye far away from Washington, and cemented some strong bonds with leaders like Mr. Burling.
"Dick Gephardt is always impressive -- the man has a mind like a steel spring," Mr. Burling said after accompanying Mr. Gephardt for most of a whirlwind Thursday here that included a news conference, a private meeting with Democratic legislators, a closed-door discussion with prospective Congressional candidates and a visit with the Democratic Governor, Jeanne Shaheen. It was a day that guaranteed Mr. Gephardt statewide media exposure.
Mr. Gephardt has been joined on his travels this week by his new deputy chief of staff, David Plouffe, who has extensive political experience in Iowa and New Jersey and helped run the successful 1988 Iowa campaign of the favorite-son Presidential candidate, Senator Tom Harkin.
Democrats who have been involved in private talks with him on the trip, in either Iowa or New Hampshire, say Mr. Gephardt does not discuss the next Presidential campaign.
Still, State Representative Amanda A. Merrill, the deputy minority leader of the New Hampshire House, said that in the private meeting with Democratic legislators here on Thursday, questions were askedabout Mr. Gephardt's "core values," which in the local political lexicon is an invitation to lay out one's stump speech.
"He talked a lot about family values, education and the lives of our children," Ms. Merrill said.
For his part, Mr. Gephardt insists that his visits to Iowa and New Hampshire, during the first week of Congress's two-week Easter recess, had more to do with coincidence and the opportunity to steal seats in two traditionally Republican fiefs thanwith his own political ambitions.
"The future will take care of itself," he said when "the question" was raised a fifth time on Thursday.
For all of that, trips like this one are not without personal benefits for Mr. Gephardt, who for one thing has received heavy local media attention this week. In Des Moines, the major television station led its evening newscast with an account of a visit by "a former and potential Presidential contender." And here in Concord, where his public appearances were limited to a brief photo op with the Governor and a 10-minute news conference, his visit nonetheless attracted reporters from four national newspapers.
"It is sort of a no-lose situation for Gephardt," said a former senior aide to him. "He still has a lot of friends in Iowa and New Hampshire from 1988. Even if he does not know what he is going to do, you keep in touch with those guys.
"His trips this week are not completely brazen. If you look at John Kerry, Jay Rockefeller, Bill Bradley, you notice that they are also going places that are not politically harmful to be."

HEADLINE: HOCKEY;
Devils Draftee Wins Hobey Baker Award

BYLINE: By WILLIAM N. WALLACE 

DATELINE: MILWAUKEE, March 28

 BODY:
The Hobey Baker trophy, awarded to college hockey's player of the year, was presented today to Michigan's Brendan Morrison, who may someday play for the Devils.
Morrison, a senior center from Pitt Meadows, British Columbia, and the highest scorer in Michigan history, was drafted by the Devils in the second round in 1993. 
When asked about his future, Morrison said: "I really haven't made up my mind what I'm going to do. Going pro is a possibility, but I haven't heard anything."
His coach, Red Berenson, said the 5-foot-11-inch, 182-pound economics major is "a player that what you see is not what you get."
"When I first saw him he was just a scrawny kid," Berenson said. "I had no idea he was going to be the player he has become."
Morrison's career with the Wolverines ended here Thursday night in bitter circumstances. Michigan, the defending champion, was eliminated in the semifinal round of the National Collegiate Athletic Association tournament in a 3-2 loss to Boston University.
"Sometimes the best team doesn't win," Morrison said.
Morrison was made almost invisible by the smothering Terrier defense until the last minute. With 57 seconds to go, he scored the game's last goal. It was his 31st this season in 43 games, the 102d in his four-season career.
His most memorable goal was the 71st. It came a year ago, in overtime against Colorado College in the championship at Cincinnati, which was won by the Wolverines, 3-2.
Morrison, who is the first Michigan player to win the Baker award, led the nation in scoring as a sophomore and again this season.
The award was initiated in 1981 by the sponsoring Decathlon Athletic Club of Bloomington, Minn. The prize is named after a pre-World War I Princeton athlete, who is widely considered the foremost American-born player of this century. Baker, a pilot, was killed in a plane crash a few days after the Armistice in 1918.
The runner-up for the trophy was Boston University's Chris Drury, who was all over the ice in the match against Michigan.
Drury's perfect centering pass to Tommi Degerman set up Degerman's game-winning goal on a power-play in the second period.
Drury, a junior from Trumbull, Conn., has been drafted by the Colorado Avalanche but has not been offered a contract and will most likely play a final season for the Terriers, the Hockey East champions.
Boston University will meet North Dakota of the Western Association in the championship game Saturday afternoon here at Bradley Center.
The Fighting Sioux from Grand Forks, N.D., reached the championship game by defeating Colorado College, 6-2, in another semifinal match.
North Dakota's sophomore center, Jason Blake, was one of the 10 Hobey Baker finalists. The winner was chosen by a panel of coaches, scouts and news media representatives.

HEADLINE: Now, Beijing Hears Another U.S. Voice: Gingrich's

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER 

DATELINE: BEIJING, Saturday, March 29

 BODY:
Speaker Newt Gingrich told China's Communist Party leaders on Friday that they would have to begin tolerating freedom of speech, assembly and religion if they wanted the respect of Hong Kong, Taiwan and the international community.
Speaking far more bluntly than Vice President Al Gore, who visited China this week, Mr. Gingrich called on China's leaders to release political prisoners, stop arresting religious leaders and cooperate with the United States to track down any "renegade" Chinese officials who might have tried to funnel illegal campaign contributions to American politicians. 
 But at the same time, the Speaker of the House said in an interview here and in a speech to business executives in Hong Kong that Washington sought an approach to China that would promote the rule of law and greater religious freedom. He repeated his Hong Kong speech here this morning to a group of students.
"I mean, we don't want people in jail," he said in an interview at the China World Hotel after his meeting with President Jiang Zemin. "We don't want persecution of people who are engaged in religion." China routinely arrests and persecutes Catholic clerics who are not members of the state-controlled Catholic Patriotic Association.
Mr. Gingrich said Congress would like to play its own role in opening a dialogue with Chinese leaders under a "coordinated foreign policy" with the Administration. In his speech this morning, he said American emphasis on democracy was "the greatest gift" the United States could give to the world.
The Speaker's muscular formulation of American expectations for China and his commitment to support President Clinton's policy of engagement reflected the political pressure on Mr. Gingrich as he tries to recover from an ethics investigation, even as new criticism arises over his ideological commitment to the "Republican revolution."
His trenchant remarks about repression were an effort to appease the hard-line wing of the Republican Party, which has been calling for a tougher line on China, while his offer to open a new lines of discussion was an attempt to stay within the bounds of statesmanship.
Mr. Gingrich said he had held extensive discussions with President Clinton, Vice President Gore, the State Department and the National Security Council before delivering these messages, but it is not clear how great an expansion of Congressional involvement in China policy he expects.
He said he would "regularly" dispatch Representative Douglas Bereuter of Nebraska, chairman of the House subcommittee on Asia, to consult with Chinese officials on issues of contention. "He's there to help," Mr. Gingrich said. "He's not there to judge. He's not there to interfere."
Like Mr. Gore before him, Mr. Gingrich divided his time between tricky meetings with Chinese leaders and sightseeing excursions. Wearing pin stripes and holding hands with his more casually dressed wife, Marianne, the Speaker led his Congressional flock through the Forbidden City. Chinese tourists were pushed aside by the police.
Naturally, the Speaker with a Tyrannosaurus rex skeleton in his House office wanted to go to the National History Museum. "Do you want to go see the dinosaurs with me?" he asked reporters excitedly.
Unlike Mr. Gore, who seemed a bit stiff as he pulled out his charts on carbon dioxide emissions, Mr. Gingrich seemed to relish the opportunity to argue history with the Chinese.
"They don't apparently get many historians coming through," he said. "These are people who pride themselves on depth of history, on complexity and thinking strategically, and I think it's been sort of enjoyable to swap."
The arrival in Beijing of Mr. Gingrich and a 12-member bipartisan Congressional delegation followed a four-day visit by Mr. Gore, the highest-level American official to visit China since the Tiananmen massacre of 1989. Mr. Gingrich and his colleagues met on Friday with President Jiang, Prime Minister Li Peng and other senior officials.
Like Mr. Gore, Mr. Gingrich said the Chinese had raised and denied allegations that they had illegally funneled money into American political campaigns.
"I simply said that on the assumption that their Government statement that they were not involved is true, that meant there were apparently renegades that were violating both our law" and Chinese Government policy "and that we would appreciate their help as we seek to determine who these renegades are." The Chinese response "was neither positive nor negative," he said.
Where Mr. Gore gingerly admonished Beijing on the need for political reform and for greater protection of human rights, Mr. Gingrich spoke more bluntly.
"I characterized Hong Kong as an orchid," he said. "It is very delicate, and a giant can crush an orchid while having the best of intentions."
Mr. Gore bypassed Hong Kong on his trip, but Mr. Gingrich stopped there on Thursday to deliver a pointed speech about freedom as the British colony prepares for its return to Chinese sovereignty on July 1.
"No American leader would be true to our tradition if they came here and congratulated you on your economic achievements without also saying we believe that economic vitality ultimately depends on political and personal freedom," Mr. Gingrich told an American Chamber of Commerce audience. "For that reason, America cannot remain silent about the lack of basic freedom -- speech, religion, assembly, the press -- in China.
"The truth is that any effort to provide a partial freedom to any people, to tell them that they can be free in one sphere but not in another, will ultimately fail. China needs to understand that political freedom must accompany economic freedom."
Mr. Gingrich emphasized his concern about religious freedom, an issue that the right wing of his party has taken up as a cause celebre. Sixty-five members of Congress sent a letter with Mr. Gingrich for delivery to President Jiang saying China's Christians believed that "this is the worst period of persecution for them since the Cultural Revolution." The letter called on China's leaders to recognize "the deep concern in our country and in the U.S. Congress about the increased persecution of those who worship outside the aegis of official churches."
A relaxation of religious persecution began in the 1980's, and millions of Chinese have since joined officially supervised churches, but they have also flocked to underground churches, which are subject to frequent campaigns of repression like the one that has been under way since 1994.
Before his arrival, Mr. Gingrich discussed with aides the idea of inviting leaders of China's underground Roman Catholic Church to meet with him, but he indicated here that the plan had been abandoned as too risky. Instead, he will attend an Easter service at a Government-supervised Protestant church in Shanghai.
"Part of the tightrope we are walking on," Mr. Gingrich said, "is that it is easy for American politicians to come in here and have a nice press event and then leave behind an imprisoned person, and so we are following the embassy's guidance with great care."

HEADLINE: Growing Gap Between Old Allies Creates New Obstacles for Haiti

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti

 BODY:
Less than three years after the United States sent troops here to restore democracy, a bitter and widening dispute between supporters of the country's two most powerful political leaders has broken apart the American-backed ruling coalition and brought the Haitian Government to a virtual standstill.
The political realities of today are a far cry from the one-party dictatorship that opponents of the American intervention predicted in 1994. But the rift between former President Jean-Bertrand Aristide and the Government of his hand-picked successor, Rene Preval, has provoked economic and political instability and a mounting sense of concern among politicians and diplomats here. 
 The breach became a formal one when Mr. Aristide shifted his allegiance to a new political party, Fanmi Lavalas, while many officials elected under Mr. Aristide's banner have stayed in the original Lavalas Political Organization, which is rallying around Mr. Preval.
"This is really a struggle for hegemony, in which one side will erase the other," said Fritz Robert Saint Paul, a former leader of the Chamber of Deputies, the lower house of the parliament. Alix Fils-Aime, another deputy, added that the split between the two branches of the Lavalas movement, which originally brought Mr. Aristide to power in elections in 1990, "is pure politics and pure opportunism."
Relations between the two camps have frayed to the point that some of Mr. Preval's supporters are now openly blaming Mr. Aristide for fomenting recent outbreaks of violence that have undermined confidence in the current Government, which took office in February 1996.
In the last six weeks, more than 50 people have been killed in unexplained attacks, including six police officers, a Senator's bodyguard and the Justice Ministry's chief of security. [Two more policemen were killed this week.]
One clear byproduct of the split has been virtual paralysis of the legislative branch of Government. Parliament has not met in weeks because neither the Chamber nor the Senate can reach a quorum.
And although some committees are meeting informally, laws needed to comply with international agreements and to rebuild institutions left shattered by years of military dictatorship are not being passed.
Government ministries are short of cash, barely able to pay employees and blocked in their efforts to move ahead with essential economic and development projects that would create jobs and reduce the mounting pressure on the Government.
[On Wednesday the lower house assembled to vote on a no-confidence motion asserting that Prime Minister Rosny Smarth's Government was "incapable of coping with the current social, economic and political disorder and preventing the country from plunging into an ungovernable state." Though the measure was supported by Mr. Aristide's followers, it was defeated, 37-29, with 6 abstentions.]
On the surface, the two wings of the Lavalas movement have taken markedly different stances on some important and controversial political questions like the privatization of state enterprises. With vital foreign assistance linked to the results, Mr. Preval and Mr. Smarth are committed to a program of selling off inefficient Government-owned companies. Mr. Aristide and his followers, on the other hand, have sought to sabotage that effort.
"There is no alternative but to control spending and have more efficient fiscal measures," which foreign donors are demanding as a condition for aid, Mr. Smarth said in an interview. "We have negotiated this program with the international agencies, and since 70 percent of our budget comes from abroad, our latitude is quite limited."
One of the main organizers of the new Lavalas party, diplomats and politicians pointed out, is Fred Joseph, Mr. Preval's own Finance Minister and the official in charge of carrying out privatization. Two other Cabinet ministers are also founding members of the Lavalas Family, suggesting that the split has as much to do with personalities as policy.
Mr. Aristide, who remains the most popular political figure in this country and is widely believed to want to return to power, was not available for an interview. But in remarks this week to a conference of his followers, he appeared to accuse Mr. Smarth and his Cabinet of directing the violence, saying "insecurity is one means they are using to cow us and make us accept" an American-supported plan to restructure the Haitian economy.
The ruling coalition is disintegrating as the American presence and commitment here are declining, with Washington encouraging the United Nations and especially Canada to play more active roles.
[On Wednesday the Canadian Foreign Minister, Lloyd Axworthy, met with Mr. Preval and announced that Canadian troops will remain in Haiti until the end of the year, saying he recognized "there is a need for a longer-term stay to guarantee security."]
Mr. Aristide founded the Lavalas Family in November, describing it then as neither a political party nor a vehicle to advance his personal ambitions. But in January, his closest associates formally registered the new movement as a political party and began recruiting candidates for elections scheduled for April 6, in which members of 500 local councils and one-third of the 27-seat Senate are to be chosen.
Leaders of both camps suggest that the division can be traced back to a 1995 campaign within Lavalas to extend Mr. Aristide's term as President by three years. In forming his Government, Mr. Preval drew mostly from those who opposed the extension, apparently leaving some of Mr. Aristide's most ardent followers feeling neglected.
Kely Bastien, the current leader of the Chamber of Deputies, said that since most of the members of Parliament are from the original Lavalas party, it has an automatic source of power. "Those supporting Aristide don't have access to that power, and they want to use these elections as a way of gaining power," the parliamentary leader said.
But Gerard Pierre-Charles, who is the secretary general of the original Lavalas party, argues that the dispute is much more about basic principles of governance than about spoils. The underlying cause of the breach is "two very distinct concepts or visions of the state, change and power," he said, with Mr. Aristide wanting a situation in which "he decides everything," as Haitian presidents historically have.
"We want to get away from an exaggerated presidentialism that neglects political parties, concentrates power in the figure of one person and takes populist positions," he said. "We want to see a modernization of the state, respect for the system of separation of powers and an end to traditional patronage politics."
The recent acts of violence here are intended to "interrupt the road to institutionalization," Mr. Pierre-Charles said. When asked who was behind such destabilization efforts, he replied, "the people connected to the three years," a reference to Mr. Aristide's unconditional loyalists, and "those sectors which believe democracy has brought them nothing."
Mr. Aristide has particularly distressed former allies by allowing three former officers from the military he once castigated as hopelessly brutal and corrupt to run for Senate seats under his party's banner. One of them, Jean-Marie Fourel Celestin, was appointed chief of the Haitian National Police in 1995, but could not win Senate confirmation because of concerns about his past.
"We rejected him not only because he was incapable of doing the job, but because we heard a lot of disturbing evidence about him, including drug trafficking," said Senator Jean-Robert Sabalat, an independent who in 1991 served as Mr. Aristide's Foreign Minister. "To choose this same man as a candidate now is a deliberate blow to the Senate."
Several political leaders and diplomats argue that Mr. Aristide decided to found a new party because he felt Parliament was becoming too independent and, by seeking to open investigations into allegations of corruption involving those around him, had endangered his own image. The Haitian Constitution prohibits Mr. Aristide from succeeding himself, but he is widely expected to run again in 2000, when Mr. Preval's term expires.
"The man felt that something was being contrived against his person and has reacted," Mr. Fils-Aime said. "He was in a position where he had to flex some muscles."
Mr. Preval's own role throughout the dispute has been ambiguous, political leaders said. He continues to socialize with Mr. Aristide, and some of the officials who support his privatization plan, risking their own political futures by doing so, complain that he and the Cabinet have not made an effective case to the Haitian public in support of their own program.
"Preval likes to swim around the outside" of the split, Mr. Saint Paul said. Circumstances have forced Mr. Preval into an alliance with the original Lavalas party, Mr. Pierre-Charles added, but the President continues to "identify himself ideologically with Aristide" and "is not a party man, and doesn't really like political parties or having to count on one" for support.
"We confront a situation that is very alarming, with the people in power divided and shooting at each other verbally," Mr. Sabalat said. "It is sad, truly a pity, and I don't know how the country can operate on that basis or change into what everyone hoped it would become after the 1990 elections."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 1150 words

HEADLINE: Welfare Family Advocates, Once Allies, Become Rivals

BYLINE: By RACHEL L. SWARNS 

 BODY:
The charter buses from New York City rumbled into Albany this week, carrying hundreds of advocates for the poor and disabled. Counselors for battered women and AIDS patients sat side by side, united in their opposition to Gov. George E. Pataki's welfare overhaul plan.
But when the lobbying began, longtime allies became reluctant rivals as they urged legislators to spare their constituents from welfare cuts. 
 The new Federal law that imposes a five-year limit on public assistance allows states to exempt 20 percent of welfare families from that limit. And as legislators weigh the merits of each group, advocates for battered women, advocates for foster parents and advocates for those infected with H.I.V., among others, find themselves competing for consideration.
In their view, they are sparring for space aboard a metaphorical lifeboat, battling over who will be saved (and keep welfare benefits) and who will not (losing them).
"Welfare reform hurts all of us," said Lisa Cortes, the outreach coordinator for the Boriken Neighborhood Health Center in East Harlem, who helped organize the group trip to Albany on Tuesday. "But you can't help but think, "Who is going to be saved?' "
As the nation's welfare recipients march steadily toward the day when benefits will cease, advocates and legislators across the country are struggling to decide: Which needy groups deserve continued public assistance in the new welfare world?
"We're playing God in some respects," said Rhoda S. Jacobs, a Democrat from Brooklyn, who as chairwoman of the Assembly's Social Services Committee has listened to the advocates' pleas. "It's a terrible choice to have to make. It's very painful."
In New York, where about 400,000 households currently receive public assistance, only 80,000 could be exempted from time limits if benefits were stopped today, said John Signor, a spokesman for the State Division of the Budget.
With so few seats, the lifeboat is likely to fill up fast. Advocates for battered women, for instance, say that victims of domestic violence are the heads of about 30 percent of all welfare families and could easily consume the coveted exemption slots.
Advocates for AIDS patients say that 20 percent of welfare recipients may be H.I.V. positive. "That's your 20 percent right there," said Michael Kink, the legislative counsel for Housing Works, an AIDS advocacy group.
Advocates for foster parents, on the other hand, say the number of foster parents caring for toddlers or children with disabilities is so small -- fewer than 7,000 families -- that no one would be hurt by their inclusion. "We're not asking for much," Edith Holzer, a spokeswoman for the Council of Family and Child Caring Agencies, said.
So far, Mr. Pataki has suggested exemptions for those people whose physical or mental disabilities prevent them from working. Some Democrats in the Assembly, including Ms. Jacobs, favor exempting victims of domestic violence, among others. The Republican leadership in the Senate has yet to say which group it will support.
Some groups would clearly overlap. The sickest AIDS patients and the most psychologically damaged battered women would qualify for exemptions under Mr. Pataki's plan, or for aid from the Federal disability program. But advocates worry that many people who deserve extra help would not meet those strict criteria.
Legislators and the Governor are expected to resolve those issues during negotiations over welfare legislation intended to bring the state into conformity with the new Federal law. Legislators in both parties said the final measure would most likely include language giving certain groups special consideration for the exemptions, but leaving it up to the counties, which administer the programs, to apply the guidelines.
For those who fail to win exemptions, Mr. Pataki has promised to invest $1.2 billion in a program that would offer vouchers for essentials like food, clothing and shelter when benefits run out. But advocates say that system would be inadequate.
That leaves advocates for drug addicts, for the disabled, for the chronically unemployed and for the homeless, among others, jockeying for the exemptions.
It is a polite but unsavory competition, and many lobbyists prefer to tiptoe around the topic and each other, avoiding any talk of division in the ranks. In the position papers they send churning through legislators' fax machines and in their conversations with politicians, they are careful to push their own causes rather than attacking those of others, emphasizing collective protest whenever possible.
"We shouldn't have to compete within this narrow window of exemptions, but we do," said Fred Brancato, the executive director of the Council of Family and Child Caring Agencies in Manhattan. "Everyone is being squeezed into a tiny box and the reality is that not everyone will fit."
No one knows how many heads of household will fail to find work when benefits cease, or which groups will be disproportionately affected. In the absence of such data, some states, like Michigan, have simply opted to put off their decisions until later.
New Jersey, on the other hand, has already decided to exempt the elderly, the chronically unemployed, those with physical or mental disabilities that prevent them from working and those who care for the incapacitated -- though officials acknowledge that these exemptions may change. In that state, too, advocates lobbied fiercely for their collective and individual interests.
"We told them, 'We're not going to take this competition; we can't afford to exempt everybody,' " said Kathy Krepsio, the director of policy and planning for New Jersey's Department of Human Services.
In New York, advocates joined forces months ago to attack Mr. Pataki's plan, which proposes sharp reductions in welfare benefits even before the five year cutoff mark. They have urged the Governor to use state funds, which would not be subject to Federal time limits, to expand benefits for the needy. And they have charged that the Governor's plan, which would also exempt people over 60 and those who care for disabled children, is far too limited.
"We're all in this boat together," said Mr. Kink of the Housing Works group. "The hope is that we'll work our way out of it rather than fight our way out of it."
Some say battered women have the best chance of winning exemptions because the Federal welfare law urges states to grant them special consideration. But even their advocates take nothing for granted.
"There are so many people who are legitimately in need that battered women are essentially competing for inclusion in that 20 percent," said Patti Jo Newell, the spokeswoman for the New York State Coalition against Domestic Violence.
"It's an uncomfortable concept and an uncomfortable reality," she said. "Everybody's scrambling. It's terrible."

SECTION: Section 7; Page 9; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1462 words

HEADLINE: Black Like Me?

BYLINE: By William Finnegan; William Finnegan is the author of "A Complicated War: The Harrowing of Mozambique."

 BODY:

OUT OF AMERICA
 A Black Man Confronts Africa.
 By Keith B. Richburg.
 257 pp. New York:
 A New Republic Book/Basic Books. $23. 
 I still remember a story Keith B. Richburg did for The Washington Post in late 1993. It was about the death of a 65-year-old Somali man who happened to be standing in the doorway of a Mogadishu pharmacy when a passing patrol of United Nations peacekeepers, having heard two shots, opened fire. The story was a small but devastating indictment of the United Nations force's "rules of engagement," and it exemplified the dogged, passionate reporting that Mr. Richburg did in Somalia from 1992 to 1994, while he was The Post's Africa bureau chief.
"Out of America: A Black Man Confronts Africa" is a memoir, organized more or less chronologically, of the years that Mr. Richburg, who is a black American, spent in Africa. Surprisingly, it retains almost nothing of the careful, fact-chasing spirit of his daily reporting -- though it does shed some light on how that reporting was done. Being black was sometimes an advantage, he writes, because it let him move unnoticed through places where his white colleagues in the foreign press could not easily go, which "helped me get some of my best firsthand reporting details." But being a black reporter in black Africa was more often a disadvantage, he says. It made the job more dangerous, threatening to land him in the middle of ethnic disputes when he was mistaken for an African, and emboldening bureaucrats and border guards to hassle him in ways that they would not hassle whites. Local politicians, he suspects, dumped more of their anti-white paranoia on him than they did on white colleagues. Above all, there was the persistent low-grade identity crisis he suffered as he struggled to define his relationship to the continent of his ancestors.
This crisis, which inspires repeated heartfelt assertions of Mr. Richburg's happiness to be an American, clearly accounts for some of his book's overwhelmingly bleak, almost phobic depiction of Africa. The big stories he covered -- famine and civil war in Somalia, genocide in Rwanda, cholera in Zaire, civil war in Liberia -- did nothing, for obvious reasons, to lighten his view.
There was irony in Mr. Richburg's landing in Africa. As he tells it, his reason for becoming a foreign correspondent in the first place was to "escape America's race wars, escape the pressure to choose sides." He'd been feeling this pressure since his school days, when other black kids resented his white friends, and it had followed him to The Post, where, covering city politics, he resented the inner-city view that he was "brainwashed" and working for Whitey. He found relief during an assignment in Asia, but the racial pressure found him again in Africa, where "various African despots and their spokesmen . . . suggested somehow that as a black reporter, I was supposed to report more favorably on them." There were also the delegations of well-heeled black Americans visiting countries like Gabon and the Sudan, gullibly toasting the local dictators. It is easy then to share Mr. Richburg's disgust and outrage.
His own take on Africa is, unfortunately, often as unsubtle as that of the Afrocentric ground-kissers. Disappointed by the failure of the multinational intervention in Somalia, he concludes, stunningly, that "in Somalia, there aren't any good guys, just varying gradations of evil." Noting that Hutu militiamen are carrying clubs in Rwanda, he is reminded of "The Flintstones": "The country has reverted to prehistoric times, to a kind of sick version of Bedrock." After reviewing the Hutu extremists' murderous methods, he even contends that "fully evolved human beings in the 20th century don't do things like that." (Are the Hutus, then, subhuman? And the Nazis and the Khmer Rouge, were they subhuman too?)
Mr. Richburg's prose, when he forsakes the newsman's terse, impersonal style, lacks the weight to carry major themes. And in his determination to show just how hopeless, how horrifying present-day Africa is -- "I want to put it all right out here, right in your face" --he falls frequently into bathos and shaky surmise. Watching people starve in Somalia, he asks, "Does anyone care about their names?" In fact Somalia is a country where family and clan -- and names -- count for everything. His free-swinging, ahistorical asides on American virtue -- "in America, we don't reach for our pangas if our tribe loses the election" -- also come across as gratuitous. At times he lets his polemic carry him far beyond the facts. "It often occurred to me that my yearly bill for dog food and veterinary bills probably exceeded what most African governments spend on health care." This is the sort of thing you hear grumpy, ill-informed expatriates and old colonials mutter all over Africa. Indeed, by the end of "Out of America" we have Mr. Richburg in South Africa sympathizing with whites there who fret that "Africa" is coming to get them.
To his credit, Mr. Richburg lays out his own confusion and guilt about saying some of the things he does. "I found myself thinking things that one shouldn't really be thinking, feeling what I know must not be felt." He doesn't pretend to feel more than he does at Goree Island, the old embarkation point off the coast of Senegal for slaves being shipped to the New World. While he is adamant that he is "not making a defense of slavery," he is candid about his gratitude that his ancestors made it to America. He is also candid about feeling "cynical, jaded" and elsewhere "bitter and largely devoid of hope, and largely drained of compassion" -- a condition for which he blames "Africa" entirely.
His claims to be revealing unpopular truths about the dire state of Africa -- "I'm trying to tell it to you straight" -- ring hollow, however, in an era when writing off Africa (and much of the rest of the third world) has long been a popular exercise in the more truculent journals and think tanks of the developed world. Mr. Richburg lambastes whites in the West who, for fear of appearing racist, hesitate to place responsibility for Africa's woes on African shoulders, and then he extends this criticism to white Americans who are allegedly afraid to hold black Americans responsible for their own woes. He himself has some stern things to say about various hot-button topics in black America -- separatism, dependency, paranoia (he's against all three) -- but seems conveniently unaware that he is in comfortable, tenured company saying them.
What he doesn't have much to say about, oddly enough, is Africa. Shocking images, bitter jibes and bits of potted history amount to little, finally, in a book that remains, in the preferred mode of American publishing today, relentlessly focused on the author's thoughts and feelings. There are few characters of any heft in this fervently self-absorbed volume, few whole scenes, scant dialogue -- almost no concrete sense, really, of how things look and sound and feel, or of how ordinary Africans live. But Africa, for all its political and economic problems, is far more than the sum of its horrors. It is a place of staggering complexity, natural beauty and intense sensuality -- all of which Mr. Richburg seems too busy with his jeremiad to notice.
And when he widens his lens to compare, at some length, the postwar economic performances of East Asia and sub-Saharan Africa (finding Africa, of course, severely wanting), he fails to take into account some of the most basic factors, like climate, soil and irrigation, that have so far helped prevent African agriculture from achieving its own green revolution, without which prosperity will never come. Indeed, he doesn't even mention agriculture in this discussion, preferring to focus on the shortcomings of African "culture."
Much of Africa is in awful shape, clearly. War, corruption, hunger, disease, environmental destruction and a world economy increasingly indifferent to African products and markets have all contributed to a broad decline in the quality of life for many Africans. Mr. Richburg cites a few countries not currently trapped in the downward spiral -- South Africa and Zimbabwe, where white businessmen still essentially control the economies; Malawi, where a dictator was democratically overthrown; and Mozambique, where decades of war recently ended -- as exceptions that, somehow, prove the rule. In fact, many other countries -- Botswana, Namibia, Tanzania, Eritrea, Ethiopia, Ghana, Mali, Senegal, Benin, Ivory Coast -- are stable or finding their feet. Keith Richburg may have stayed true to his own experience with this wildly overdrawn portrait of Africa's agonies, but he has poorly served both his American readers and his subject.

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 3; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1324 words

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Robert Sternberg;
The Trouble With School Testing Systems

BYLINE: By NANCY POLK 

 BODY:
LIKE students everywhere, Robert Sternberg hates tests. But as a parent, a professor and a taxpayer, he is also sympathetic to adults' need to know just what they are getting for their investment.
Professor Sternberg, the I.B.M. professor of psychology and education at Yale, believes the desire for accountability in American education is overdue. "Having a standard that you're held to always makes sense," he said. 
 "The problem is that the standard they are being held to isn't the right standard," he said. As for the familiar tests: "They are the wrong measurement. They measure trivial facts."
In his latest book, "Successful Intelligence: How Practical and Creative Intelligence Determine Success in Life" (Simon and Schuster), Professor Sternberg dismisses the Scholastic Aptitude Test, Graduate Record Exam, Law School Admission Test and the Medical College Admission Test, all perhaps better knows by their acronyms: S.A.T.'s, G.R.E.'s, LSAT's and MCAT's.
Professor Sternberg has discussed testing with his two children, Seth, 18 and recently accepted at Yale for next fall, and Sara, 16. "I told them there's more to life, more to successful intelligence, than just these test scores. Doing well is a kind of game, a stupid game. Kids all know what going on," he said.
Recently Professor Sternberg discussed his ideas about testing and how to change and improve educational assessment. Following are excerpts from that conversation:
Q. Why do you object to standardized tests?
A. Together, tests of practical, creative and analytical intelligence are much greater predictors of success in life than those standardized exams, which only measure analytical intelligence. Abilities are more predicative of success than test scores. Unfortunately, testing is becoming even more important, with greater emphasis on S.A.T.'s, and in Connecticut on the Mastery Tests.
The field of psychology has grown and developed considerably during the last 50 years. But I.Q. tests like the Wechsler Intelligence Scales for Children used in elementary school have remained essentially the same since the 1940's.
College admissions tests have not evolved either. The S.A.T. in use today is not significantly different from the test introduced by Carl Brigham in 1930's. That's a disgrace.
The testing industry also determines the academic and employment future of America's children. At the end of secondary school students who want to continue must take standardized tests. Only a handful of American colleges and universities, including Bowdoin and Bates in Maine, have made admissions tests optional. For most, the tests are still mandatory.
I have no problem with appropriate testing. I think testing in general is fine. I have a problem with the narrowness of the current tests.
Q. Why does the testing industry appear uninterested in research and development?
A. They are a well entrenched monopoly. They have no competition; why should they change? It is alarming! Research and theory in psychology have moved since the 1930's, but to translate the ideas into tests is simply too expensive.
Q. Who are the testing companies?
A. Ability testing companies, achievement testing companies and the textbook companies are all the same companies. That tells you something. When you have the same companies serving all three functions, it creates a potential conflict of interest situation. How could it not?
We have created that system. It's not a natural born system. It was not handed down by the invisible hand of God. If you went back 40 years, we used parental socio-economic status, religion, race, which all still count to some extent. Now we rely on test scores.
Q. Who performs the worst on standardized tests?
A. The students who lose out tend to include more minority students and those who attend less affluent schools. It is also true that students possessed of a more creative or practical turn of mind do less well than their classmates. They are not viewed as smart in their own schools, they are not often the top grade getters.
I was considered a mediocre student in elementary school in New Jersey. I got low I.Q. scores. After that teachers treated me like a dummy. So I started acting like a dummy. I had a fourth grade teacher who expected more, and then I became a high performer. If I hadn't had her I would have gone through school as a poor student.
This is about wasted talent. Sadly these students who do poorly just think they're dumb.
Some students do succeed in spite their inability to perform on simple analytical tests but they are the exception and it is a stupid way to run an educational system. It is a system that is set up so that we waste talent as a nation. We have no call to waste talent; we need all the talent we have.
Q. Who is responsible for this situation? Is it teachers?
A. I do not blame teachers as much as anxious parents who worry that their children will not perform well on admissions tests and get into "the right colleges." The result is that we have created a closed system. Textbooks emphasize conventional analytical abilities, teachers are trained to teach to students strong in analytical skills and then achievement is measured according to this single criteria.
Q. What do you think of the Connecticut Mastery Tests?
A. It is a step above the conventional fact test but I dislike multiple-choice tests by themselves. America is almost unique in relying upon multiple-choice tests. For example, at the college level in England they use all essay tests.
Q. Is teaching style significant in test performance?
A. In a research study funded by the Federal Office of Educational Research and Improvement, we studied a group of high school students who took a Yale summer course taught in four different styles. One style emphasized simple memorization of facts, the other three stressed the use of analytical, practical or creative abilities.
We saw the worst way to learn was solely through simple memorization. The best results came when teachers matched their teaching style to students' learning style. When kids are taught and assessed in a way that matches their pattern of ability they perform at higher levels. Every kid will do better.
This matching is particularly promising for reaching bored and frustrated students who might do so poorly in school they drop out. Everyone can benefit when creative and practical abilities are recognized.
Q. Do you believe that practical and creative intelligence are equal to analytical intelligence?
A. I believe practical and creative intelligence are at least as important as analytical skills. Think about any field. These talents are critical in to occupations like psychologist, lawyer, doctor, salesperson, clerk.
Measures of practical intelligence predict job performance at least as well as or better than I.Q.
Q. What if a person is really missing one or two of the three types of intelligence?
A. I seek out people to work with me who complement my own pattern of abilities and styles. The really smart person is the one who capitalizes on strengths and compensates for and remediates weaknesses. Compensation means working with people who are good at what you not good at.
Q. How does the United States compare internationally in teaching styles?
A. In international comparisons United States students often score behind other countries. American teachers spend more time and deliver more information than the Japanese but in Japan they take time to carefully go through errors on tests. They have the kids reason through the problem. Although teachers know that it would be helpful to emphasize the full process of understanding, teachers tell me they do not have time. They must get through a certain amount of curriculum. It's ironic that teachers believe that if they give more time to the process rather than reaching the correct answer students will miss out.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Robert Sternberg, I.B.M. Professor of Psycology at Yale, argues for updating tests. (Joseph Kugielsky for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Q and A

BYLINE: By JANET PIORKO 

 BODY:

Victoriana in London
Q. I will be in London in late May, and would like to see the intact upper-middle-class Victorian house that has been preserved and made accessible by a member of Lord Snowdon's family. Could you give details? And perhaps suggest a restaurant or pub where one might further capture the feeling of the era? -- Jan E. Adlmann, Santa Fe, N.M.
A. You must be thinking of Linley Sambourne House, once owned by Lord Snowdon's great-grandfather Edward Linley Sambourne, a successful artist and cartoonist for Punch. Situated at 18 Stafford Terrace in Kensington, west London, it belonged to the family from the 1870's until 1978, when it was offered to the nation by the Countess of Rosse, Mr. Sambourne's granddaughter. (A footnote of interest to Americans: Mr. Sambourne's father, Edward Mott Sambourne, was born in Easton, Pa.)
The Greater London Council bought the property, transferring it in the 1980's to the Kensington and Chelsea Council, with the agreement that the property would be administered by the Victorian Society, a nonprofit charity that campaigns for the protection of important Victorian and Edwardian buildings. 
 Very little has been changed in Linley Sambourne House, which still contains much of its original bric-a-brac, according to Richard Seedhouse, administrator of the Victorian Society. He describes the house as "probably the best-preserved middle-class townhouse in Britain."
Built in the 1870's, the four-floor house is typical of its date and situation: Italianate in style, with molded stucco ornamentation on a structure of gray Suffolk brick. All eight rooms above basement level may be seen.
The house has stained-glass windows, original William Morris wallpapers, period furniture and a cornucopia of ornaments, lamps and knickknacks, including Neapolitan copies of Etruscan vases, Japanese vases, a French Boulle clock and a bronze nymph by Barbedienne, as well as ceramics and memorabilia.
The house is open from March 1 until the end of October on Wednesdays from 10 A.M. to 4 P.M. and Sundays from 2 to 5 P.M. Admission $5, based on $1.67 to the British pound. The closest Underground stop is High Street Kensington. Information: the Victorian Society, 1 Priory Gardens, London W4 1TT; telephone (44-181) 9941019.
There are a number of intact Victorian pubs in the city, but none is particularly close to the house, Mr. Seedhouse said. He recommends the Princess Louise, 208 High Holborn, WC1, (44-171) 4058816, for its magnificent interiors. The closest Underground station is Holborn.
 
'Saturday Night' Seats
Q. Is it possible to get tickets to see "Saturday Night Live" this summer? I'll be in New York City between mid-June and early September. -- Andrew Wright, Chicago
A. Alas, your trip coincides neatly with rerun season. The only way you would stand a chance would be to arrive earlier or stay later. The last show of the current season is May 17; the date for the first show of the 1997-98 season had not been set in late March, but it is usually in late September.
Even if you were to reschedule your trip, it would not be easy to get seats. Requests are accepted by postcard only, and only during the month of August (one postcard per person). Tickets are awarded by lottery throughout the season, and winners are notified one to two weeks in advance. They receive two tickets to either the live show or the dress rehearsal.
Standby tickets offer another opportunity to attend the show, though they do not guarantee admission. They are distributed for both the rehearsal and the live show on the morning of the show at 9:15 A.M. at the 49th Street entrance to 30 Rockefeller Plaza. One standby ticket is issued per person.
Write NBC Studios, Saturday Night Live, 30 Rockefeller Plaza, New York, N.Y. 10112.
A taped message describes the ticket procedure for this and other shows in detail; the number is (212) 664-4000.
 
The Train in Spain
Q. We will be in Spain in May and June, based in Malaga, and would like to tour the country by train. We are not under 26, but would like to buy a pass for unlimited travel within the country. Also, we would like to attend Corpus Christi in Toledo; can you find out the dates? -- Mary Saavedra, New York, N.Y.
A. Actually, of the many rail passes sold in the United States by Rail Europe, only a few carry an age restriction (under 26); most are available to travelers of any age.
Two passes for travel exclusively within Spain are the Spain Flexipass, which allows three days of rail travel in a two-month period, for $144 in second class and $180 in first class; up to seven extra days may be purchased for $32 and $40 a day respectively. So 10 days of rail travel would cost $368 in second class, $460 in first.
The other pass is a Rail 'n Drive pass that combines three days' train travel and two days' Avis car rental with unlimited mileage in Spain. The logic is to use the rail pass for the long hauls and the car for side trips.
Travelers have two months to complete their travel; the price starts at $199 a person for each of two adults in second class, $229 in first class, with a minimum car. An economy car with manual transmission, such as an Opel Corsa, is included in the lowest price; upgraded cars are available. With the most expensive car, a small automatic, the pass costs $249 for each of two people in second class, $279 in first class.
Prices are lower for the third and fourth people in a group. Up to seven extra rail days may also be added to this pass, as can additional days of car rental.
The passes (including the extra days) must be purchased in the United States before departure. For additional information and reservations, call (800) 438-7245. There is also a Rail Europe Web site at http:// www.raileurope.com
According to the Tourist Office of Spain, this year's Corpus Christi processions will take place on June 1 throughout the country, including in Seville, Cordoba, Santiago de Compostela, Avila, Burgos and Valladolid. In Toledo, the monumental silver-gilt monstrance normally displayed in the Cathedral Museum is taken out and paraded through the town. Streets are covered with white awnings, the ground is carpeted with flowers and herbs, and a bullfight is customarily held in the evening. JANET PIORKO
