HEADLINE: TRAVEL ADVISORY: CORRESPONDENT'S REPORT;
Alaska Airlines Opens Russia's 'Wild East'

BYLINE: By JAMES BROOKE 

 BODY:
FOR most of the 20th century, Russia's Far East has been off limits to Americans. The closest part of Russia to the United States, Russia east of Siberia, seemed also the most forbidding. Among its sites: Magadan, port of entry to Stalin's Arctic gold mines; Sakhalin Island, home base to the Soviet fighter that shot down Korean Air Lines Flight 007; and Vladivostok, a city closed to all Westerners when it was home port to the Soviet Pacific fleet.
But the ice curtain has melted, and Alaska Airlines is building a network of flights between Seattle and Anchorage and Russia's back door. 
 It began flying there in 1991. In its latest expansion, it will offer weekly service starting on May 10 from Seattle and Anchorage to the airline's fifth destination in the region, Yuzhno-Sakhalinsk, the provincial capital of Sakhalin Island.
In another innovation, this summer Americans holding multiple-entry visas will be able to city-hop within Russia on Alaska Airlines.
The airline, which says it makes money on its Russian routes, has built its service gradually. In 1991, summer service started to two cities: Magadan, a sister city to Anchorage, and Khabarovsk, a European-style interior city is the industrial and commercial hub for Russia's Far East.
In 1993, to facilitate trans-Pacific tourism and trade, Russia opened a consulate in Seattle and the United States opened a consulate in Vladivostok. That year, Alaska Airlines started flying to "Vlad," a hilly city overlooking the Pacific that once was known as Russia's San Francisco. After consolidating summer service, the airline started year-round flights in 1994.
The following year, the airline added Petropavlovsk, the largest city on Kamchatka Peninsula. Noted for smoking volcanoes and active geyser fields, Kamchatka recently was added to Unesco's list of places of outstanding universal value.
Now, with American oil companies starting to drill on Sakhalin, the airline plans to fly to the island. "With direct service between Anchorage and Yuzhno, it will allow the oilfield support companies to arrive there in six hours, ready to do business," said Douglas K. Barry, a professor at the University of Alaska Center for International Business. "As the oil projects go on line, traffic will grow enormously."
Building a flight network to Russia's Wild East has been as bumpy as its airfields. When an inaugural flight was to land three summers ago in Petropavlovsk, ground authorities told the pilot that he did not have landing rights, forcing him to turn the jet, loaded with dignitaries, back to Anchorage.
A few years earlier, when Alaska Airlines' inaugural flight landed in Magadan, there was no de-icing service at the airport, 400 miles south of the Arctic Circle.
"The pilot rounded up every bottle of vodka around, and sprayed it on the wings with a garden hose," Professor Barry recalled. "That's the pioneering spirit. Now the flights are predictable as clockwork."
The flights have helped to thaw relations between the United States and Russia's Far East, a vast and isolated region where 7 million people are sprinkled over an area two-thirds the size of the lower 48 states.
During the summer high season, Alaska Airlines offers two flights a week to most of its destinations. After September, it falls back to once-a-week service. Year round, the airline flies McDonnell-Douglas MD-80 jets.
Aeroflot, the sole competitor on the trans-Pacific routes, offers one flight a week from Seattle and Anchorage to three Russian destinations: Khabarovsk, Magadan and Vladivostok.
Aeroflot, which flies Russian-made jets, charges about $900 for its round-trip economy fares, about $200 less than Alaska Airlines's comparable fares.
The Russian Far East is slowly drawing a hardy band of business and adventure travelers. To fill a growing need, two English guidebooks cover the region:
"The Russian Far East," a highly readable 320-page guide written by an American couple living in Vladivostok, Allegra and Erik Azulay. (Hippocrene Books, 1995)
"The Russian Far East: A Business Reference Guide," a bible for telephone numbers and economic data. Elisa Miller, the editor, has prepared an updated edition that will be available May 1. Available from Russian Far East Update, a Seattle-based business bulletin. Information: (206) 447-2668.

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 2; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1243 words

HEADLINE: The View From: Waterford;
Prison Partners Make a Renovation

BYLINE: By CAROLYN BATTISTA 

 BODY:
AT the Eugene O'Neill Memorial Theater Center in Waterford, an 18-room Victorian mansion called Ironsides and a little stone-walled cemetery overlook grassy fields that stretch to a beach on Long Island Sound. There, with help from a prison, a long-stalled renovation is under way.
William O. Taylor of Massachusetts established the cemetery so that he could rebury the remains of his deceased relatives, including those of his grandfather, at what was once his grandfather's estate. He also donated $100,000 toward renovating Ironsides. The physical work -- from hauling heavy trash to finishing delicate woodwork -- is mostly done by inmates. 
 Such partnerships of private money and public labor may be a bit unusual, but they're fine with all concerned. The renovation is expected to be completed by June, the workers are learning a lot, and there's now another nice twist to the tale of two men who were never nice to each other -- Mr. Taylor's grandfather, Edward Crowninshield Hammond and Eugene O'Neill.
Around 1914, O'Neill was a young man who often sneaked onto -- and was thrown off -- the private beach belonging to Hammond, a railroad tycoon. Later the playwright would satirize Hammond, and Yankee millionaires in general, in his plays. Today the old Hammond estate is Waterford Beach Park, where the O'Neill Center leases buildings and about 10 of the park's 90 acres from the town, for a nominal fee. The center is known especially for helping playwrights develop their work.
For the O'Neill, as for most arts organizations today, financial support can be hard to find. "You have to be resourceful," said Stephen Wood, president of the center. The center began work on Ironsides several years ago, to provide much needed conference rooms, guest rooms and rehearsal space. But it took Mr. Taylor's gift, along with about $100,000 worth of donated labor, to "make the economics work," Mr. Wood said.
After years in which problems like water damage were getting worse, Ironsides is now getting spiffier by the week.
On a cold morning, Paul McMasters of Waterford, a restoration carpenter who's in charge of the renovation, made his way past bins of trash and piles of wood. He eyed stained-glass windows, arched doorways and tiled fireplaces; he studied paint samples in yellow, gold, bronze and a crimson called pigeon's blood. Everywhere, workers climbed ladders, lugged plywood, cut sheetrock and scraped and sanded assorted surfaces.
In an upstairs hall Mr. McMasters helped Fernando Santiago do some measuring in an area that needed attention before carpeting could be laid. "The floor's a bit rough," said Mr. Santiago, "so we're going to put in some one-inch plywood."
Nearby, Samuel DeLaTorre was sanding shutters. "The primer coat was too thick in places; I'm getting it nice and smooth," Mr. DeLaTorre said.
Mr. Santiago and Mr. DeLaTorre are on the inmate crews that arrive daily from the Gates Correctional Institution in Niantic. Other workers come -- although not as often -- from the Alternative Incarceration Center in New London, where people do community service instead of going to jail. Also helping -- with wiring, plumbing and drafting -- are students from the Ella T. Grasso/ Southeastern Regional Vocational Technical School in Groton. Local stores have also assisted; one, for instance, lends items like sanders to the project and sends personnel to demonstrate their use.
"This is a nice place to come out and work," said Mr. Santiago. "Paul is a nice guy to work for; he teaches you. Once you learn, he lets you do the job." Mr. Santiago, who likes the view of the Sound and the friendliness of the O'Neill staff, expects his renovation experience to be a help when he gets out of prison. "It's a start," he said.
"They keep me busy," said Mr. DeLaTorre. "They keep me positive."
Mr. McMasters -- known for wearing shorts, no matter what the weather -- said that in eight months, workers from Gates put in 3,250 hours of labor at Ironsides; workers from A.I.C. put in 340. "These guys work out well," he said. "They're not just getting out of jail and having a nice time. I send in work reports; I give them honest reports." He noted that besides learning specific skills, the inmates are getting accustomed to working with care and being treated with courtesy. "I have people here," he said, "that I don't think were ever treated with 'please' and 'thank you.' " The inmate workers have lunch every day in the O'Neill dining room.
Mary K. Maynard, deputy warden at Gates, said that five state prisons send crews of inmates to work on projects for public agencies or nonprofit groups. In the two years that Gates has participated, 376 inmates have worked on 78 different projects. The state pays inmates about $2.25 a day, for work within prison or outside. Working outside, "in a normal environment," Ms. Maynard said, is positive. What's perhaps more important, she said, they learn "that they have something to give."
She stressed that jobs aren't being taken away from regular workers. The inmate crews tackle jobs that otherwise just wouldn't get done, she said.
Thomas A. Sheridan, Waterford's First Selectman, agreed. "Nonprofits are hurting these days; this is a wonderful help to the O'Neill," he said. Of the inmate crews, he added: "Mostly, they're kids who made a stupid decison. It would be wise for Connecticut or any state to boost programs like these."
The only slightly unsettling feature of the project, said Mr. Sheridan, was the cemetery work: "You have concerns about moving dead people around."
Until last fall, the remains of Mr. Taylor's grandfather and other relatives -- and those of a family chauffeur -- were in Cedar Grove Cemetery in New London. But there had been some vandalism there, and Mr. Taylor, the publisher of the Boston Globe, thought of his grandfather's estate.
"He would always be there in the summers," said George White, a Waterford native and the founder of the O'Neill center. "We played together." Mr. Taylor pledged $10,000 a year until his death to maintain a small cemetery and its surroundings at the O'Neill; he also put $50,000 in an account to draw interest and provide maintenance after his death. He, his wife and another family member will eventually join the 13 others in the cemetery.
As for the family that lent its name to the mansion, "The Ironsides were related to the Hammonds," Mr. White said. Their house stood across the road and in 1987 was donated to the O'Neill by developers who'd bought the property and were selling off building lots. In August 1987 the 240-ton structure was moved to the theater center's grounds overlooking the beach.
As a young man, Eugene O'Neill loved that beach. A poem, "Upon Our Beach," went to his girlfriend, Beatrice Ashe, noted Lois McDonald, associate curator of the Monte Cristo Cottage in New London, where the O'Neill family summered. But, she noted, "He knew he wasn't welcome."
Years later, O'Neill based two unpleasant characters in his plays -- Harker in "Long Day's Journey Into Night" and Harder in "Moon for the Misbegotten" -- on Hammond and Hammond's even wealthier neighbor, Edward S. Harkness.
Still more years later, Stephen Wood stood in front of Ironsides. He gazed from the front doors, with their images of Eugene Gladstone O'Neill, to the nearby plot with the grave of Edward Crowninshield Hammond. "They're back together," he said, smiling.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 41; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 859 words

HEADLINE: ARTS/ARTIFACTS;
For the Fabulous Eggs, The Magic Only Grows

BYLINE: By RITA REIF 

 BODY:
THE RUSSIAN JEWELER Peter Carl Faberge never publicized the exquisite Easter eggs that he made for clients in turn-of-the-century St. Petersburg. He shrouded their production in secrecy, even refusing to reveal to his royal patrons, Aleksandr III and Nicholas II, which tiny surprises he was concealing inside the eggs -- a thimble-size palace, perhaps, or a gilded carriage, or an entire train.
His wizardry worked; these bejeweled fantasies enchanted the Empresses Mariya and Aleksandra, to whom the eggs were given from 1885 to 1916. The Imperial clients were secretive, too. They never told the Russian people about the eggs and kept them in their private apartments, out of sight of palace visitors. The public's only glimpses of the eggs were mainly at the Exposition Internationale Universelle in Paris in 1900, at which Faberge was awarded a gold medal and the Legion of Honor, and at a 1902 charity benefit in St. Petersburg. 
 Indeed, it was not until after the Russian Revolution and Faberge's death, in 1920, that the eggs came to wider public attention. Newly available documents now show that Faberge made at least 50 Imperial eggs, of which 40 survive. Born of the lurid curiosity about the Romanovs' excessive life style and their horrible deaths, the fascination with the eggs continues to grow as more details of their history and their owners emerge.
"They are magical toys that fuel the mystique surrounding Faberge's works today," said Alexis de Tiesenhausen, Christie's Russian-art specialist.
Since 1987, with the beginnings of glasnost, Faberge's works have become increasingly more visible at museums and in the marketplace. "Faberge in America," the largest such exhibition ever held in this country, opened last year at the Metropolitan Museum of Art before moving on to museums in San Francisco, Richmond, New Orleans and Cleveland, where it will close in May. The show has so far drawn about 900,000 visitors.
Several auctions at Christie's in Geneva have rewritten the records for Faberge. In 1989, the Kelch Pine Cone Egg brought $3.1 million, and five years later the Imperial Winter Egg was sold for $5.6 million.
Now the Pine Cone Egg is back at Christie's, this time in New York. Enameled in blue and awash with swags of diamonds, the egg opens to reveal a walnut-size automaton in the shape of a silver elephant with an Indian passenger on its back; when wound, it sways, swishes its tail and lumbers forward. The Pine Cone Egg is the finest of the seven made a century ago for Faberge's wealthiest clients, Aleksandr Ferdinandovich Kelch, a gold-mining industrialist, and his wife, Barbara.
It will be auctioned on April 15 for Joan Kroc, the former owner of the San Diego Padres and the widow of Ray Kroc, who was founder of the McDonald's fast-food chain. She had been the high bidder in 1989; later that year, she exhibited it with 25 other Easter eggs, mostly from the Kremlin and the Forbes Magazine Collection, at Treasures of the Soviet Union, the San Diego arts festival. Mrs. Kroc, who maintains that the Kelch egg is as good as any made for the Romanovs, has not said why she is selling it. Christie's estimates that it will bring more than she paid for it: $3.5 million to $4.5 million.
Timed to coincide with the auction is the publication of yet another book on the subject, "The Faberge Imperial Easter Eggs" (Christie's, $125). It is the first to incorporate the new findings on Faberge from the Russian Government archives. The book is a collaboration of Tatiana Faberge, a great-grandddaughter of the jeweler, Lynette G. Proler, a Faberge dealer in Dallas who was the principal writer, and Valentin V. Skurlov, who did extensive study on Faberge and his clients in the official Russian archives.
What has come to light from the archives so far is the stuff of soap opera. Barbara Kelch, a mining heiress, left her husband and Russia in 1905, when she moved to Paris after much of her fortune was wiped out during the Russo-Japanese War. In 1920, she sold her Faberge eggs there.
ALEKSANDR KELCH HAD A harder time: unemployed for years, he ended up selling cigarettes on the streets of St. Petersburg in 1925. In 1930, he was arrested, tried and convicted of sedition by the Communists for corresponding with his former wife in Paris. He was sentenced to hard labor in Siberia, where he is believed to have died.
Mr. Skurlov's investigation also uncovered many invoices for the Imperial Easter eggs but none for the Resurrection Egg and the Cuckoo Egg, more recently called the Chanticleer Egg, both of which are in the Forbes Collection in New York. While Mr. Skurlov has no doubts that Faberge made them, he suggests that they were commissioned by Kelch, not the royal family.
Christopher Forbes, vice chairman of Forbes, dismisses Mr. Skurlov's suggestion. "Until we have fuller documentation, the debate is still open," he said. "Both eggs were identified as Imperial when two of Faberge's sons were still alive."
But he is still impressed by the public interest in the subject. "Every time I think this is the last hurrah for Faberge," he said, "another wave starts."

LENGTH: 1283 words

HEADLINE: A Town in New Hampshire That Is Courting Growth

BYLINE: By SUSAN DIESENHOUSE 

DATELINE: BEDFORD, N.H.

 BODY:
LOOKING forward to retirement in this pastoral town about 50 miles north of Boston, Carol A. Descoteau, 59, and her husband Harry, 61, paid $300 this month to reserve a two-bedroom unit in what will be Bedford's first rental apartment development.
Although they have not lived in an apartment for 39 years, the Descoteaus who are from Litchfield, N.H., south of Bedford, were drawn to Waterford Green, a 204-unit garden-apartment complex under construction on the eastern edge of town. They liked the size of the apartments and the extensive amenities offered by the developer, the Waterford Development Corporation of Needham, Mass. 
 "This is the life style we want," said Mrs. Descoteau, a part-time secretary for the New Hampshire State Police. "It will be pleasing to the eye, and a little upscale."
The Descoteaus were undaunted by monthly rents as high as $1,400, including hot water, but not other utilities. They will have a clubhouse, covered parking, no property maintenance, 24-hour security and not too many children or young singles as neighbors, Mrs. Descoteau said. The apartments at Waterford Green run from 900 to 1,500 square feet.
The Descoteaus and the Waterford Group are among many residents and developers who in recent years have zeroed-in on 250-year-old Bedford. Location has much to do with the town's growth.
The town is at the intersection of Route 101 and Interstate 293, New Hampshire's major east-west and north-south highways, and is 10 miles from the Manchester Airport. Its proximity to highways puts it within an hour of ski resorts, beaches and Boston.
Bedford has about 4,700 dwellings, almost all single-family houses. Last year the Buildings Department issued permits for 374 new housing units, 204 of them in Waterford Green and the rest single-family houses. In the boom year of 1986, it issued permits for 279 units, and in the bust of 1990, permits for 53 units.
The value of new housing completed last year was $32.5 million; the corresponding figure for commercial projects was $18 million. Since 1990, the population has grown from about 12,400 to 19,000.
Such fast growth is by no means universal in southeastern New Hampshire. The neighboring town of Amherst, with about 11,000 residents, prides itself on its ability to resist change. Last year Amherst issued only 112 residential building permits, all for single-family homes. Over all, new development completed last year amounted to $14.8 million in homes and $3.1 million in commercial projects.
Even in Bedford there are limits to how much growth residents will tolerate. While most of the housing stock is single-family homes, Bedford has permitted the development of multifamily housing, in part to encourage the commercial development needed to maintain a broad tax base, said Karen White, the town planner.
"Five years ago, we rezoned our commercial corridor to allow multifamily units so people could commute to work more easily," she said. But with two more complexes totaling 302 rental apartments now seeking town permission to build "people have mixed feelings," she added.
This month the town voted to halt further apartment development in the commercial corridor on the eastern side of town. On the other hand, as the town plunges into the highest volume of residential development it has ever experienced in one year, it feels prepared.
"Bedford is a very pretty, very expensive place to live and it started planning for growth 20 years ago," said Artherline L. Robersen, who has been town manager for the last six years. It had only limited municipal sewers, but it created a fund, supported by user fees, with which to build connectors to the Manchester and Merrimack municipal sewer systems. The connectors serve the commercial district, along South River Road. Work began in 1989 and continued through the next few years of recession.
"We figured businesses would flock to us when things picked up, and they have," said Ms. Robersen, who talks with a rapid-fire cadence and has a reputation as a wily negotiator. "The Sewer Enterprise Fund now has $500,000 in the bank," she said.
IN 1993, Bedford created what it called a performance zone on this eastern commercial corridor bordered by the Merrimack River and adjacent to the City of Manchester. In this area of about seven square miles, a mix of industrial, commercial and multifamily residential development is permitted. This is where Waterford Green is being built.
"We don't care what you do, but we do care how you do it and how it affects your neighbors," Ms. Robersen said. "We don't want cookie cutters. To keep the flavor of the town we want to mix it up, have a lot of greenery, big buffer areas and minimum signage."
Land already developed with small, old houses in the zone can be bought and consolidated into larger parcels appropriate for commercial development. "A good development program also thinks of redevelopment," Ms. Robersen said.
Another attraction to home buyers is Bedford's low property-tax rate of $17.99 for $1,000 of assessed value. "With a large commercial tax base, we can afford to have a property tax that amounts to only 37 percent of our budget," she said.
Jane E. Hager, president of the Prescott Investment Corporation, a subdivision developer and land dealer based in Lyndeborough, N.H., said: "There's a very good reason for all this activity -- a progressive, pro-active, professional town management eager to broaden its tax base. They don't always say yes but they're well prepared. Occasionally, you can find good government. I credit the voters."
Ms. Hager is developing 201 lots in three subdivisions here. She says she sells 30 to 45 lots a year at an average price of $70,000. Once completed, the houses are valued from $250,000 to $400,000, she said.
Buyers usually come from surrounding towns although with new businesses moving into New Hampshire lately, some have been transferred to the area. "This is a move-up community," Ms. Hager said. "Right now, the market is good but not overheated. Mortgage rates are good, land and house prices are reasonable. Lot prices haven't returned to the $100,000 level of the 1980's but have rebounded from the recession level of about $50,000."
Robin E. Simard, 38, and her husband Richard, 54, have three young children to raise as they plan for the retirement of Mr. Simard, the owner of an office supply company in Manchester.
Last November, they sold a 5,000-square-foot house here and bought a 3,000-square-foot, four-bedroom condominium at the Village Green complex west of the Performance Zone. "Even with the condominium fees, we spend only half of what we spent for upkeep on the house," Ms. Simard said. "In the house, the kids were isolated. Here, they can walk through the complex or bike to the town pool."
The developer, Peter Thompson of Peter Thompson Associates, started the complex in 1988. He built and sold seven units for $250,000 to $300,000 until 1991, when his lender, The Bank of New England, failed. In 1994, construction of smaller units resumed. So far, 33 have been built and sold for $150,000 to $250,000. Roads are being installed for 60 more units. Prices for those will range from $175,000 to $325,000.
Robert E. Shapiro, executive vice president of the Waterford Development Corporation, said that Waterford Green was able to obtain favorable financing with a 40-year Federal loan guarantee and an institutional loan from WMF/Huntoon, Paige of Edison, N.J. The financing, Mr. Shapiro said, "gives us a low monthly carrying cost." But the mainstay of the deal is the high demand for housing in Bedford. "This ZIP code equals rental dollars," Mr. Shapiro said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Waterford Green, 204-unit complex under construction, is giving Bedford, N.H., its first rental development. (Rick Friedman for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Critics Scrutinize Lilco Accord

BYLINE: By JOHN RATHER 

 BODY:
WHEN Gov. George E. Pataki traveled to Long Island this month to announce an agreement to reduce Long Island electricity rates up to 23 percent, top Republicans and Democrats were lavish in praising his efforts to fulfill his pledge of double-digit rate relief.
Now, less than two weeks after that visit, some critics are attacking the pact as flawed and misleading, and they are calling the cuts illusory. 
 The critics -- an assemblage of business, industrial, civic and environmental groups; utility experts, and a handful of elected officials -- said that the agreement was far too generous to the Long Island Lighting Company and that it was at the customers' expense.
The critics said the accord would also slow the introduction of competition, denying Long Island benefits that other regions may experience in the next few years.
Those criticisms and details of the agreement are to be discussed at hearings by the Long Island Power Authority. No dates have been set for the hearings.
Last week the plan continued to enjoy broad support among elected officials and others involved in the agreement. Some influential groups, including the Long Island Association, have not yet made a final judgment on the plan.
But the criticisms suggest that the agreement, after the initial reception, may encounter turbulence from customers who have grown skeptical about promises of relief from the highest rates in the continental United States. The rates would drop to about 10th place if the agreement proceeds, the authority said. But some critics questioned that estimate and said the rates would continue to be expensive, the second highest among major utilities.
Under the agreement, among the authority, Lilco and the Brooklyn Union Gas Company, the authority would sell $7.262 billion in tax-exempt bonds to finance a partial takeover of Lilco. The sale would be the largest municipal bond issue in United States history, producing fees estimated to run from $40 million to $70 million for the companies that would sell the bonds. Bond insurance and legal and engineering fees would add tens of millions more dollars.
The authority would use some of the money to buy the Lilco transmission and distribution system, as well as its 18 percent share in the troubled Nine Mile Point 2 nuclear power plant in Scriba, on Lake Ontario. Critics said that that share could be costly in case of an accident or upon decommissioning.
The authority would also compensate Lilco for the remaining debt on the Shoreham nuclear power plant and for tax claims. Those claims include a scaled-down $625 million payment for a $1.1 billion judgment for excess assessments for the Shoreham plant.
A settlement of the Shoreham tax case would relieve the town and the Shoreham-Wading River School District from paying their share of the refund. But the $625 million would be repaid by all Suffolk residents through electric rates that would be a total of $150 million higher than the rates in Nassau County over the next five years.
Although the accord would eliminate a major tax problem for the county, the town and the school district, critics said it would unfairly charge all Suffolk residents for excess taxes that benefited solely Brookhaven and the school district.
Under Mr. Pataki's plan, the Lilco gas business, electric generators and other equipment would become part of a new company formed by a merger of Lilco with Brooklyn Union, fulfilling the Governor's pledge to do away with Lilco. No layoffs of Lilco workers would occur, the authority and Lilco said.
The authority would hold an option to buy the power plants in four years, and it would buy electricity from the generators for at least 15 years, although imported power from other sources, including imports from a possible new transmission line beneath Long Island Sound, would also be delivered to customers.
The authority would control electricity rates. The company formed by the Brooklyn Union and Lilco merger would manage the transmission and distribution system for at least eight years.
Immediate savings created by refinancing the Shoreham debt at lower rates and by exemptions from other taxes that Lilco now pays would allow an initial rate reduction of 18.2 percent in Nassau and 16.4 percent in Suffolk, according to the agreement. It projects that the rates would remain frozen for at least five years. The lower initial rate cut in Suffolk reflects the Shoreham settlement. In addition, if the agreement is approved, customers would receive checks for $232 in Nassau and $100 in Suffolk, with $82 of that sum from an accelerated payment that Lilco owes from a racketeering suit in 1989.
In that case Lilco was charged with overestimating the costs of Shoreham to obtain rate increases from the State Public Service Commission.
Critics said the money remaining in that $390 million settlement had already been guaranteed to customers in the form of lower rates. They said using that money in the checks would create a false impression that the money resulted from savings achieved in Mr. Pataki's plan.
Gordian Raacke, executive director of the Citizens Advisory Panel, a watchdog group formed under the Lilco settlement, said the panel would oppose discounting the remaining amounts that Lilco owed. The settlement, Mr. Raacke added, stipulated that all money had to be returned to customers.
His group is concerned that customers would receive less than the full amount.
The accelerated payment would need the approval of Judge Jack B. Weinstein of Federal District Court in Brooklyn.
Under the plan, Nassau would have a peak rate reduction of 23 percent in the fifth year, a figure that represents what customers would save compared to what Lilco projects that it would charge that year.
Critics said that that way of expressing the rate cut was misleading because it was based on Lilco estimates, and not on rates that the P.S.C. might set. The commission, the critics said, could order Lilco to reduce rates substantially over the five years.
"These are not savings at all," Gregory A. Palast, an economist from Southold, said. "These are just the elimination of Lilco rate increases that in fact would never have taken place. It's all a fantasy."
Others said the $7 billion in bonds would be repaid through electric rates for more than 30 years and would be reflected in transmission costs that outside power providers would have to pay. Those costs would be passed on to customers, negating the benefits of lower-cost power.
The transmission costs and the authority's need to collect enough from ratepayers to pay off the bond debt would block the benefits of competition from outside suppliers just as the utility industry underwent sweeping changes to encourage competition, the critics said.
"This deal will keep competition and low-cost electric power off of Long Island for at least 15 years, because the deal continues to force all of Long Island to bail out Lilco's shareholders over Shoreham," said Wayne Prospect, a consultant to the Hauppauge Industrial Association, a group that represents manufacturers and other industries.
The association is a member of the Initiative for Competitive Energy, a new group that opposes Mr. Pataki's plan.
Other critics said the bond sale was set up to maximize the initial rate cut by deferring the repayment of interest on some of the bonds, increasing the total debt up to $1 billion. They said the checks and the initial rate reductions were timed to arrive in the fall of 1998, when Mr. Pataki will be running for a second term.
The chairman of the power authority, Frank G. Zarb, said that was untrue.
The critics hold views that are vastly different from those of Mr. Zarb, other financial experts and public officials who shaped Mr. Pataki's plan.
In one example, a trustee of the authority, Richard M. Kessel, a pivotal negotiator, said last week that the Shoreham debt, which the bonds would pay for, would be discounted 22 percent in the agreement.
The critics said that the agreement actually showed that Lilco shareholders would be paid 99 percent of the company's book value, which included the value of the Shoreham debt to the company. The critics said that showed that there was no discounting of the debt.
Many critics argue that the P.S.C. should have compelled Lilco to write off all or much of the $4.5 billion to $4.7 billion remaining in Shoreham debt before any partial takeover of the utility was attempted.
Mr. Pataki's settlement, the critics said, signifies the second time that a governor has been outnegotiated by the Lilco chairman, Dr. William J. Catacosinos. The first, they said, was in 1989, when Dr. Catacosinos reached a final agreement with Mario M. Cuomo on a plan under which Lilco abandoned the $5.5 billion Shoreham plant because of safety problems.
That accord created a so-called regulatory asset that allowed Lilco to recover the cost of the plant in rates granted by the P.S.C.
Critics said the total cost of the plant, including interest, would be $20 billion or more. But they said the total cost of the plant under Mr. Pataki's plan, when the costs of repaying $7 billion in tax-exempt bonds was included, might run higher.
Because Mr. Pataki's plan envisions paying Lilco cash for the remaining value of the regulatory asset, which is just a so-called paper asset, the agreement would require approval by the Internal Revenue Service of provisions that would allow Lilco to avoid about $2 billion in capital-gains taxes.
Mr. Zarb said an unfavorable rulings on that and other questions could wipe out projected rate reductions. But he added that he was confident that the I.R.S. would rule favorably.
Irving Like, a lawyer from Babylon who has been involved in issues involving Shoreham and Lilco for more than 30 years, said the plan given to the I.R.S. should be public. "Normally," Mr. Like said, "when a company asks the I.R.S. a tax question, they get a private ruling. In this case, however, you have a party to the transaction that is a public entity, the Long Island Power Authority."
Mr. Like and others said the tax questions and filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission for the bonds would disclose financial assumptions, costs and other insights into the agreement.
The critics appear to be convinced that after the agreement is more fully disclosed it will be rejected by customers and ultimately opposed by elected officials. Some officials have already questioned the plan. "This plan rewards Lilco stockholders far too much and Lilco ratepayers far too little, especially considering the billions ratepayers have already paid for the Shoreham asset," said Assemblyman Paul E. Harenberg, Democrat of Bayport.
The views of Democrats in the Long Island delegation to the Assembly could sway Speaker Sheldon Silver, Democratic of Manhattan. Mr. Silver, who would have to approve any agreement, has said he will want assurances that the plan is "as advertised."
Mr. Kessel, a Democrat, said he believed that the plan had wide support among customers. "They want their bills cut," he said. "That's the bottom line."
But because the agreement would take an estimated 12 to 18 months to take effect, other critics said there was ample time to examine flaws.
"It's not a question of what people think about it in March or April," said Charles M. Studness of Manhasset, an expert on utility finances. "It's what they think about it a year from now."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. George E. Pataki, flanked by Richard M. Kessel, left, trustee of Long Island Power Authority, and Frank G. Zarb, chairman, at announcement of Lilco agreement this month. (G. Paul Burnett/The New York Times)(pg. 6)

LENGTH: 986 words

HEADLINE: In State of the County Address, O'Rourke Sets Tasks for Board

BYLINE: By DONNA GREENE 

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS

 BODY:
IF Westchester lawmakers had any notion that County Executive Andrew P. O'Rourke would go out with a whimper, he made it clear that they were mistaken last week as he delivered a State of the County address filled with a number of calls for action.
In the course of the half-hour speech to the Board of Legislators, presumably his last as County Executive, Mr. O'Rourke said, "Westchester's future cannot and should not be put on hold." He proposed several new initiatives and action on some other items of old business, all of which he said could be achieved before he leaves office at the end of the year. 
 But while the audience in the legislative chambers of the County Office Building, largely made up of past and present county and local officials, interrupted Mr. O'Rourke with applause about 14 times and gave him a standing ovation, it seemed certain that some of his ideas would not easily win acceptance.
The one idea that seemed clearly a lost cause was a suggestion that if the New York Legislature permanently exempts clothing purchases from the sales tax that Westchester should ask for a 0.5 percent increase in its sales tax to make up for the lost revenue to the county, local governments and school districts.
Interviews after the speech with legislators also revealed differences of opinion on other initiatives of Mr. O'Rourke, including those dealing with welfare, the arts, taxes and transportation.
Mr. O'Rourke, who became County Executive in 1983, announced in February that he would not seek re-election.
In his speech last Monday he chose not to reflect too much on what he considered his accomplishments, saying he would leave that to county historians. Rather, he said, he and the legislators still had much to do.
Here are some of the proposals Mr. O'Rourke made or proposed again in his speech:
*Mr. O'Rourke called on the Board to take steps to require that in the future property taxes may only be increased by a two-thirds vote of the Board of Legislators, not a simple majority.
*Announced a program he called Power, for Permanently Off Welfare Through Education and Responsibility. Among its requirements is that all heads of households who are on welfare earn a high school diploma if they do not already have one and that parents of welfare recipients be held accountable for their children's school attendance. There would also be an expansion of county day-care programs and educational opportunities as well as drug and alcohol testing of welfare recipients.
*Called on the Board to enact a $1-a-day surcharge on hotel and motel rates and car rentals. The money would go to a fund for the arts.
*Announced that beginning in July, the county's Department of Correction will start a young offenders program, featuring a "military-style daily regimen with an emphasis on education, treatment and self-discipline." The program is meant to decrease the recidivism rate of first-time offenders. As part of the program, participants will be closely monitored and counseled after their release, he said.
*Called for a county Commission Against Bias, which would coordinate anti-bias efforts of other agencies and government levels and hold hearings on whether broader local antidiscrimination legislation is needed in Westchester. "Anti-bias protection should extend to all Westchester residents regardless of economic class or social status," he said, later adding: "State law fails to protect certain classes from bias-related violence and discrimination in housing and employment. That's simply not right."
*Suggested that the county ask the state for money to cover lost revenue if the State Legislature decides to exempt clothing from the sales tax, and failing that, that the county should ask the state for permission to collect an additional one-half of 1 percent sales tax, which it would share with local governments and school districts. Mr. O'Rourke said the county stands to lose $20 million a year in sales-tax revenue.
*Proposed making the county's bus system an independent, regional transit agency. As a free-standing agency, he said, it would give the bus system more flexibility to meet its needs.
"I think the County Executive made it clear he intends to keep working us and working himself," said Legislator Richard G. Wishnie, a Democrat from Ossining, after the speech.
Reaction from legislators showed strong differences of opinion about Mr. O'Rourke's proposals. Mr. Wishnie, for example, generally liked the idea of a fund for the arts, while Ursula G. Lamotte, a Republican from Bedford, said she did not like the concept of dedicated funds in general.
She added that she supported the concept of Mr. O'Rourke's welfare proposals, saying they were aimed at helping children and at-risk families. But other legislators questioned the availability of jobs for members of such families.
"I'm a little concerned with the welfare proposal, that he is considering adding additional stipulations to the existing parameters" for eligibility, said Andrea Stewart-Cousins, a Democrat from Yonkers.
Only on the matter of raising the sales tax was there unanimity among those legislators questioned.
"No," Mrs. Lamotte said.
Mr. Wishnie said: "I don't think it will fly."
Thomas J. Abinanti, a Democrat from Greenburgh, said: "I don't see us supporting that."
Mr. Abinanti and George Latimer, a Democrat from Rye, also expressed reservations about creating a new agency to run the county's transportation system. Mr. Latimer questioned how it would affect fares and service. Mr. Abinanti said, "We don't want a Westchester version of the Port Authority."
As to whether the Board could take up all of Mr. O'Rourke's ambitious agenda before he leaves office, George Oros, the Board chairman, said it could. But he added:
"We have an agenda of our own as well. It includes term limits, campaign financing and other things."

HEADLINE: THE AGE BOOM

 BODY:
Max Frankel's article on Social Security ("Less Medicare, More Magic") offered one of the best definitions I've read on the problem. Although I thought the handling of Social Security was sensitive and objective, there was no mention of the hardship incurred by the aged poor. 
 My hope is that an entire issue would be turned over to the problem of poverty in the U.S. We keep emphasizing the magnificence of the economy. We neglect the disparity between the haves and the have-nots and the growing drudgery of the poor in America.
 
VICTOR GOTBAUM
New York

HEADLINE: In the Region/Long Island;
An Auto Race Track's Last Lap

BYLINE: By DIANA SHAMAN 

 BODY:
IN its heyday, the Bridgehampton Race Circuit drew 50,000 spectators on a weekend. Its challenging loops and curves made it world renowned until a local ordinance adopted in 1983 required race cars to be equipped with mufflers. That drove professional racers away and eroded track revenues.
On Tuesday the historic track opens its 41st, and probably last, season before beginning a new life as a golf/residential community. The checkered flag was waved in January when the owner of the 516-acre property, Robert M. Rubin of Greenwich, Conn., won appovals for a zoning change to redevelop the site for a golf course and 20 residential lots. The controversial zoning change was approved by the Southampton Town Board in a 4-to-1 vote. 
 Under the proposed site plan for the community, which is to be called Golf at the Bridge, the residential lots would be clustered on 70 acres, and 153 acres would be dedicated to the town, with the rest of the land used for the golf course. The existing zoning had permitted construction of 114 houses.
Southampton's Supervisor, Vincent J. Cannuscio, said he had cast the lone dissenting vote on the plan because he feared that the herbicides and pesticides that keep fairways green would filter into the ground water and pollute local wells. The race track is in a watershed.
Golf course experts scoff at such notions, citing studies conducted by the Federal Environmental Protection Agency, among others, that show that properly used chemicals and fertilizers are safe.
Construction of an 18-hole golf course designed by Rees L. Jones of Montclair, N.J., is to start in the fall. The three-acre residential lots cannot be subdivided, but they can each contain two houses -- a main house and a guest house. No time frame for sales has been set.
Now that the race track has been doomed, some nearby residents are fighting to preserve it -- even to the point of suggesting that noise restrictions be lifted on specific weekends to bring back professional racing associations and spectators.
"It's true that the track, as it's run now, is noisy, but we prefer the track to having our wells contaminated," said Stacy Kaufman-Riveras, a lawyer and a resident of Noyac who is a member of the South Fork Coalition for Fresh Water, a group of residents opposing the golf course.
A lengthy environmental-impact statement prepared on behalf of Mr. Rubin, and accepted by the Town of Southampton, found that the golf course would have no negative impact on the ground water. But environmentalists are not convinced, saying that a chemical judged to be safe today may prove to be harmful tomorrow.
"The principal concern we have is that many of the pesticides and fertilizers that are approved for use by the Environmental Protection Agency and by the State of New York have already turned up in people's drinking-water wells across eastern Long Island," said Robert S. DeLuca, the president of the Group for the South Fork, in Bridgehampton, a nonprofit environmental organization.
Dacthal, for example, a turf-building product used by golf courses, is suspected of having contaminated local wells, and certain products containing it are now banned in Suffolk County.
Mr. DeLuca noted that his group was particularly concerned with the Bridgehampton race track proposal because of the property's location over a water recharge area.
"You may not be able to go back and retrofit past mistakes, but you can stop those mistakes from occurring, especially in a case such as this where the existing zoning didn't even allow a golf course," Mr. DeLuca said.
William W. Esseks of the Esseks, Hefter & Angel law firm in Riverhead, who represents the race track owner, Mr. Rubin, said concerns over potential water contamination were just a smokescreen for antidevelopment sentiment.
"The hypocrisy is overwhelming," he said. "The very people who are objecting to the golf course would be perfectly willing to see all 500 acres used for farming purposes."
Suffolk County has spent millions of dollars to purchase development rights to sustain farming, a program that has also been heavily supported through local bond issues.
A farmer, Mr. Esseks pointed out, is a heavy user of fertilizers and pesticides and land is plowed each year, allowing chemicals to penetrate the soil.
His client's golf course, on the other hand, would use only 80 of the 516 acres for fairways, and chemicals would be applied in a "very protected way," he said. After the turf is established, Mr. Esseks said, all the materials will be used up by the root structure, in contrast to what occurs in farming.
MR. RUBIN, a car-racing enthusiast, purchased the race track property 13 years ago, hoping, he said, to continue auto racing there. But critical to making the track economically viable was getting some changes made to Southampton's noise ordinance, which would have permitted professional racing to resume, he said. His various proposals to the town were repeatedly rejected, he said.
Building a golf course and limiting residential development to 20 lots, he said, is a better plan from every perspective, including an environmental one, than building the 114 houses that zoning permitted. Most people are satisfied with the idea of a golf course and a few houses, Mr. Rubin said, "but the constituency for no development at any cost is by far the most vocal and aggressive."
Experts on golf course design and management say that concerns over water pollution by golf courses are rooted in outdated studies done 10 to 15 years ago. "People will dredge those up, but they're not relevant because they made assumptions they couldn't prove, " said Michael J. Hurdzan, a golf-course architect in Columbus, Ohio.
Moreover, newly developed fertilizers and new grass types needing less fertilization further reduce potential risk to ground water, he said.
"Well-designed, well-managed and properly built golf courses don't pollute ground water," Mr. Hurdzan added. "Housing is a much greater polluter."
A management program for the Bridgehampton golf course was designed by Environmental & Turf Services, environmental consultants in Wheaton, Md., who also were the consultants for the environmental impact study prepared for Mr. Rubin.
Stuart Z. Cohen, who heads the firm, directed an extensive study of the impact of golf courses on ground water quality in Cape Cod.
The study, conducted over one and a half years and released in 1990, was commissioned by the Environmental Protection Agency and by Barnstable County.
It concluded that though some pesticides had been found, golf courses were not significantly impacting ground water quality on Cape Cod, which relies on its aquifer for water, as does Long Island.
Racing buffs who also opposed the golf course, hoping to save the race track, say that those hopes have been rekindled now that some local residents have said they would sue the town over the rezoning.
If neighbors could convince the town to mitigate its noise ordinance so that professional racing events could resume even two weekends a year, "we have investors who could come up with as much money as we would ever need" to buy and upgrade the track, said Guy L. Frost.
Mr. Frost is an amateur race-car driver and organizer of the Bridgehampton Racing Heritage Group which has been seeking to preserve the track.
Not likely, said Mr. Rubin, the property owner. "If tomorrow I said, I've had a religious conversion and I'm now going to try and run a race track and I started taking steps to overturn the sound ordinance, you'd have an even more vocal and equally aggressive minority of people say, We don't want the race track."
Dennis Macchio, who leases the track from Mr. Rubin, said he was resigned to the inevitable. "It will be a sad day when Bridgehampton Race Circuit closes and we see an important chapter in the history of road racing in the Northeast come to an end," he said. "The track has had nine lives and the question is, 'Have we used up the ninth?' I seriously doubt that we will see racing beyond this season."

SECTION: Section 9; Page 7; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

HEADLINE: Commercial Property/42d Street and Broadway;
Technology in the Front Seat at 4 Times Square

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
SINCE it is the major office building to go into construction in midtown Manhattan in the 90's, the structure being built at the northeast corner of 42d Street and Broadway could be expected to include a few new technical wrinkles.
When the project was announced last year, the Durst Organization, which is developing the 48-story, 1.6-million-square-foot building, struck an environmental theme. The building would have energy efficient lighting, special chutes for materials to be recycled and high-tech windows that admit light, but little heat. 
 The intention was to develop a building that was pleasant, efficient and easy to work in. The building, which has the address of 4 Times Square, will be named for its first -- and anchor -- tenant, the Conde Nast publishing group, which took 585,000 square feet of space. But the success of the semi-speculative project was insured when Skadden Arps Slate Meagher & Flom, the law firm, agreed to lease 660,000 square feet.
Since then prospective tenants have grown more interested in the area, and developers have been working with renewed vigor trying to put together deals that might involve the three other Times Square office sites. These are on the west side of Seventh Avenue -- between 41st Street and 42d Street, and between 42d Street and 43d Street -- and on the east side between 41st Street and 42d Street. Prudential Insurance, the owner, is trying to sell these sites.
While attention has focused on potential development, the Dursts, their architects,
Fox & Fowle, and construction manager, Tishman Construction Corporation, have continued to refine the design of a building that is actually under way.
If necessary city approvals can be obtained, 4 Times Square could become the first major office building in New York to have photoelectric panels incorporated in the outer wall of the building. The panels, which convert sunlight directly into electricity, would contribute power to provide lighting for the building and the garish signs that are a requirement for buildings facing Times Square.
In addition, the design team is working to incorporate fuel cells into the building's power system. Fuel cells convert natural gas into electricity, while emitting only water and carbon dioxide, a gas that is already in the atmosphere. The panels and cells would make the building largely self sufficient in energy, with managers purchasing supplementary power from Consolidated Edison, or another supplier as deregulation proceeds, as needed.
Energy specialists say that the value of the panels and cells would be twofold: to demonstrate that these techniques have advanced beyond the experimental stage and are now actually usable in buildings, and to give them a showcase in a prominent location.
"It's a great showcase for real technologies that work," said Ashok Gupta, a senior energy economist with the Natural Resources Defense Council, an environmental organization based in New York. "Someone has to lead in introducing new technologies to increase demand. Increased demand attracts new producers and the competition makes the price go down."
The plan to incorporate solar panels in the skin of the building came after several other ideas for generating power were considered and discarded, said Robert F. Fox Jr., one of the founding partners in Fox & Fowle. "We looked at wind power, but there was nothing available that would work now," he said. "Nobody is doing research on using wind power on office buildings."
Putting solar panels on the roof was considered as well, but they were costly, took up valuable roof space and had an unacceptable 30-year payback on the investment, he said.
BUT Daniel J. Kaplan, another partner in the firm, kept looking into the possibilities for solar power and found a place where they could fit -- in the spaces between the top of one row of windows and the bottom of the next row, spaces called spandrels. Since the spandrel panels are needed anyway, the only increase in cost was the difference between a solar panel and a conventional one, or about $10 a square foot.
Because of the design of the building, spandrel panels are relatively small, only 14,000 square feet of the 600,000-square-foot surface of the building. Still, they are estimated to provide enough power for six or seven suburban homes, or about 1.5 percent of the anticipated interior load at 4 Times Square.
One reason for the relatively small area for the solar panels is that they were an afterthought. "If we had known in the beginning, we would have designed in more panels," said Bruce S. Fowle, the other founding partner.
Since the outside walls of a modern office building are not structural and serve mainly to keep the tenants in and wind and rain out, strength was not an issue. The photovoltaic material is deposited on a thin piece of glass that is laminated between two other pieces of glass and installed in the wall.
The panels will be connected electrically to a device that converts the direct current they produce into the alternating current usable in the building. Mr. Fox said photovoltaic technology is expected to improve over time by converting more of the sunlight falling on them to power.
"We've designed it so that as the technology evolves, in 10 or 20 years, you could pop out the old panels and install new one to generate more power," He said.
If the panels will be the most visible mark of the design's novel approach to energy, the more significant part will most likely be hidden in the mechanical recesses of the building. The current plan is to install eight (up from three just a few weeks ago) fuel cells, each capable of putting out 200 kilowatts of power an hour.
If all eight run continuously, as they are intended to do, they would supply 12.8 million kilowatt hours a year, enough to handle 100 percent of the estimated power requirements of the signs or 60 percent of the demand by tenants. "You do not turn fuel cells on and off, because it takes two hours and a lot of power to get them going," Mr. Fox said. "You just turn them on and let them run for four and a half to five years. And they produce very clean power, which is important for people using computers."
Mr. Fox said that by using the fuel cells to provide the base electrical demand of the building, the Durst Organization, which manages the buildings it owns, will lower the rates paid to Con Ed for whatever supplemental power it needs. The utility, he said, not unreasonably bases its rates on peak demand -- reached for example on a hot summer afternoon -- since it has to invest in equipment to meet that demand.
"Using the fuel cells as the base load takes out that peak, so you pay a lot lower rate," he said.
Fuel cells work at about 96 percent efficiency converting the chemicals of natural gas into energy, but only about half of that is electricity, with the rest coming off as heat, Mr. Kaplan said. But that presents possibilities as well.
The developers and designers rejected conventional electrically powered chilling equipment in favor of devices that burn natural gas to produce cooled air. "We are looking into whether we could use the heat from the fuel cells in the air-conditioning equipment to supplement the heat from burning gas," Mr. Kaplan said.
The decision to use heat-driven cooling equipment blocked any chance to use ice storage as a means of reducing electrical power costs. This approach involves using electric chillers at night, when power costs are the lowest, to make ice. The ice is then used during the day to help produce the cool air all large office buildings need year round. "We looked into ice storage pretty carefully, but it would not quite pay for itself as it worked out," Mr. Fowle. He said using heat-based cooling changed the economics. "It would have worked if the building was all electric," he said.
Mr. Fowle said the failure to find adequate wind power technology was disappointing, since tall buildings have a natural draft, which can often be felt in elevator shafts. "There is natural air movement," Mr. Fowle said. "You could design a building to funnel the air to a paddle wheel array to generate power if the technology were more mature."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Robert F. Fox Jr. of Fox & Fowle with type of photoelectric panel that may be used at 4 Times Square. (Chang W. Lee/The New York Times)

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 4; Column 1; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1358 words

HEADLINE: IN PERSON;
The Duke and I

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN 

 BODY:
AS a stage director, Sheldon Epps has been there, done that. Miller in Minneapolis, Mastrosimone in New Brunswick, McNally in Philadelphia. Ibsen, multicultural style, and Shakespeare in San Diego, with Stoppard coming up. But theatergoers know him best as a conceiver.
A conceiver? 
 If that sounds too abstruse, call Mr. Epps a man with a concept. His is the name that completes the credit "Conceived by . . ."
Let him explain it all for you: "It is gathering composers' existing material -- buried treasures, mostly -- and finding ways of making you listen to it as if it's new."
It all began with "Blues in the Night" (Off Off Broadway, 1980; Broadway, 1983; London, 1987). Concept: three women in a seedy hotel; one piano player; 24 lamentations by Bessie Smith, Alberta Hunter, Johnny Mercer, Vernon Duke, Walter Melrose and other disciples of the blues.
It continues with a Broadway musical set in "the Magical Kingdom of Harlem in the Swingin' 40's" and titled "Play On!" -- a free-floating retelling of a comedy set in the mythical Illyria on the Adriatic Sea, written by Shakespeare in 1601 as "Twelfth Night." It opened on March 20 with a score advertised as "the best score in town." Small wonder: the music is by Duke Ellington, with more than a little help from Billy Strayhorn.
The next Epps conception, "Disgustingly Rich," is bound for his home town, Teaneck, having evolved from a cabaret show by the Production Company, an Off Off Broadway group he founded with Norman Rene in 1980. The revue, at the American Stage Company from April 24 to May 18, begins at the end of a penthouse party. Now it's the servants' turn to sing, drink leftover champagne and comment on the departed guests. The buried treasures are by Noel Coward, Cole Porter, Rodgers and Hart, and other emissaries of elegance. As with his other conceptions, Mr. Epps said he hoped audiences in Teaneck would "listen to songs they know and find out they don't know them at all."
When it comes to the songs in "Play On!" Broadway audiences may also not know who wrote them: Ellington or Strayhorn, who composed "Take the 'A' Train," the show's opening number and the signature song of the Duke Ellington Orchestra.
Strayhorn, Ellington's arranger for nearly three decades, dedicated his entire professional life to the service of Ellington but received little credit (or royalties) for his compositions. "Billy and Duke are the only two people who knew who wrote what, and they are no longer with us," Mr. Epps said. "Something to Live For," a stunning duet late in the second act between the Rev (read: Malvolio), the manager of the Cotton Club, and Lady Liv (Olivia), the club's resident diva, "has lyrics credited to Billy," Mr. Epps said.
"They were such a close team; they functioned as one," he went on. "They finished each other's musical phrases. They were four hands on a piano, instead of two."
Mr. Epps had access to 1,200 to 1,500 compositions, Ellington or Strayhorn in whatever measure, and many unpublished. "Most were written as instrumentals for the Duke Ellington Band, with lyrics added on much later," he said. "This was the big-band era. People didn't bother to listen to lyrics; they were dancing.
"One of the things Ellington wanted most was a hit Broadway book musical. He tried, but none -- 'Queenie Pie,' 'Jump for Joy' -- got there. So the heart and soul of this one is to celebrate his songs."
"Play On!" is one of several song-and-dance shows based on "Twelfth Night," the most musical of Shakespeare's plays. The title is derived from the play's first line, "If music be the food of love, play on." In its latest incarnation, Viola Wilhemena Caledonia Pea goes to Harlem (on the A train) to be a composer in a man's world. Her uncle, the Jester (Shakespeare's Feste), tells her she is horning in on a man's world. Hence, the motivation for the cross-dressing of Vy to Vy Man, who falls in love with Harlem's ace songwriter, who happens to be named the Duke (after Orsino, the Duke of Illyria).
For Mr. Epps, the jazz musical is a continuation of a longtime collaboration with Mercedes Ellington, Duke's granddaughter, who choreographed "Play On!" as well as "Juba," an Off Off Broadway musical about racism, which Mr. Epps directed in 1991. It evolved from two staged readings in 1987 by the Musical Theater Project of the Paper Mill Playhouse in Millburn.
Perhaps ahead of its time, "Juba" concerned William Henry Laine, the first professional black dancer in America; his love affair with Polly Spracket, a British actress, in the 1840's, and his rivalry with John Diamond, an Irish step dancer.
"It was not a happy show," Mr. Epps recalled. "But that was before 'Jelly's Last Jam,' and no one thought a tough black musical could survive on Broadway.".
Ms. Ellington took charge of the dancers in two touring productions of "Blues In The Night," a six-week Broadway loser, albeit a Tony-nominated one in a dismal season. But a minor musical turned into a mega-event in the career of Mr. Epps, who now directs four to five productions of the show every season. "There have been 100 in the last 10 years, all over the world -- in Africa, in Australia, in Japan, in Barcelona, all over the British Isles," he said. "Blues" even played at the McCarter Theater in Princeton in 1979.
Associate director of American Stage from 1988 to 1993 and advisory producer from 1994 to 1996, Mr. Epps is now "unofficially a friend and admirer of what Jim" -- James N. Vagias, the theater's executive producer -- "is trying to do in my home county." Mr. Epps is also associate director at the Old Globe Theater in San Diego, where "Play On!" had a successful world premiere last year. There he has directed a range of plays, from what he calls "a conventional 'Private Lives' to 'Hedda Gabler' with black actors as Hedda and Judge Brack, a Latino Lovborg, an Asian woman as Thea and a white Tesman." Next up at the Old Globe: "The Real Thing," by Tom Stoppard.
BORN 44 years ago to St. Paul (a minister who "always found someplace to preach") and Kathryn Epps (who taught home economics in Thomas Jefferson Junior High School in Teaneck), Sheldon Epps lived in Los Angeles until he was 11. "We moved to Teaneck when I was in the seventh grade, and there I stayed through junior high school and through college," he said. After graduating from Carnegie-Mellon University in Pittsburgh in 1973, Mr. Epps lived in Manhattan until 1985. "Then my parents moved to North Carolina and offered the house to me and my sister." He and his older sister, Frances, a flight attendant for Trans World Airlines, still share the house.
He discovered theater when he performed in a summer musical program at Teaneck High School. "I was a shy kid looking for a place to be. The combination of doing Lord Brockhurst in 'The Boy Friend' and the Prince in 'The King and I' and being close enough to see Broadway shows -- that was like Disneyland to me," he recalled.
Still looking like "a shy kid" after all these years, Mr. Epps stood at the rear of the Brooks Atkinson Theater before the opening-night of "Play On!" 10 days ago, unfazed by the din. He was leaning against a rear wall, smiling benignly. During the performance, he did not pace. At the final curtain, the audience whooped it up and hollered. Mr. Epps stood at the rear of the theater. At the teeming party afterward, he was not at the table reserved for him, but in a small room upstairs with several cast members.
Later, he mused on his life in the theater. "It's about getting the work right," he said. "The problems and difficulties are always the same. Solving them costs different amounts of money. Off Broadway or American Stage or Broadway, the difference is the number of zeros next to the problem."
Regional theaters like New Jersey's "provide a supportive, comfortable atmosphere for developing new work to ease the pressures and economics of producing in New York," he said. "You can't develop them otherwise.
" 'Blues in the Night' has made it possible for me to remain in the theater -- to have a choice. With 'Play On!' I hope I'll have a new annuity now."

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 5; Column 2; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 763 words

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY & CO.;
Office Buildings (Remember the 80's?) Make a Comeback

BYLINE: By KIT R. ROANE 

 BODY:
They bought the land nearly a decade ago when New Jersey was awash with new construction. It was a time when buildings were rising with little or no money down and speculators roamed the landscape to buy up parcels for new office complexes or commercial parks.
Now, six years after building virtually stopped, SJP Properties, a development and construction concern, is finally getting back into speculative office building, rolling the dice on a new eight-story office tower that was begun before it had any tenants. 
 "It was a risk but one that has paid off," said Peter Eppie, the company's vice president, at its headquarters in Parsippany. "No one had signed a lease on it until two months ago and now it's 80 percent taken."
"This shows the market is getting tight and demand is picking up again," he added. "We're so confident now, we're planning a second tower later this year. And it's being done completely on spec too."
The 240,000-square-foot building being erected in Bridgewater Township is perhaps the first commercial site developed completely on spec in the state since 1991, when SJP built one of the last. But this developer is not alone in seeing a rebound in New Jersey's commercial real-estate market. Dozens of other office buildings are popping up to meet the needs of corporations that are expanding and require technological amenities like fiber optic cable, rooftop communications and computer-friendly climate controls.
Noting that the state has already made up 85 percent of the jobs lost at the deepest point of the recession five years ago, James W. Hughes, an economist and dean of the Edward J. Bloustein School of Planning and Public Policy at Rutgers, said that other economic indicators, including steep occupancy rates in existing commercial buildings, pointed to a resurgence in the real-estate market.
Commercial vacancy rates in New Jersey are 16 percent now, trimmed from 23 percent in 1992, Dr. Hughes said. He added that class A spaces, the newest and best located buildings, were reporting vacancy rates around 9.2 percent in the most heavily populated central and northern sections of the state. Other economists, like Bob Cotter, director of planning for Jersey City, said that real estate investment trusts were selling well again and that banks were lending on proposed buildings with less collateral.
"While it is still in its early stages, the commercial real estate market does appear to be coming back," Dr. Hughes said.
"It remains to be seen how deep the demand really is," he added. "At least it's not a free-for-all of excess, like in the 1980's, when everyone pretended to be a builder. This is a much more rational and slower trend."
The 1980's building boom has come to be remembered as a sea of extravagance, when banks and thrifts lent millions to developers who offered little collateral and often no more than a promise of tenants. Buildings rose at such a pace that by the early 1990's, supply had greatly outstripped demand. Banks began to fail, and investors and taxpayers absorbed the losses.
By 1994, office construction virtually halted throught the country, with only 1.6 million square feet being completed that year in the United States. That was less office space than was erected in some weeks during the previous decade, Dr. Hughes said. In fact, 85 percent of all office buildings in New Jersey were built in the 1980's, he said.
"We have gone from one extreme to another and now we are going the opposite way again," said John Maddocks, manager of Somerset County's economic development office, listing several construction sites. "Not long ago you had tenants demanding the first year free to sign a five-year lease," he said. "You don't see that anymore. The market is tight."
Among the desirable areas are stretches along Jersey City's waterfront, often called the Gold Coast; Route 1 as it approaches Princeton, and the intersection of Routes 287 and 78, where SJP Properties is beginning development of six buildings on a 118-acre site.
Few of them suggest the excesses of the 80's, either in design or in financing. Still, the trend is toward more risk, with lenders granting more money for less commitment every day and builders seeing old plots as potential cash cows.
"Builders are becoming more aggressive and lenders are becoming more willing to lend," said Robert J. Wolfe, president of Picus Associates Inc., whose four-million-square-foot Princeton Forrestal Center is now 80 percent occupied.
"The real estate industry has a very short memory," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The skeleton of a new corporate center in Bridgewater is reflected in windows on a portion of the facade parked near the site (Norman Y. Lono for The New York Times)
.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 6; Column 1; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 726 words

HEADLINE: SCHOOLS;
'We Don't Just Baby-Sit.' Classrooms for Child Care With the Future in Mind

BYLINE: By GEORGE JAMES 

DATELINE: OAKLAND

 BODY:
Kyle Whittle, 3, has waved goodbye to his mother and yelled, "I love you, Mommy!" and then hidden behind his cubby in the classroom and jumped out at his friend, Kevin Johansmeyer, also 3, and said, "Boo!" Now he is dying an Easter egg in a cup.
"We'll leave it in there for this much minutes," he says with exuberance, holding up both hands with 10 widespread fingers. 
 "How many minutes is that?" asks Marybeth Moroney, 17, who is monitoring his egg-dying skills.
"Sixty!" says Kyle.
"No-o-o," says Ms. Moroney, laughing. And then she helps Kyle to count to 10 on his fingers.
So went another day in the child-development class at Indian Hills High School in this Bergen County community, where students like Ms. Maroney get to work with and observe pre-schoolers as part of their studies in family and consumer science, (formerly home economics) on their way to becoming schoolteachers, child-care workers, and, of course, parents.
The course, which began here last September as a two-year pilot program, involves, in effect, teen-age students running a free day-care center three days a week for 13 children, ages 3 to 5. Although it is new in Indian Hills, it is patterned after similar courses that have been offered around New Jersey and other states for more than 20 years. The original courses were designed to teach young people how to care for children in their own families at home, but as more women have entered the workforce the course broadened to include day care as an occupation.
"We just don't baby-sit them," said Elaine Arendas, a veteran teacher who developed the Indian Hills course. "All the activities, which the students in the class plan, have an educational purpose. To help with the growth and development of the children in our care -- physically, intellectually, emotionally and socially."
There were 11 pre-schoolers this day in a classroom at Indian Hills that the students have helped to design and transform into one with a pre-school atmosphere, with little tables and chairs, toys, puzzles, books and colorful decorations, like the Easter bunny decals on the windows.
The children attend from 9:15 A.M. to 10:45 A.M. here every Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday, said Ms. Arendas, taking part in activites like arts and crafts, music and dance, story telling and science demonstrations: how objects float or sink.
Thirty-four teen-agers like Miss Moroney, who plans to study elementary education at Rutgers University, are enrolled in the course, which is split into two 45-minute classes. Eighteen students take the first period, 16 the second. They assume roles as "floaters" or "observers."
Floaters supervise the children's activities and prepare their snacks while observers watch how the children respond to tasks, their teen-age "teachers" and each other. Each observer, who is assigned a child to observe for that week, evaluates the child's progress and special needs.
On Monday, Ms. Arendas said, the students meet to plan the week's activities and on Friday review how the class went and could be improved.
Lacie Edelstein,17, a senior who plans to major in child development at Susquehanna University in Pennylvania, explains how a guitar works. She then leads them in numerous verses of "Old McDonald Had A Farm," getting stuck only when no one can figure out what sound a panda bear makes.
Kelly Torell, 17, leads the children in a game of matching paper egg shapes by color and the designs on them. Besides dying Easter eggs the children also get to make baby chicks with Styrofoam, toothpicks, pipe cleaners and glue.
"It's really fun," says Vicky Kenney, 17, who plans to become a bilingual counsellor in English and Spanish. "At first you think it's just baby sitting. But it's not because you have to really think about what activities to do that are interesting and that they'll learn from. You also have to learn how to deal with different behaviors."
"I like the interaction with the kids," says Kathleen Pope, a 17-year old junior who hopes to be a kindergarten teacher.
The parents also appear to be pleased with the program. One of them, Janet Watkins, says of her 3-year old son, William, "He enjoys it. It's his first experience with school and he's learned a lot. To socialize and make friends. Everytime he comes home, he shows me something new he's learned."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Vinny Cavalo, a visting guitarist, entertains children at the Indian Hills day-care class. (Joyce Dopkeen/The New York Times)
