SECTION: Section 6; Page 26; Column 1; Magazine Desk 

LENGTH: 3066 words

HEADLINE: The Faces of Hedda Nussbaum

BYLINE: By Francine Russo; Francine Russo, based in New York, writes frequently about human behavior.

 BODY:
As Hedda Nussbaum, in a red coat with faux-fur trim, met the press on the courthouse steps in Manhattan on March 10 to announce a legal victory giving herself and other battered women more time to sue their abusers, she dabbed her left eye with a tissue. Photographers caught the moment and news accounts suggested she was crying tears of joy. "I knew they would say that," Nussbaum says with a hearty laugh two days later. In fact, that eye, which Joel Steinberg's fingers gouged more than a decade ago, "weeps" nearly all the time from an injured tear duct.
Nussbaum, 54, and I are chatting in the law office in suburban Westchester where she works. It is the last in a series of conversations we have had over many days since 1995, after the hearings in Nussbaum v. Steinberg. 
 When I first met Hedda Nussbaum in the summer of 1995, she was driving a teal-green Geo -- her first decent car after two lemons. She escorted me and her lawyer, Betty Levinson, to a women's center in a northern suburb of New York where Nussbaum helped to lead a support group for battered women. Resting on the back seat was her shipment from Avon. Remarkably, Nussbaum, one of the most famous battered women in America, is an Avon lady. She doesn't actually knock on neighbors' doors but sells the beauty products mostly to family and friends. That day, she raved about a night cream that smoothed her shriveled arms overnight.
This is one of those surreal moments blending the commonplace and the unthinkable that characterized my conversations with Nussbaum. Scarred on much of her body, she has your average vanity and interest in cosmetics. Her only special need, she says, is waterproof mascara on the lower lid of the injured, tearing eye.
Seen straight on in a flattering light, her face framed by abundant curls styled to hide a cauliflower ear, Nussbaum can look surprisingly pretty. But when she turns her head in profile, I am jolted. Her nose, reconstructed twice, looks as if it has been sheared off. The asymmetry of her features -- one cheekbone flattened, the lips askew -- is unsettling.
Nussbaum walks with a slight limp; her back is hunched like that of an old woman. I cannot look at her without superimposing the image from a videotape made by the New York City Police Department on Nov. 2, 1987, the night Nussbaum and her companion, Joel Steinberg, were arrested for the beating death of 6-year-old Lisa Steinberg. Repeated viewings have not dimmed the shock of Nussbaum's sunken, glassy eyes in a bruised and swollen face, the split lip, the inflamed leg that left untreated would have killed her.
In those days, before events like the brutal death of Nicole Brown Simpson or Susan V. Smith's murder of her two young children, the nation was riveted by the tale of two affluent Manhattan professionals -- Steinberg, a lawyer, and Nussbaum, a former children's-book editor at Random House -- and their descent into sadism, cocaine
and madness. When police officers entered their brownstone apartment in Greenwich Village, they found a chaotic scene -- Lisa, who was dying; Mitchell, a urine-soaked toddler tethered to a playpen, and Nussbaum, battered almost beyond recognition -- and opened it up to the public's horrified gaze.
Steinberg's trial in 1988 -- many people referred to it as the "Nussbaum trial" though charges against her were dropped -- was the first marathon TV trial. Viewers were hooked on Nussbaum's days of grisly testimony. Nussbaum waived immunity when she agreed to testify against Steinberg. He was found guilty of first-degree manslaughter in the death of Lisa and sentenced to 8 to 25 years in prison, where he remains, his request for parole recently denied. Nussbaum spent a year at the Four Winds psychiatric hospital in Westchester and is sentenced to be Hedda Nussbaum for the rest of her life.
Despite testimony that nussbaum was delusional and near death herself when Steinberg killed Lisa, many people would not forgive her for allowing her daughter to lie comatose on the floor for 12 hours without calling for help. Her acquiescence to Steinberg's "demand" that they smoke cocaine while waiting for him to "heal" the child still outrages those who believe a mother ought to die protecting her child. Friends and supporters say those people could not forgive Nussbaum because she survived. Even feminists were split. As Ann Jones wrote in her book about battered women, "Next Time, She'll Be Dead," many public figures joined Gloria Steinem's plea for understanding; others like Susan Brownmiller blasted her.
But there has been a sea change in the public's understanding of domestic violence since 1987, when it seemed beyond comprehension that any woman -- especially an educated professional -- could remain with a man who beat her nearly into oblivion. Since then, the psychology of domestic abuse has been better understood, especially the victim's profound attachment to her abuser. Women's shelters have multiplied, police departments have been better-educated and a growing body of national and state legislation, including mandatory arrest for batterers in some jurisdictions, has been enacted. But in 1987, Nussbaum was vilified for her inability to save her daughter.
When Hedda Nussbaum met Joel Steinberg in 1975, the 32-year-old book editor, shy and socially awkward, friends say, was entranced by the charismatic lawyer. After she moved
in with him, he coached her in nightly "therapy" sessions on how to walk, dress, get raises. Her popularity and success grew.
"I thought he was godlike," says Nussbaum.
Steinberg, inventing tales about sinister cults and convincing her he had healing powers, gradually isolated Nussbaum from friends and family. He hit her for the first time in 1978, Nussbaum says. A few months later, the beatings accelerated, and in 1981 he ruptured her spleen. Soon after, Steinberg arranged to have the infant Lisa brought to them. Nussbaum describes a honeymoon period, then more beatings, burnings and torture. She lost her job because she was frequently absent as a result of her injuries. Believing the beatings were her own fault, she strove ever more hopelessly to please him. Eventually she had to beg his permission to eat, to sleep, to telephone. So when Lisa lay comatose, Nussbaum did not call for help but obeyed Joel Steinberg so that he would "heal" Lisa.
At first, Nussbaum refused to consider Betty Levinson's suggestion that she sue Steinberg. "It was so awful to hear her say he was a healer," Levinson says. "It was spooky, terrifying." Not until Nussbaum heard that Steinberg was planning to blame her for Lisa's death did she agree to testify against him, and shortly afterward she filed suit for assault.
In more than 20 years of legal practice, Levinson says, she has represented dozens of battered women -- most of them affluent, educated and self-supporting -- whose batterers could afford their victims' medical care and therapy. But New York had a one-year statute of limitations on civil suits for assault. Battering victims often are in no condition to sue within a year, and in Nussbaum's case, Levinson wanted to hold Steinberg liable for all 10 years of his beatings. She saw her opportunity in a provision allowing the clock to be stopped if the injured person has been unable to sue because of "insanity," which courts have interpreted to mean "an overall inability to function in society."
The suit, filed late in 1988, sought $3.6 million. But it soon looked as if the disbarred, jailed Steinberg had little left after his defense costs and tax problems. Levinson and Nussbaum agreed to continue the battle anyway, hoping to make new law for other battered women. Levinson says she has donated more than 2,100 hours representing Nussbaum in this and other legal matters. Fourteen women's groups, including the New York State League of Women Voters and B'nai B'rith Women, joined the suit.
At the pretrial hearings on the statute of limitations, Levinson relied on the expert testimony of Dr. Samuel Klagsbrun, a psychiatrist and director of Four Winds Hospital, and Dr. Bessel van der Kolk, a Harvard Medical School expert on psychological trauma. Levinson argued that Nussbaum was not a masochist who enjoyed or chose to endure her beatings but a reasonably normal woman trapped by an extraordinary manipulator.
In court, Klagsbrun and van der Kolk testified that Steinberg had systematically brainwashed, isolated and tortured Nussbaum, who merely survived from one beating to the next, functioning at a low level incompatible with bringing suit. Van der Kolk also testified that like Patty Hearst when taken hostage, Nussbaum formed a traumatic bond with Steinberg that took many months apart from him to loosen. He also noted that constant traumatic experience causes a victim's fight-or-flight response to be replaced by chemicals that numb pain and terror.
"It was true in Hedda's case," Levinson argued, "and it is true for other battered women, that when your surroundings are limited by your captor, you become focused on the tiny, tiny events of your life, like 'Will I eat today? Will I sleep on the floor today? Will I sleep at all today?"'
Steven Liebman, the special referee in the case, wrote in his decision in New York State Supreme Court that Nussbaum's inability to get help or seek legal assistance was "consistent with prolonged and extensive psychological and physical abuse" and noted that in domestic violence cases, "the victim may very well turn to the tormentor for connection and support....The destructive impact of violence in such an intimate relationship may be so complete that the victim is rendered incapable of independent judgment even to save one's own life."
While it is still unclear whether Steinberg will appeal, the groundbreaking decision now opens the way for battered women in New York and other states to sue their abusers. Levinson calls for legislation to extend the statute of limitation that would eliminate the need for costly and burdensome individual suits.
During one of our first meetings, Hedda Nussbaum showed me around her tree-shaded cottage in one of New York's northern suburbs. In each of her artistically decorated rooms were displayed the few mementos of Lisa and Mitchell she was able to save or scavenge -- snapshots, crayon drawings, dolls. As she led me around and pointed them out, the effect was of a ghastly theme-park tour.
We sat down in her living room, and she took her prize possession from the lamp table. It was a photograph of herself, young and pretty, bending over Lisa, then a baby. Tucked into the glass frame was a small cutting of Lisa's strawberry-blond hair. Nussbaum tenderly traced the hair strands with her little finger. There were tears in her half-closed eyes and a cast of such desolation over her smile that it was hard to look at her.
Yet within minutes, she was bustling about to show me a T-shirt she had decorated with one of Lisa's designs. The splotchy watercolor often attracted compliments. "I tell them, 'My daughter made this!' " she said zestily, as if a thriving Lisa made it in school only last week.
Her feelings about her adopted son, Mitchell, are different. She recalls the profound physical attachment she had to him as a baby -- he was returned to his biological mother -- and she fantasizes about a reunion when he is grown.
The emotional leaps are dizzying. Nussbaum is by turns touching, disturbing, even funny. Her dogged persistence -- attested to, for example, by her thick folders of job applications and resumes -- is moving. But harrowing awareness of her past colors her every utterance and act. From small talk and easy laughs she slides into grief, then reminisces with an eerie detachment. She becomes incensed about an issue and talks nonstop. She gets sidetracked by flickers of anger: taking ibuprofen for her sore tooth reminds her suddenly that Steinberg wouldn't let her take aspirin for the pain of her injuries. At other times she laughs at memories like his sending her to forage in supermarket dumpsters. "He liked free food -- he was so cheap. And I was so hungry I wanted to do it."
Chatting about food sensitivities, being a vegetarian, decorating decisions, her clothes, Nussbaum could be the Upper West Side professional she was before she met Steinberg. But try to engage her on deeper issues of her life, and she often seems naive and bewildered. I find many of our conversations painful, but when I apologize for how hard they must be, she chirps suddenly with girlish enthusiasm. "Oh, no, I find it exciting." She wants to use her notoriety for good, she says, and has five albums bulging with neatly filed clips. But, in her fragmented state, she talks with childlike eagerness of liking her "fame," as if she has forgotten what she's famous for. If she connects the pieces of her life, she will confront a picture few of us could look upon.
"Everything reminds me," she says later, as if responding to my unspoken concerns. "When I'm occupied with thinking about something specific, I don't." When she chops the carrots for our tuna salad, when she studies a menu, she is so absorbed that it's clear that she experiences nothing beyond that task.
Dr. Judith Herman, author of "Trauma and Recovery," explains that this lack of "modulation" of emotions and "unpredictable expression of feeling" is typical of trauma victims, whether battered women or prisoners of war. When such people get completely in touch with their feelings, van der Kolk explains, "the feelings are virtually unbearable." He adds, "Others in similar situations have killed themselves."
At this time, Nussbaum is slowly inching her way over the shards of her life toward accepting responsibility for her own role in the tragedy. Where she used to insist, in complete denial, that she was a wonderful mother, she now says she loved Lisa very much and was the best mother she could be. She says she doesn't feel "guilty" for her relationship with Steinberg but "regrets" it.
Van der Kolk says much damage remains from her abuse despite what Klagsbrun calls "absolutely astonishing" progress. Nussbaum herself, when I ask about the scars that remain, the work still to be done, looks lost. Then she responds with a quivery little smile and a sad quip that is haunting. "No, I'm poifect."
On the last day of february, Nussbaum, keyed up about the imminent court decision, teases a lawyer in her office about his messy desk. Her path to this, her second job as a legal secretary, began after she left Four Winds and was retrained as a paralegal. "One thing I got from Joel that was positive," she joked during one of our meetings, "was an interest in the law."
Failing to get jobs as Hedda Nussbaum, she used a pseudonym, she says. But despite mountains of applications, her real identity always scuttled her prospects. She worked part time as a word processor and applied decals to salt and pepper shakers in a ceramics factory. Finally, a lawyer, Ralph P. Katz, hired her as a legal secretary in April 1993. When he died of lung cancer in January 1996, she was unemployed for eight months. She obtained 21 interviews using her pseudonym but after revealing her identity was never hired. Finally, she says she decided: "Enough of this. I'm tired of using another name."
Using her own name, she soon found a job. "Being a secretary is not what I want to be when I grow up," she says, laughing. Still, she says she feels more comfortable with herself than ever, more than as a rising editor because then "I was giving the credit to Joel rather than myself." A realistic appraisal of her options battles in her with an eager, wistful hope for a normal life -- career, husband and even -- through marriage -- children and grandchildren. Seven years ago she began a correspondence with a 10-year-old "foster child" in the Philippines and has proudly kept that girl's letters to "Mama Hedda."
Her notoriety makes dating difficult, but she has attended singles events and used personal ads and dating services. "Hedda, huh?" one man asked, questioning her until he confirmed his suspicions, then disappeared. Another greeted her disclosure with a "freaked out" silence. Still, there has been the occasional nice guy, the no-chemistry evening. Nussbaum has the usual set of standards for men, plus a special one of her own.
"Right now," she tells me during one of our talks, "I can spot a controlling man a mile away!"
She gives a resounding no to the idea of seeing Steinberg again but concedes that she has not eradicated all feeling for him. "I still feel good about how I felt," she says, remembering some of the happiness.
"A healthy recovery from domestic violence includes remembering the good feeling," Levinson says."It's a part of herself."
On our last day together in March, at her office, Nussbaum is beaming. The press coverage of her legal victory has been overwhelmingly positive. Her phone-message tape is filled with calls from well-wishers. One women's group in New Jersey has requested her as a speaker, and she expects other invitations. She hopes to publish a book she has written for children about domestic violence and soon will help edit a domestic violence Web site planned by the Greenwich Junior League.
With the lawsuit, she says, she feels she has accomplished something positive for women. "I'd like to turn the tragedy into doing something for" -- she struggles to find the words, gesturing helplessly -- "although, of course ...I wish the tragedy hadn't happened." She subsides into silence, closes her eyes and sits utterly motionless.
A question from Levinson rouses Nussbaum, and almost immediately she is speaking briskly and knowledgeably about the lawsuit. She says she had explained to her battered-women group that the ruling can include women who are emotionally and economically abused. "You don't have to look" -- she breaks into a laugh -- "like I did."
Nussbaum will soon undergo further reconstructive surgery -- donated by a plastic surgeon -- to repair her nose and her cheek. She has decided not to undergo a third surgical attempt to repair her eye.
As we leave, Nussbaum heads outside to buy her lunch. Nimbly, she applies her Avon lip gloss and, her limp accentuated slightly by her elegant high heels, steps into the elevator and then out into the street.

SECTION: Section 6; Page 42; Column 1; Magazine Desk 

LENGTH: 2948 words

HEADLINE: Clinton vs. the First Amendment

BYLINE: By Floyd Abrams; Floyd Abrams, a partner in the New York law firm of Cahill Gordon & Reindel, is a constitutional lawyer.

 BODY:
At 10 a.m. on March 19, the nine justices of the United States Supreme Court walked to their leather chairs, which faced the attorneys before them. On one side of a podium were lawyers for a coalition of civil liberties and computer-industry groups prepared to argue that the Communications Decency Act signed into law by President Clinton last year violated the First Amendment. Standing on the other side, ready to defend the constitutionality of the law, were lawyers for Attorney General Janet Reno and, by extension, President Clinton himself.
From any First Amendment perspective, it was a depressingly familiar sight. Government lawyers routinely defend governmental conduct. But it has become the norm, not the exception, for Clinton Administration lawyers to find themselves minimizing First Amendment interests and defending laws or policies that maximize threats to free expression. Time and again, the Administration has opposed serious First Amendment claims in court, acquiesced in serious First Amendment damage by legislation and ignored First Amendment limits in its own conduct. Even when the Administration has raised First Amendment concerns, it has done so haltingly and briefly. 
 It is not hard to understand why. First Amendment claims tend to be made by unpopular people or institutions who wish to say unpopular things. Popular speakers rarely need legal protection. To lend meaningful support to First Amendment protections, a President must be prepared to put aside popular political positions and defend the rights of those who say things of which the public thoroughly disapproves.
They include a cadre of sometimes disagreeable candidates: Nazis and pornographers; companies (including those that sell liquor and cigarettes) that seek to advertise; citizens who wish to contribute large amounts of money to political parties and others who wish to protest abortion within shouting distance of the clinics where they are performed. The First Amendment's reach also includes the often despised and always feared media. To protect such people and institutions to the fullest requires a good dose of political courage. Often a price must be paid. It has not been one Clinton has been prepared to pay.
In some ways, Clinton had seemed a perfect President to defend the First Amendment. A former war protester himself -- a First Amendment user, that is -- he had moved from his Rhodes scholarship years at Oxford to Yale Law School and then to a position teaching constitutional law before entering politics. While serving as Governor of Arkansas, he opposed the nomination of Judge Robert Bork to the Supreme Court because of the "threat" posed by "Judge Bork's constitutional theories of individual and civil rights," which were "perhaps the most restrictive ... of anyone who has been nominated to the Court in decades." In his first week as President, Clinton reversed the policy of the Reagan and Bush Administrations that had silenced doctors in publicly funded hospitals from advising their patients of their right to an abortion.
But he had a problem. Running as a "new Democrat," Clinton had carefully positioned himself to avert Republican attacks on him as a liberal. Repeatedly, he declared himself in favor of the death penalty and welfare reform. More broadly, he promised to promote family values, to defend children, to empower parents. He would use Government more sparingly than others had done but use it vigorously to accomplish his policy ends.
It was good politics. Some of it may even have been good public policy.
But it was at war with the First Amendment. No part of our Constitution is less family-friendly than the First Amendment. None is more hostile to government. The First Amendment unnerves many parents precisely because it protects the right to speak in a robust and uninhibited way. It unsettles Government officials when it interferes with their ability to make and execute Government policy -- sometimes even beneficent public policy. It is counterintuitive enough for any Administration to be genuinely protective of the First Amendment. For Bill Clinton's Administration, it has been impossible.
The Communications Decency Act, co-sponsored by Senator James Exon, a Democrat from Nebraska, was a response to the extraordinary level of vulgarity and sexual explicitness found in some Web sites on the Internet. In keeping with longstanding Congressional practice, the legislative response was not only unnecessary (technology already exists permitting parents to control what their children watch) but also of the most dubious constitutionality.
With maximum prison terms of two years and fines of $250,000, the law makes it criminal to post on the Internet material that is not obscene, that may have serious artistic value and that adults cannot be barred from seeing. So long as the material may be "indecent" or "patently offensive" to children under 18, it is unlawful. As Chief Judge Dolores K. Sloviter of the Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit pointed out, "photographs appearing in National Geographic or a travel magazine of the sculptures in India of couples copulating in numerous positions, a written description of brutal prison rape or Francesco Clemente's painting 'Labirinth"' may all now be barred from the Internet.
The Administration understood this well. In a letter from Acting Assistant Attorney General Kent Markus to Senator Patrick J. Leahy on May 3, 1995, when the act was being considered, the Administration warned that the law would "threaten important First Amendment" rights by imposing "criminal sanctions on the transmission of constitutionally protected speech."
Nonetheless, the Administration generally played a low-volume role as the legislation proceeded through Congress. Even when House Speaker Newt Gingrich denounced the law as "clearly a violation of free speech" and "a violation of the rights of adults to communicate with each other," the President stood mute, and his Administration made no effort to join Gingrich in opposing the legislation. (Gingrich himself ultimately voted for the bill.)
When the law eventually passed, as part of a far broader piece of telecommunications legislation, the President signed it without comment on the "decency" provisions. Since then, the Administration has vigorously defended it in the courts as a method of protecting children from "patently offensive" material on the Internet at the same time it encouraged adults "to use what has become an unparalleled educational resource."
Two three-judge courts, one in Philadelphia and another in New York, have already concluded that the law violates the First Amendment. Judge Stewart Dalzell, a member of the Philadelphia panel, described the Internet as "a never-ending worldwide conversation," which the Government is not permitted to interrupt. "As the most participatory form of mass speech yet developed," he wrote, "the Internet deserves the highest protection from governmental intrusion." Not from the Clinton Administration, which responded with a statement from the President himself, saying that "our Constitution allows us to help parents by enforcing this act to prevent children from being exposed to objectionable material."
A similar scenario occurred with the passage of the Military Honor and Decency Act of 1996. Adopted for the supposed purpose of promoting the "honor, courage and commitment" of American troops, the law's response was to bar Army PX's from selling magazines like Penthouse.
When the legislation was being considered, the Defense Department sent a memorandum to Congress stating that the law "would raise serious constitutional and administrative problems" since the rights of service members "may not be restricted without compelling justification." Again, the legislation was sent to the President as part of a broader piece of legislation, and once again the President signed it. When a constitutional challenge was mounted by Penthouse, Government lawyers defended the law as a "reasonable" effort of the Government "to disassociate itself from lewd and vulgar expression when such expression undermines a legitimate governmental objective."
On Jan. 22, Federal District Judge Shira Scheindlin (a Clinton appointee) held the law to be unconstitutional. "Citizens," she wrote, "do not jettison their constitutional rights simply by enlisting in the armed forces. ... Our Constitution has effectively protected us for over two centuries from living in a society where the Government intrudes on our individual rights by deciding what a consenting adult can read or view." The Administration has appealed.
In the Supreme Court itself, the Clinton record on First Amendment claims has been regrettably predictable: rejection of the claims and mini-mization of First Amendment interests. The Court itself, with seven appointees of Republican Presidents and two (Justices Ruth Bader Ginsburg and Stephen G. Breyer) of President Clinton, has routinely rebuffed the Administration's efforts to limit First Amendment rights, opting in favor of far broader protection.
Consider some examples.
*An Illinois public hospital discharged a nurse because of reports that she had made "unkind and inappropriate negative" comments about her supervisor and was "knocking" the obstetrics department. The
Administration defended the hospital, arguing that government employers may fire their employees based on such reports without doing any investigating to determine whether the subject of the speech really involved a matter protected by the First Amendment. The Supreme Court disagreed, concluding that "the possibility of inadvertently punishing someone for exercising her First Amendment rights" requires that government employers undertake a reasonable investigation of the subject of the speech before disciplining an employee based on the speech.
*The Administration argued for a provision of the Ethics in Government Act that broadly prohibited employees from accepting any compensation for making speeches, even when there was no connection between the speech and the Government employment. The Supreme Court disagreed, concluding that the law violated the First Amendment since "its prohibition on compensation unquestionably imposes a significant burden on expressive activity" and "on the public's right to read and hear what the employees would otherwise have written and said."
*In a significant case involving the protection afforded by the First Amendment for commercial speech, the Administration argued that Federal regulations that prohibited labels on beer cans from truthfully displaying their alcohol content were constitutional. The Supreme Court again disagreed, concluding that the statute violated the First Amendment's protection of commercial speech.
*In the area of political campaign contributions, the Administration argued that the First Amendment permitted limits to be imposed on spending by political parties. The Supreme Court rejected the argument, concluding that "the independent expression of a political party's view is 'core' First Amendment activity no less than is the independent expression of individuals, candidates or other political committees."
*The Administration defended the constitutionality of provisions of an injunction issued against abortion clinic protesters. It established a 300-foot "no approach" zone around the clinic and a 300-foot buffer zone around staff residences, and it also banned the protesters from using "images observable" to patients inside the clinic. The Supreme Court concluded that the three provisions violated the First Amendment since they burdened "more speech than is necessary to prevent intimidation and to insure access to the clinic."
What is most telling about these cases is not their ideological overview -- some pleased conservatives, others liberals -- but the Court's consistency. In all the cases, a Supreme Court that has hardly embraced an A.C.L.U.-like agenda has rejected the Administration's tepid reading of the First Amendment. While the Clinton Justice Department has occasionally taken a more sympathetic view of First Amendment claims with which the Court has agreed, it has never sought more protection than the Court granted and almost always sought less.
When prior Administrations cared deeply about issues, they vigorously tried to change the Supreme Court's direction. Franklin Roosevelt's Administration sought (and, with a sufficient change of judicial personnel, obtained) a significant shift in judicial thinking about the reach of Federal authority over most aspects of the econo-my. The Reagan Administration was unswerving in its efforts to persuade the Court to change its views on the right of a woman to have an abortion.
If the Administration believed it especially important to protect First Amendment rights, it might well have refused to defend one or more of the enactments in the first place. That would have left it up to Congress to defend its own dubious laws before the Supreme Court. The Clinton Administration has never taken this course.
The Administration defends many of its policies as having been adopted in the service of protecting a special, particularly threatened group: our "kids." It is on this basis alone that the Administration has defended the constitutionality of the Communications Decency Act. It is the same argument the Administration has made when it has sought, more than any other since Richard Nixon's, to affect the content of what appears on television. Intense Administration pressure in 1996 for the adoption of a V-chip on television sets was followed in 1997 by still more intense pressure for programs to be rated on the basis of their content. One of the advantages of the V-chip, the President has stated, is that it "might actually change the content" of television programming. So it might. But these poll-tested and undoubtedly popular Administration efforts to change the content of television programming run headlong into the single best-established First Amendment rule of all: that Government involvement in determining the content of what is and is not said is almost always unconstitutional.
The need to protect children is real enough -- compelling enough, the courts say -- that the law allows some actions to be taken that could not be held constitutional with respect to adults. But it is a dangerous doctrine too, one that could, if not constrained, result in what Supreme Court Justice Felix Frankfurter referred to as "reducing the adult population to reading only what is fit for children." If read too broadly, it is also a prescription for governmental involvement in areas that have historically been left to parents, not Government officials.
The Administration has exhibited a striking insensitivity to the impact of the efforts of some members of Congress to establish a "safe harbor" on television, a time period during which otherwise perfectly lawful programs could not be shown for fear of their adverse impact on children. Senator Fritz Hollings headed the Senate Commerce Committee when Attorney General Reno appeared to testify in 1993. Hollings had proposed making it unlawful for broadcasters to show "any violent video programming during hours when children are reasonably likely to comprise a substantial portion of the audience." Farewell, then, to "Schindler's List," which NBC showed one evening last month at 7:30 P.M. But even more important: what sort of First Amendment would permit Congress to impose such a limitation on any broadcaster's right to show the film? Or any adult's right to watch it? And how committed to the First Amendment could any Administration be if it gave Congress a green light to pass such legislation?
Testifying before Hollings and his committee, Reno did just that. Asserting, without qualification, that all the different forms of legislation then being considered by Congress (including that of Hollings) were "constitutional and can be passed," the Attorney General offered the Administration's imprimatur.
As a legal matter, the Attorney General's advice was dubious. In the past, the Supreme Court has concluded that the fact that material aimed at both adults and children contained predominantly violent depictions could not itself justify barring its sale. The Court has permitted (in one of its most First Amendment-threatening opinions) limits on when scatological terms in a George Carlin record could be broadcast on the radio. But it has yet to countenance any scheme that would bar "Schindler's List" or any other program that is far from being obscene from being broadcast at any time in the evening based upon its content. Far from acquiescing in the legislation, the Administration should surely have have raised dire warnings about it.
President Clinton himself has not always chosen to oppose First Amendment interests. At some political risk, the President opposed a proposed (and generally popular) constitutional amendment to the First Amendment itself -- the first in the country's history -- that would have permitted legislation making it a crime to burn an Ameri-can flag. And he has said nothing as shameful about civil liberties issues as did former President George Bush in his McCarthyite attack on Michael Dukakis as a "card-carrying member of the A.C.L.U."
But these are exceptions. The President, a former constitutional law teacher, is entitled to be judged on his Administration's entire First Amendment record. Sum it up this way: Lee Atwater would have admired it; Dick Morris may yet claim credit for it; Bill Clinton should know better.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: EDGEMERE;
Grant Fizzles at a Housing Project

BYLINE: By CHARLIE LeDUFF 

 BODY:
Two years ago, things looked good for the people of the Beach 41st Street Houses, a public development that had come to be known as the stereotypical social failure. Drug dealers were the major employers, criminals more prevalent than cops and hopelessness the companion of many schoolchildren, residents say.
But in 1995, the government came in promising to change all that. With the help of residents, the New York City Housing Authority secured a $48 million grant from the Federal government. A new school was promised, as were a center for the elderly, an athletic complex, job-training courses, structural upgrades on the 712-unit complex and best of all, low-interest loans to attract business to this isolated community surrounded by water and vacant lots on the east end of the Rockaway Peninsula. 
 What's more, the Housing Authority promised to hold a percentage of empty apartments for working-class people like police officers, to create an economic mix that would ignite a social rejuvenation. By the sound of it, residents like Frances Johnson said, life at Beach 41st Street was going to be a Shangri-La.
Then the roof caved in. "They promised to take us to the moon," said Mrs. Johnson, president of the Beach 41st Street Houses Resident Council. "They ended up dumping us in the sewer."
The deal fell through. The money, to come from the Department of Housing and Urban Development, was stripped from the development in January and reallocated to the Edgemere Houses, another project 10 blocks away, after officials of the Housing Authority decided that they could not work with the resident council at Beach 41st. Tenants in the development had exploded when they learned that the equivalent of 100 apartments would have to be destroyed as part of the redevelopment plan.
"When I saw them breaking ground near Edgemere last month, it broke my heart," said Patricia Frye, the council's vice president, who lives in the development with her 12-year-old son, Chris. "People were going to lose their homes. But housing officials didn't want to hear about it. We're not supposed to have a brain because we're project people."
The HUD program, known as HOPE IV, is supposed to give local community leaders and tenants a role in designing the revitalization plans for their beleaguered housing projects and more control over its operations.
However, it calls for the demolition of apartments. Eighty percent of the funds must go to razing, building and upgrading. It is something that housing officials like to refer to as the "alleviation of density issues." In cities like Atlanta, Chicago and Detroit, public developments that have been torn down were dilapidated and abandoned. But in New York, low-income housing is at a premium. Housing Authority officials admit that the Beach 41st Street project is structurally sound and was near capacity when the plan was rubber-stamped. The $48 million in improvements would have meant sacrificing some homes.
"We've asked the Federal authorities to reconsider the demolition requirement for New York City," said John Martinez, deputy manager of the New York City Housing Authority.
The agency, Mr. Martinez said, made every effort to come up with alternatives to appease the residents. A plan was floated to redevelop vacant ground-level apartments for nonresidential uses, and the agency promised to relocate the few people who would lose their homes.
"But the residents and the council were difficult to work with," Mr. Martinez said. "They wanted veto power over everything. The process dragged on for too long." Eventually officials from HUD got fed up and pulled the plug. Residents at the Edgemere Houses are scheduled to get the money in 18 months. CHARLIE LeDUFF

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
Building a Nest Egg and a Community

BYLINE: By JANE H. LII 

 BODY:
TWO years ago, when Kike (pronounced KEE-kay) Hiciano Payano, a video and film producer in Washington Heights, decided to buy a new video camera, he first went to his bank for a $500 line of credit.
The bank, the Citibank branch at Broadway and 181st Street, denied his application, but told Mr. Hiciano that he could bolster his credit standing by buying a certificate of deposit in the same amount as collateral, Mr. Hiciano said. 
 "It made no sense," Mr. Hiciano said recently through a translator. "If I had that kind of money to put in the bank, what do I need to borrow from them for?"
Out of desperation, Mr. Hiciano did what many in Inwood and Washington Heights have done for years: he went to a "prestamista" -- a loanshark -- and took out a four-month loan at 4 percent weekly interest, which compounds to 341 percent annually. Two months later, Mr. Hiciano wanted to borrow another $3,000 to buy a computer, at 5 percent a week for three months. Even though he started paying back the loans immediately, Mr. Hiciano still had to pay interest on the full principal until the end. By the time the loans were paid off, Mr. Hiciano had shelled out $3,360 in interest alone during a seven-month period.
"I never wanted to go to a prestamista," said Mr. Hiciano. "But when the bank turns you down, how much can you borrow from your family and friends?"
But Mr. Hiciano and others in Inwood and Washington Heights now have an alternative to exorbitant interest rates and out-of-reach bank loans: the Neighborhood Trust Federal Credit Union, which opened on March 20 in the George Washington Bridge Bus Station building at 178th Street and Fort Washington Avenue.
The credit union, in a renovated office with wood paneling and industrial carpeting, is run by Mark Levine, 27, and Luis De Los Santos, 31, who formed Credit Where Credit Is Due, a nonprofit organization that operates the credit union. Their goal is to offer affordable banking services and personal loans to those who are overlooked by the banks in the area, said Mr. Levin, the executive director of the organization and the president of the credit union.
To realize their goal, Mr. Levine and Mr. De Los Santos quit their jobs as teachers in the New York City public school system to work on forming the credit union. In the interim, Mr. Levine also received a master's degree in public policy from the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University, writing his thesis on the conditions of financial services in Washington Heights.
"We just wanted to make a change," Mr. Levine said.
In subsequent research, Mr. Levin and Mr. De Los Santos found a staggering 73 percent of the 800 residents they surveyed said they depend on relatives, friends, loan sharks and pawn shops for loans.
For years, residents have complained that the banking industry ignores low-income neighborhoods like Inwood and Washington Heights. Sarah Ludwig, executive director of Neighborhood Economic Development Advocacy Project, a nonprofit organization, said that while there is one bank branch for every 5,000 people in other parts of Manhattan, the ratio is three times that in upper Manhattan, or one branch for every 15,000 people. Recently, some full-service banks have been reduced to automated teller services. Cultural barriers and other problems also keep many from the banks.
Instead of opening accounts, Mr. Levine said, people here prefer to stash their money under mattresses or in drawers, symptoms of their residual distrust of institutions and authorities in their home countries. This prevents people from building up a credit history, which can be helpful during the loan process. Some also stay away because of high fees, Mr. Levine said. To receive no-fee service at banks, a minimum balance might be set as high as $3,000. When a customer's balance drops below that, most banks charge a monthly fee of $10 to $30.
"This adds up to a lot of money," Mr. Levine said. "A lot of people can't afford it."
Yet others are scared off by rumors throughout the neighborhood.
"Some people who are receiving public assistance are under the wrong impression that their benefits would be cut off if they opened a bank account," Mr. Levine said.
At the credit union, the minimum for a no-fee account is $50. If the balance falls below that, there is a $1 monthly charge. Mr. Levine said members can borrow short-term personal loans of $500 to $5,000 at a rate of about a quarter point below market. Despite the Federal Reserve's decision to increase interest rates last week, Mr. Levine said the credit union will fix its short-term lending rate at 14 percent annually, a bargain compared to the more than 300 percent rate the prestamistas now charge.
In its first four days of business, more than 50 people, including Mr. Hiciano, have signed up. "This is really going to help me," said Janet Morales, who stopped by last Monday. Ms. Morales, a single mother of four who said she was looking to get off public assistance, wanted to borrow $500 to open a day-care center at home.
"I applied for a loan through the Citibank," Ms. Morales said. "They turned me down even with my sister as a co-signer."
But there are limitations to the credit union. So far, many have signed up with the intent to borrow. Although major banks like Citibank, Chase Manhattan Bank and Fuji Bank have pledged nearly $500,000 in low-interest deposits to the credit union, and the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone has given it a $46,000 grant and nearly $250,000 in low-interest loans, it must attract enough customers to keep going. "The success of a credit union depends on the number of depositors," Ms. Ludwig said.
Clifford Rosenthal, executive director of the National Federation of Community Development Credit Unions, a non-profit organization that provides support to the credit unions, said there are 25 such credit unions citywide, with deposits averaging about $2 million each.
To help residents to overcome their fear of the institution, Mr. Levine and Mr. De Los Santos are planning to visit local churches and community groups and to offer basic finance courses. Their goal is to sign up 1,000 members within the year and to get $500,000 in deposits.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Mortality Wears a Profitable, Noncyclical Edge

BYLINE: By SANA SIWOLOP 

 BODY:
SOME investors may find the subject ghoulish, but the fact is the funeral business is alive and flourishing. Although its stocks are not markedly low-priced, and its revenues may be threatened by the cheaper option of cremation, the "death care" industry is now enjoying increasing demand and a wave of consolidations, all while retaining its enviable ability to raise prices.
The aging of the baby boomers is one reason for the sector's sunny prospects. The number of deaths in the United States is rising by about eight-tenths of a percent a year, and is expected to do so until 2005 or so. But after that, and until 2040, deaths are expected to rise by 1.4 percent annually. That step-up will mean more demand for everything from funerals and coffins to bereavement support groups and remembrance services. For a company like Service Corporation International, the industry leader with more than 2,800 funeral homes, 330 cemeteries and 140 crematoriums worldwide, the potential growth in revenue is great. 
 And for investors who expect an economic slowdown, the companies may also hold appeal. "These stocks should be looked at as defensive stocks," said Susan R. Little, an analyst at Raymond James. "They're in a noncyclical industry, where demand for services is always there."
Moreover, funeral companies are working hard at cutting costs. For example, they are increasingly clustering their funeral homes within geographic areas so that the homes can share, say, a central embalming place or a fleet of cars. Ms. Little particularly likes Stewart Enterprises, an industry leader, because of its high potential savings from a clustering strategy.
Many kinds of businesses can lower costs, but few have this industry's ability to raise prices with so little consumer resistance. The reason is not only that people hate to scrimp, or appear to be scrimping, on the funerals of their loved ones, but also that people shop for funerals infrequently and have little sense of what prices should be.
Consequently, the average traditional funeral with a coffin and an in-ground vault cost more than $5,400 in 1995, the last year for which statistics were available. That is an increase of almost 22 percent since 1991.
And funeral companies that are adept at creating good will can reap the rewards for a long time, said Barry Fingerhut, the president of Geo Capital, a money manager.
"If you do a good job in this business, it can last 100 years, through subsequent generations," said Mr. Fingerhut, whose company holds $140 million in shares of several industry leaders. "I expect the good will will help these companies as they expand into other businesses, like the so-called pre-need business, where companies sell funerals to consumers before they're needed."
The stocks of many funeral companies are not bargains, at least by some measures. Service Corporation International is trading at 24.20 times its estimated earnings for 1997, for example, while Stewart Enterprises and Equity Corporation International, another leading company, are trading at multiples of 24.95 and 32.29, respectively. In light of the 20.31 multiple of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, these stocks seem highly priced.
By other measures, however, the stocks may not seem too expensive. Analysts compare stock multiples to the companies' expected annual rates of earnings growth, for instance. And they consider the stocks to be reasonably priced if the multiples are at or below the yearly growth projections.
Analysts predict very good growth for funeral-industry earnings -- about 25 percent a year, on average. At that quick pace, multiples that may seem excessive in slower-growing industries may be reasonable.
Joseph Chiarelli, an analyst at J. P. Morgan Securities, said the funeral companies that he tracks had multiples below their company-specific growth projections when he checked those numbers last week. As stock prices fluctuate, these companies' multiples may surpass 25, the average percentage growth projection, but the lesson for investors may be to check the multiple and to buy on price dips.
ONE reason for the industry's rosy growth projections is acquisitions. According to Mr. Chiarelli, the four largest funeral companies -- Service, Stewart Enterprises, Equity and the Loewen Group -- own 18 percent of the $10 billion domestic market, up from 14 percent four years ago. As those numbers suggest, there is room for many more mergers: The quartet of companies still owns fewer than 5,000 of the 24,000 funeral homes in the country.
"We think it's an industry that's very ripe for more consolidation," said Zack Shafran, a portfolio manager at Waddell & Reed, which manages $25 billion and owns shares in Equity and Stewart Enterprises.
Both those companies, Mr. Shafran said, have the tightly knit management that seems to work in the industry, where customers often prefer to deal with long-established companies they know. Stewart still has family members at its helm, while many of Equity's 163 homes are run by second- and third-generation families.
At J. P. Morgan, Mr. Chiarelli likes the stocks of Service, Equity and Stewart, in that order. Equity, he said, should benefit from its strong presence in those rural markets that have high death rates. And Stewart has more homes combined with adjacent cemeteries than its rivals, enabling it to profit from both businesses.
But Mr. Chiarelli's top pick is Service, particularly because of its promising acquisitions overseas, where the market potential is large and where the chance to improve efficiencies is great. In 1995, for instance, Service bought more than 1,000 funeral homes in Europe, mostly in France. In less than a year and a half, Mr. Chiarelli said, those acquisitions expanded their operating margins to 13 percent from 9 percent.
Ms. Little also likes Mr. Chiarelli's three favorites, but she likes Stewart the most. The company began using clustering and other strategies more recently than its rivals did, and thus may see more earnings improvement going forward. The company has also given regional managers more autonomy and has instituted a bonus program; both moves could help it increase its revenues.
Mr. Shafran dismisses the shares of Carriage Services, a smaller company, as too costly; it was trading at 31.61 times estimated earnings at the week's close. But some analysts think the small size of Carriage may give it a leg up when it seeks acquisitions. Owners of independent funeral homes, who usually become employees of acquiring companies, may feel they can play larger corporate roles within such a smaller enterprise.
But what about Loewen, the industry's second-largest company? Service Corporation International failed in an attempt to take over Loewen last fall, but the ordeal was costly for the target.
Partly as a result, "Loewen is a capital-constrained company, while this is clearly an acquisition-driven industry," Mr. Shafran said.
Nevertheless, with its stock trading at just 20.52 times earnings, Loewen is among the industry's cheaper buys. And given that analysts expect the company's earnings to grow 25 percent a year, Loewen stock looks attractively priced.
But the funeral industry, whatever its promise, also faces some troubling risks. Some analysts worry that as the acquisition race heats up, some companies will overpay. Mr. Chiarelli said some companies had already come close to doing so.
OTHER dangers involve the industry's fabled strong point: pricing power. Mr. Shafran, for instance, says discounters who charge half as much as regular funeral companies are now appearing in some domestic markets. An example, he said, is Charter Funerals in Kansas City, Mo.; it plans to be in a half-dozen or more areas over the next 12 to 18 months.
"We don't think they have broad-based appeal, but we've seen a number of markets where traditional funeral-home-and-cemetery arrangements have been bypassed in favor of low-end, cut-rate operations," he said.
Some discounters just want certain parts of the business. Direct Casket of Van Nuys, Calif., one of several "coffin only" companies in Southern Calfornia, says it sells coffins at 30 percent to 70 percent below funeral-home prices.
Traditional funeral companies must also deal with an increase in cremations. The cremation rate is now about 22 percent for all domestic deaths, but one estimate calls for it to double by 2010. Although many funeral companies run their own cremation businesses, the revenue risk is great because a simple cremation costs just $800.
Can the industry avoid this danger? "The challenge for the funeral industry will be to try to bridge the revenue gap between direct cremations and those that come with an accompanying service," Ms. Little said. "The consolidators within this industry have the marketing power to do that."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 4; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Jitters for the Port Authority's Bondholders?

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: ALBANY

 BODY:
WITH the recent appointment of his good friend and trusted lieutenant, Robert E. Boyle, as executive director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, Gov. George E. Pataki has revived talk about breaking up the sprawling agency that operates the road, rail and air gateways to the region.
For the many people who own a piece of the Port Authority's $5.5 billion in outstanding debt, that raises the inevitable question: How could such a major restructuring affect the value of their bonds? 
 History can provide little guidance, Port Authority officials and bond analysts said, because no multistate agency has disbanded in the many decades that Congress has allowed their creation.
But history aside, bondholders should relax, the experts said. Dissolving the agency would require far more political cooperation and willpower than currently exists, they said. And a major downsizing of the Port Authority through, say, the divestiture of its airports, would face huge financial and political obstacles.
Even if there were a huge restructuring of the agency, the political pressure from Wall Street, major institutional investors and elected officials to protect bondholders' interests would be overwhelming.
"My feeling is that there is no way they could split it up without bondholders being covered, because it would be so politically unpopular," said Andrea Bozzo, managing director of Fitch Investor Services. Fitch, a credit rating agency, has not changed the bonds' rating or put a watch on them since the recent developments, she added.
Nevertheless, some bondholders may view Mr. Pataki's recent statements with concern. He has echoed Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's complaints that the agency favors New Jersey, does a poor job of running John F. Kennedy International and La Guardia airports and ought to reduce its real estate holdings. Indeed, Mr. Pataki told Crain's New York Business recently, "In my mind, it is still an open question whether the Port Authority is salvageable."
Mr. Pataki has made the issue more real by asking Mr. Boyle to study how to make the Port Authority work more efficiently, and, if that is deemed impossible, to propose ways to restructure it.
Aides to both Mr. Pataki and Mr. Boyle said the new executive director's primary goal was to make the agency succeed, not kill it. "The Governor sent him in here with the mission to see what it would take and how to go about making this place work," said Mark Hatfield, director of communications for the Port Authority.
Making any changes would be highly complicated because the agency is the child of two states. Dissolving it would require the consent of both states' Governors and Legislatures. And though Mr. Pataki is raising the idea, there is little interest in Trenton.
The agency has sold assets, as it did last year with the Vista Hotel and is considering doing now with the World Trade Center. Because all Port Authority revenues go into a general fund, the sale of a few properties does not affect the agency's ability to repay bondholders, analysts said.
But what if the Port Authority were to reduce significantly its revenue stream by divesting itself of some big assets, like the airports? Many experts question whether there are even any takers for such properties, and if there were, whether the public would stand for privatizing something as important as a leading airport.
Nothing in the bonds' covenants prevents such sales, however. And bondholders may not be fully reassured by the statements of Port Authority officials that any sale proceeds must be used to redeem agency bonds or to invest in new projects whose revenues could be pledged to bondholders.
FOR instance, bondholders often get irked if their bonds are recalled early, said Jim Lynch, publisher of the Lynch Municipal Bond Advisory report. In such cases, investors lose a stream of income they had assumed would be there, and though they can buy another bond with the redemption proceeds, they may not find one as much to their liking as the one recalled. And even if bonds are not redeemed, bondholders who liked the idea of having their Port Authority investments backed up with revenues from, say, airports, might feel less secure if the revenues came from something else, like a real estate development.
All the talk about restructuring the Port Authority has not affected the sale of its bonds, judging by a recent $300 million offering in January. Mr. Lynch said those bonds had fluctuated with the market, but not because of Mr. Pataki's remarks.
That may change if Mr. Boyle urges Mr. Pataki to seek an agency overhaul, but for the moment many analysts are unperturbed. Mr. Lynch said major institutional investors still consider Port Authority bonds a good buy and do not pay much attention to the griping of politicians.
"They know if the state were to try an end run, selling certain assets that are key to providing revenues, the state would be in court in a flash," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Port Authority of New York and New Jersey has already sold some of its assets and is now considering whether to sell the World Trade Center in lower Manhattan. (FPG)

PUB-DATE: March 30, 1997

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 3092 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Why Both Bulls and Bears Can Act So Bird-Brained

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

 BODY:
MARKETS must be rational: most distinguished economists have bet their careers on that.
Still, your gut may be telling you something quite different -- that investors can be just plain irrational. 
 You are not alone.
To measure just how quirky investors can be, some insurgent economists are going beyond mathematical equations and computer models and are engaging in some unusual behavior of their own. They are joining psychologists in observing how laboratory humans perform in all sorts of experiments. They have watched normally rational citizens flip coins to test their inclination to gamble. They are consulting old studies of pigeons to learn how people can deceive themselves.
And they are asking: Why do investors do things that are so obviously foolish? Why do they see patterns where there are none? Why do they behave with such overconfidence when common sense says they should behave otherwise?
The answers, these economists believe, can be crucial to understanding the stock market at a time when the chairman of the Federal Reserve shakes the financial world with the frightening words "irrational exuberance."
The occasional irrational binges of investors, they argue, have helped explain why the traditional theory of market performance periodically misfires, although last Thursday's 140-point drop in the Dow Jones industrial average may be no more than a rational response to the Fed's increase in interest rates.
More practically, individual investors and money managers can benefit enormously by recognizing their irrational alter egos: the otherwise sensible investor who is prone to excessive, costly trading; who ignores history and is lured by the market action of the moment; who can't stand to take losses but ultimately is humbled into suffering yet bigger losses; whose worst fear is investor's remorse.
"If you know there are fairly consistent patterns of behavior bias and can go against those patterns, you can make money," said Susan Belden, the co-editor of the No-Load Fund Analyst, a money-management newsletter based near San Francisco.
Value investing, the investing style most dependent on behavioral quirks, has done this for a long time, capitalizing on the tendency of people to overreact to bad news and to react slowly to good news. This gives the investor a chance to profit by buying stocks sold off by the herd.
"Fundamentally, this approach tells us that human behavior matters in markets just like everywhere else," said Richard H. Thaler, a professor of economics at the University of Chicago and a leader in the field of behavioral economics.
N. Gregory Mankiw, a more traditional professor of economics at Harvard and the author of a new introductory economics textbook, agrees.
"Maybe to fully understand market behavior we have to introduce some form of irrationality," he acknowledged. The behaviorists have "opened my eyes to alternative ways of thinking."
Financial behaviorists, who are being heeded as never before, are building on years of research by psychologists and others with no economic axes to grind, adding their own experiments and new conclusions. But their interpretations of behavior and how it applies to financial markets are less precise than the equations and computer models of traditional economists.

Overconfidence
The traditional theory of markets holds that investors are rational and that markets are efficient, quickly reflecting new information and the decisions of millions of investors.
Based on this view, investors should hold stocks for the long term, unless new information dictates a change. After all, history tells us that investing for the long run is the surest route to profit, and that with information disseminated so quickly and then promptly reflected in stock prices, we cannot act fast enough to outperform the market.
Still, investors can exhibit persistent and potentially harmful symptoms of overconfidence, unshaken in their convictions that they can beat the market. Both professional and amateur investors continue to trade actively, suffering needless losses and costing themselves a bundle in commissions.
And professional money managers are thriving because of investors' enduring hopes that the stock pickers ultimately will excel.
This mentality has been tested in surveys of car drivers. Asked if they consider themselves above-average drivers, most people say yes, leaving wide open the question of who all the below-average drivers are.
"One of the hardest things to imagine is that you are not smarter than average," said Daniel Kahneman, a professor of psychology and public affairs at Princeton University.
Professor Thaler and Robert J. Shiller, an economics professor at Yale, note that individual investors and money managers persist in their beliefs that they are endowed with more and better information than others, and that they can profit by picking stocks.
Sobering experience can help those who delude themselves. But not always. That people "do not learn to correct most of their tendencies to overconfidence is apparently just one of the limitations of the human mind," Professor Shiller wrote in an article available on the World Wide Web.
Overconfident investors, behaviorists say, also affect the market as a whole. Consider the huge trading volume in stocks. Much of it, some economists believe, can be traced to the behavioral quirks of investors.
High volume is "the single most embarrassing fact for an efficient market," Professor Thaler said. "People are supposed to be buying and holding."
Terrance Odean, a behavioral economist who will join the faculty of the University of California at Davis in May, found a stunning pace of buying and selling in his study of trading at an unidentified discount brokerage firm from 1987 to 1993. Examining 10,000 accounts, Mr. Odean found that individual investors had an average annual turnover rate of 78 percent in the securities they owned.
By his calculations, the stocks that they sold outperformed the ones they bought by about three percentage points, before commissions, in the year after the sale. Such a missed opportunity is the price that investors pay for overconfidence about their own investment prowess.
Could overconfidence also foster the "irrational exuberance" over stocks that has worried the chairman of the Federal Reserve? Professor Kahneman thinks so. Investors may think they can continue doing well in the market despite warnings from many analysts that stocks are overvalued.
"Optimism has a lot to do with what is going on," he said. "Many more than 50 percent of the people in the market think they are better than average at picking stocks and picking trends, and that contributes to irrational exuberance."

The Fear of Losses
If stocks are likely to produce a much higher return than bonds over the long run, why do investors still buy bonds?
Because, the behaviorists say, the pain of a small loss is greater than the thrill of a big gain. In addition, it seems that investors become even more averse to losses through apparently irrelevant actions, like frequently checking the value of their portfolios.
As Peter L. Bernstein observed in "Against the Gods," his 1996 book about the history of risk-taking, "Losses will always loom larger than gains."
Professor Thaler argues that this fear of losses makes investors ignore the opportunity for gains provided by stocks. Staying out of the stock market during the last two years, he says, has probably meant more "losses," in missed opportunities, than the out-of-pocket losses from the 1987 crash.
Another behavior -- avoiding the pain of regret -- helps explain why investors hold on to losers for too long. By not selling, the investor avoids fully facing the fact that he made a bad decision.
Investors also tend to take much more risk to avoid losses than to secure gains. That behavior is demonstrated in an experiment once conducted by Professor Kahneman and the late Amos Tversky, two leading contributors to the psychology behind behavioral finance.
In the experiment, participants were told that there was a rare disease breaking out and that 600 people were expected to die. Two plans were proposed. Under Plan A, 200 people would survive. Under Plan B, there was a 33 percent chance that everyone would be saved and a 67 percent probability that no one would be saved. Seventy-two percent of the people chose the risk-averse Plan A, with the certainty of saving 200 lives.
Then the participants were offered another set of alternatives. Under Plan C, 400 people would die. Under Plan D, there was a 33 percent probability that no one would die and a 67 percent chance that everyone would die.
Plan A and C have the same outcome (200 people survive), as do Plans B and D (a 33 percent chance that all would survive but a 67 percent chance that no one would). But the way the second set of alternatives was phrased highlighted the loss of life -- the certainty that 400 people would die. And in that circumstance, 78 percent chose to gamble on the 33 percent chance that no one would die.
This discomfort with losses may help explain why some investors buy more shares of their losing stocks when prices fall further. They don't want to confront the loss, so they take added risk -- by buying more of the stock -- in an attempt to avoid the loss.

Magical Thinking
According to the behaviorists, investors can suffer from "magical thinking." They make connections between two occurrences when, in fact, there is no link.
In illustrating such self-deception, behaviorists mention an old study of pigeons. In the 1940's, B. F. Skinner, the psychologist, fed starved, caged pigeons small amounts of food at 15-second intervals, regardless of the birds' behavior. The birds began to behave as if the feeding was a response to their behavior at the time.
One pigeon that happened to have been doing turns at feeding time began its turns again at subsequent feeding times. Another pigeon that had been bobbing its head would repeat its behavior at the next feeding time, expecting more food.
"A lot of what goes on in the markets is no more explainable than those pigeons," Professor Shiller argued, highlighting the role of behavior in financial markets.
In the article on his Web home page, Professor Shiller suggested that magical thinking might explain why the stock market has tended to fall in recent years on positive news about economic growth.
The news media, he wrote, have frequently attributed that seemingly perverse reaction to expectations that stronger economic growth would force the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates, which would be bad news for stocks. In fact, Professor Shiller concluded, an entirely random series of dips in the stock market after positive economic news might have convinced the media and investors that there was a connection between the events when none existed.
"The whole belief could be the result of a chain of events that was set off by some initial chance movements of the stock market," Professor Shiller wrote. "Because people believe these theories, they may then behave so that the stock price does indeed behave as hypothesized."
 
Mental Accounting
How can two individuals make entirely different decisions when their circumstances are essentially the same? Chalk it up to something called mental accounting, which allows individuals to treat similar results or similar options differently, because of different starting points.
In "Against the Gods," Mr. Bernstein cites an experiment in which Professors Kahneman and Tversky asked the participants to imagine they were theatergoers headed to a Broadway show.
One group has $40 tickets. Upon arriving at the box office, however, they discover that they have lost their tickets. Distressed, most of them decide not to spend another $40 for a replacement ticket, and go home.
Another group arrives at the box office, intending to buy tickets. But they discover that $40 is missing from each of their wallets. Like the first theatergoers, they are each $40 poorer. But most of them still buy tickets and attend the show. Although both groups arrive at the theater short the same amount of money, they haves mentally accounted for it differently.
Mental accounting is a way for people to organize their experiences, Professor Kahneman says. But he adds that "it certainly can be used for self-deception." That means investors do not always respond to events in the expected rational way.
An experiment with coin tosses conducted by Professor Thaler shows how mental accounting can influence an investor's willingness to gamble.
In one case, each person in a group of students was given $30 and told he could walk away with the money. Or, he could take his chances on a coin toss, winning $9 more if the coin came up heads but losing $9 if it turned up tails. Seventy percent took the gamble, knowing that they would end up with at least $21.
Students in another group were offered a seemingly different gamble. They could flip a coin; heads would equal $39 and tails would equal $21. Or, they could just take $30 with no coin toss. Only 43 percent chose the gamble.
A little arithmetic shows that the two outcomes are identical, but the students made different choices. Why? Because, as behaviorists note, it is not just the information that determines behavior, but also how it is processed. That is mental accounting.
Professor Thaler says mental accounting may help explain why the 1987 market crash did not have the negative impact on the economy that many had forecast. Investors, he said, might not have seen it as a troubling actual loss of wealth, but instead viewed it less alarmingly, as the disappearance of a new-found gain. And so, no panic.
The economics establishment has come a long way in understanding behavioral about-faces and unexpected responses. Twenty years ago, when he was a student at Princeton, Professor Mankiw could not have taken an economics course in irrational behavior. None existed. Now it has become respectable for professors at Harvard, Yale and the University of Chicago to pick holes in the traditional view that investors are rational and markets are efficient.
The National Bureau of Economic Research and the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard will hold separate symposiums in April on behavior and its influence on financial markets; the first meeting is for academics, the second for money managers. Last fall, U.S. Trust, a money management firm for wealthy individuals, asked two of the behavioral thinkers, Professors Kahneman and Thaler, to brief its money managers.
And there are even grudging but respectful nods to behaviorists from the high priests of rational market behavior. In his 1996 edition of "A Random Walk Down Wall Street," Burton Malkiel, a Princeton economics professor, noted the words of Yale's Professor Shiller, who has counseled that "one must look to behavioral considerations and to crowd psychology to explain the actual process of price determination in the stock market."
Professor Shiller's encounter some months back with Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, has given behaviorists yet another little lift. Professor Shiller and John Campbell, a professor of economics at Harvard, were invited to join three top Wall Street equity strategists for a meeting with Mr. Greenspan last December. Two days after that session, the Fed chairman, in a speech, posed his now-famous question of whether "irrational exuberance" had pushed the stock market too high.
Professor Shiller did not provide Mr. Greenspan with his explosive phrase; the chairman had already inserted it into earlier drafts of his speech. But that didn't mean they weren't on the same wavelength. "I am pretty sure that I used the word 'irrational,' " Mr. Shiller said, recalling the December meeting. "But not 'exuberance.' "
Later, in Congressional testimony in February, Mr. Greenspan pinpointed who might be irrational. "It is not markets that are irrational -- markets merely reflect the average, the values of people," he said. "It's people who become irrationally exuberant on occasion and take actions that induce what economists like to call bubbles, which eventually burst."
Despite his choice of language, Mr. Greenspan apparently remains a believer in the essential efficiency and rationality of markets. And why not? Mainstream economic theory has done a workmanlike job in describing how markets function.
And though the traditional economists cannot explain certain anomalies -- the curious detours from the norm that are so intriguing -- the behavioral crowd has yet to come up with an overarching theory to explain how markets function.
Behaviorists "have pointed out the defects in the existing model but have not come up with a better model," Professor Mankiw said.
Nor does the sniping of behaviorists mean that markets over all, and over the long run, are irrational. "To say that investors are affected by behavioral motivation does not necessarily mean that markets are not efficient," said Mr. Odean, once an undergraduate student of Professor Kahneman.
But that does not deny the application to individual investors, who, if they are honest with themselves, know that they do not always behave rationally.
Dr. Michael Apfel, 47, an oral surgeon from Phoenix, acknowledges that he could be viewed as a case study. He freely confesses that behavioral quirks have undermined his investment performance.
A look at his portfolio suggests that he is the quintessential conservative investor. He dutifully balanced his portfolio with stocks, bonds, mutual funds, savings bonds and real estate partnerships. And yet, he said, "I used to buy and sell quite regularly." The cost of trading alone had cut his gains by 25 percent.
He had trouble getting rid of losing stocks, too. "One doesn't want to admit defeat," he said.
Now he trades only with the idea of making changes in his long-term investment posture. And he jettisons losers as part of a tax strategy to offset capital gains.
"You have to be there all the time to do well," he said of active trading. "Or you have to be a long-term investor. Anywhere in between and you are a loser."
What he has learned is that investors are better off if they know how behavior can trip them up. But millions of other investors have yet to share Mr. Apfel's epiphany. Perhaps if they were fully rational, they would have by now.
