HEADLINE: Councilmen Positioning for Bronx Primary

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS 

 BODY:
For two weeks now, City Councilman Jose Rivera has courted the clergy, met with labor leaders and implored business owners to make campaign contributions.
His feverish pitch is not about votes. At least not yet. He is trying to persuade his fellow Bronx politicians to view him as the Democratic machine's consensus candidate for Bronx borough president. 
 There were widespread predictions that the race for borough president would lead to a divisive free-for-all, with candidates from every ethnic sector of the Bronx entering the fray for the Democratic nomination.
But no such competition has emerged. Instead, Mr. Rivera seems positioned to be the leading candidate to be backed by the Democratic organization to face City Councilman Israel Ruiz Jr. in the party's primary.
Until two weeks ago, Assemblyman Roberto Ramirez, the Bronx County Democratic leader, was considered the candidate most likely to face Mr. Ruiz. But the Democratic chairman said he did not want the borough president's post, which is being vacated by Fernando Ferrer, who is running for mayor. Instead, Mr. Ramirez said, he would oversee the process under which anyone interested in getting the party's support would spend a month seeking help from various politicians, labor leaders and contributors.
"As soon as Roberto decided not to go for the borough president position, I immediately informed him that I would be reaching out to people," Mr. Rivera said. "So far, I think things are going pretty well."
At first, several black officials said that in an election year in which a Puerto Rican candidate was running for mayor, they wanted to see a black candidate run for borough president. Some Jewish leaders, too, said that they were too important a constituency to be overlooked. But so far, Mr. Rivera has mounted the most aggressive search for support, winning commitments from a variety of political leaders.
As a result, the race for the nomination in the heavily Democratic Bronx seems to be forming as a contest between Mr. Rivera and Mr. Ruiz. And the contrasts between the two candidates are sharp indeed.
Mr. Ruiz is a political phoenix who forfeited his State Senate seat as a result of his 1989 conviction for fraud. He spent four months in a Federal prison in 1990 and re-entered politics by winning a Council seat in 1991. A Columbia Law School graduate, Mr. Ruiz, 56, is known in the Council as a bright if unpredictable legislator with an accomplished speaking style. Although he is not particularly close to the county Democratic organization, he has already raised nearly $500,000 -- much of it in loans.
Indeed, Mr. Ruiz's loans represent an unconventional method of campaign finance. The New York Post reported yesterday that he received them from friends who own two construction companies: $300,000 from the Kafci Corporation in the South Bronx and $150,000 from the GMA Industrial Corporation of Scarsdale, N.Y.
Mr. Ruiz said he had put up his home as collateral for the loans, as well as his future earnings and pension. While acknowledging that the arrangement was unusual, Mr. Ruiz said he expected to repay the loans by Sept. 10, the date of the Democratic primary, as required under the city's campaign finance laws.
"I have several fund-raisers planned," Mr. Ruiz said in an interview yesterday. "They will be at the Waldorf and the Water Club."
He said it was important to take the loans because of his expectation that the Bronx Democratic organization would most likely endorse another candidate. "If the organization comes up with a half-million dollars in support of another candidate, I have to have the ability to get my message across," he said.
Mr. Rivera, who is 60, has been in the City Council since 1987, after serving in the State Assembly (he was elected to the Assembly in 1982 while receiving unemployment insurance). He began his career as a street activist and a union organizer. He is chairman of the Council's Black and Latino Caucus. And in recent years, he has become a particularly influential ally and tactician for Mr. Ramirez and is considered the political godfather of a host of the borough's Puerto Rican officials.
Mr. Ramirez has said publicly that he intends to sponsor an open process under which all candidates, including Mr. Ruiz, will be consulted, and said the party would make a decision by the middle or the end of April.
But it is no secret that the Bronx Democratic leader has no intention of supporting Mr. Ruiz. Several Bronx politicians suggest that they have some reservations about Mr. Rivera. A graying man with a somewhat rumpled look who often dresses casually, Mr. Rivera is criticized by some Bronx Democratic leaders as not fitting their image of what a borough president should be.
"Izzy Ruiz is a well-educated, experienced speaker and Jose is a man whose education was in the streets," said a Democratic Assemblyman, who asked that his name not be used. "It's a little bit like the city slicker versus the country bumpkin."
Also, Mr. Rivera is not well known to many of the politicians outside the Hispanic sections of the Bronx.
"He's got to be a little more out front with his campaign style and with his speaking style," said Jeffrey Klein, a Democratic Assemblyman from the Bronx. "But he does have passion on issues."
Mr. Rivera said he was unconcerned with such views.
"My background is one of being an activist and I continue to be like an activist," he said. "I have demonstrated that I have brought in a lot of people into the political process who would have been turned off by politicians."
So far, Mr. Rivera's efforts seem to be paying off. He said he has received the support of State Senator Larry Seabrook, one of the Bronx's influential black officials.
"Some might think Jose is a hard sell, but I don't believe that," Mr. Seabrook said. "He has a track record of working with African-Americans and on struggles that we identify with. He has stood with Jesse Jackson and David Dinkins. For me, it's an easy sell."
In the meantime, aides to Mr. Rivera said, the Councilman has started to wear suits more frequently.

HEADLINE: Police in Baltimore Try New Kind of Recruiting

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, March 27

 BODY:
Almost 30 years after the idea first occurred to him when he was an aide to Senator Robert F. Kennedy of New York, Adam Walinsky is finally watching the Police Corps come alive. He and others say it could fundamentally change the way police departments all over the country serve their communities.
As part of the 1994 Federal anticrime law, $10 million was appropriated for six states to develop programs modeled after the military's Reserve Officers Training Corps. The programs will reimburse as much as $30,000 in educational costs to college graduates who agree to serve four years on a participating police force. 
 Because Lieut. Gov. Kathleen Kennedy Townsend, Mr. Kennedy's oldest daughter, strongly supported the program, Maryland got the biggest amount, $6.5 million. All of that went to the Baltimore police. The first of three classes of 40 recruits is scheduled to begin training next month.
Proponents of the Police Corps say it will transform policing, not just by attracting college graduates but by having those older, better-educated recruits trained in a different way. They will have a new curriculum focusing on the community and emphasizing leadership, sensitivity and social skills as tools to break down the friction and distrust that officers often encounter.
Police academies typically teach skills and aspects of the law in a more sterile, textbook fashion.
"What we're trying to do is enrich and give fuller content to training for community policing," said Mr. Walinsky, a lawyer from New York who played a pivotal role in developing the curriculum, with suggestions from dozens of police officers, in the rank and file as well as the command level. "Yes, we are deliberately reaching for a higher level of recruit, but our goal is that whether they remain on the force or leave it, they will be committed to solving the difficult social problems that are affecting so many people in our country."
To deal with those problems, recruits, in addition to learning new self-defense techniques, will be taught the demographics of neighborhoods, along with communication and note-taking skills. Role playing will also be a major part of training.
"We'll have a lot of recruits from the white suburbs who have never been in a tough urban environment," said Ronald L. Daniel, chief of field operations for the Baltimore Police Department. "Their fear factor could be very high. By playing roles, you begin to understand why people are different and the pressures that people from certain economic groups are under."
Convinced by the urban riots in the late 1960's that policing needed to change, Mr. Walinsky began actively promoting the concept of the Police Corps in 1982.
He found an eager ally in Mrs. Townsend, a lawyer who was a policy analyst at the time for Gov. Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts and campaign manager for her uncle, Senator Edward M. Kennedy. Viewing it as a logical extension of the Peace Corps, which began when another uncle, John F. Kennedy, occupied the White House, Mrs. Townsend tried to sell the idea in Massachusetts but got little support from state law-enforcement officials.
Mrs. Townsend left Massachusetts for Maryland in 1984 and served in several state and Federal positions before her election as Lieutenant Governor in 1994. But she and Mr. Walinsky kept campaigning for a Police Corps, buttonholing members of Congress and other elected officials whenever and wherever possible.
The concept finally blossomed with the $10 million pilot program. In addition to Maryland, recipients of the money will be South Carolina, which is receiving an estimated $1.08 million; Oregon, $910,000; North Carolina, $450,000; Arkansas, $225,000, and Nevada, $222,500. Eleven other states are competing for money from the $20 million appropriation in the 1997 budget, and President Clinton has asked for $20 million in the 1998 budget.
Maryland gave all its share to Baltimore because local officials, senior police officers and the police union leadership all expressed eagerness for it to become the first major city to adopt the Police Corps approach.
"I had been thinking about this years ago in San Jose," said Thomas C. Frazier, Baltimore's Police Commissioner since 1994, who had served as deputy chief of operations in San Jose, Calif. "Out there, we had lots of union opposition. But the idea always stuck in my mind. Adam Walinsky knew of my interest in California, and when he asked me if I was still interested, I said I was more interested in it here than there."
In Baltimore, police union leaders were as supportive as city political officials, which made it easier for Mrs. Townsend to contend that Baltimore should become the steward of the state's pilot program.
Mr. Walinsky and other consultants were called in, and they began meeting with police officers at every level for suggestions. Many officers were particularly impressed by that.
"We got to be part of the planning process," Mr. Daniel said. "We all had something to say, and they listened. That was lacking in the current training."
Mr. Frazier said the Police Corps program would gradually replace the curriculum used in Baltimore's policy academy, which produces about 200 new officers each year.
All new officers earn an annual starting salary of $25,400.
One uncertainty about the new program is how well Police Corps graduates will work with new colleagues from the academy. Mr. Frazier said he did not expect a two-class system to emerge.
But Mr. Walinsky said the experiment was worth watching. "We're going to learn as we go along," he said. "Look, we're going to make mistakes. They'll be setbacks. Did we pick the right people? Is the training as good as we think it is? That's why we're only starting with 40 and not training them all at once."
State officials say they have already received 500 requests for applications.
One came from Darren Zeck, a 1996 graduate of Ohio Wesleyan University and a psychology and accounting major who had wanted to become an agent of the Federal Bureau of Investigation. Mr. Zeck said he had learned about the Police Corps last year while visiting a friend in Owings Mills, Md., a Baltimore suburb. He said that after the F.B.I. had told him the agency preferred candidates with several years of law-enforcement experience, he had decided that the Baltimore program might be the ideal conduit.
Mr. Zeck, 23, is scheduled to take written and physical tests next week, and, if he passes, return for psychological tests.
"I'm not getting a lot of support right now from my parents," he said. "They're a little scared about the police stuff. And my girlfriend is a little upset because I'd move to Baltimore. But I'm real interested in the community service aspect of the program, the one-on-one intersection with the community. But whether I stay after four years or not, I'm really looking forward to getting started."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Lieut. Gov. Kathleen Kennedy Townsend of Maryland and Baltimore's Police Commissioner, Thomas C. Frazier, support the Police Corps. (Marty Katz for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Maryland Lawmakers Reject Increase in Tax on Cigarettes

BYLINE: By DAVID STOUT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The tobacco industry, which has endured some setbacks of late, won a victory this week in Maryland that had more to do with local politics than with national health issues.
Gov. Parris N. Glendening of Maryland had wanted to double the cigarette tax of 36 cents a pack, which would have given Maryland the highest cigarette tax among tobacco-growing states and the fourth-highest in the country, behind Washington (82.5 cents a pack), Massachusetts (76) and Michigan (75). 
 As debate intensified in the state capital of Annapolis this week, there seemed to be no support in the Senate for an increase of more than 15 cents. In the House, some lawmakers from tobacco-growing districts proposed increases so high that they had no chance of passing -- which was just the point.
The Senate overwhelmingly defeated a 30-cents-a-pack increase, and debate finally stalled in the House after increases of 64 cents to nearly $5 a pack were proposed.
By Friday night, proposed increases in the tobacco tax were back in the Senate and House committees, where they were theoretically still under consideration. But no one expects them to surface before the legislative session ends on April 7.
The Governor was keenly disappointed. "Nothing is ever dead until the final banging of the gavel on the last day," he said. "However, the odds are obviously against it."
For Mr. Glendening, a first-term Democrat, doubling the tobacco tax was both a personal health issue -- he has said that both of his parents were smokers and that he does not want his son to smoke -- and an economic one. He had made the increase a cornerstone of an economic development plan. Doubling the cigarette tax would generate $285 million over three years and offset a planned cut in state income taxes, he contended in an interview in November.
Independent polls had suggested that a majority of Maryland residents supported a big increase in the tobacco tax. But Delegate George W. Owings 3d of Calvert County pointed out tonight that tobacco was still the largest cash crop in the state, one that many farmers and their families depend on.
"I represent an agrarian area of southern Maryland that has 1,200 farmers," Mr. Owings said, in explaining why he opposed the Governor, a fellow Democrat, on the tobacco tax.

HEADLINE: RISING PHONE BILLS ARE LIKELY RESULT OF DEREGULATION

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

 BODY:
Thirteen months after the telephone industry was deregulated in the name of free-market benefits for consumers, the public might soon see the first tangible result of competition: higher phone bills.
The Federal Communications Commission is currently weighing proposals that could increase monthly local phone bills anywhere from $1.50 to $6 a month. And even if the Federal Government stops short of imposing new charges, industry executives and economists said that state regulators might eventually have little choice but to let local telephone companies lift their rates. 
 Fatter phone bills are not what Congress had in mind when it passed the sweeping telecommunications bill early last year.
But many experts say that higher rates may be an unavoidable first step toward the competition that is supposed to bring lower prices in the long run.
Why this seeming paradox? In setting the rules for a fully competitive communications market, Federal and state regulators must strip away subsidies that for decades have allowed millions of Americans to get local service for less than it costs the telephone companies to provide it.
That leaves the F.C.C., in particular, with a dilemma. It can simply end these subsidies for the local phone companies and risk a jarring disruption of the "universal service" tradition that enables 93 percent of American households to afford a telephone. Or the F.C.C. can allow the phone companies to ease into the world of pay-as-you-go competition, by replacing a portion of the subsidies with higher rates.
"This is the Sophie's Choice of the telecommunications revolution," said Roy M. Neel, the president of the United States Telephone Association, which represents more than 1,000 local phone companies. "Something has got to give if you want to establish competition."
With the F.C.C. set to rule on these issues on May 8, the prospect of higher rates is already whipping up a political tempest on Capitol Hill. At a recent hearing of the Senate Commerce Committee, several senators said they would never have voted in favor of the Telecommunications Act of 1996 if they had thought it would lead to higher rates.
One of the senators, Sam Brownback, a Kansas Republican, said in a recent interview that he was particularly worried about rural customers. Rates for those customers are subsidized more heavily than elsewhere because it costs more for the phone companies to string lines out to them. Without an adequate replacement for the subsidies, those rates could skyrocket.
"We would be foolish to build up all these expectations about competition without saying to the American people, 'We're going to have to raise your phone bill,' " Mr. Brownback said.
Further complicating the arithmetic is the Clinton Administration's campaign to wire the nation's schools and libraries with sophisticated cable for high-speed access to the Internet. Under the White House plan, the phone companies would be responsible for wiring -- at an estimated cost of $2.25 billion -- with help from a universal service fund collected by the Government.
The question facing the F.C.C.: How does it preserve affordable local phone service and also wire schools and libraries, while stripping away billions of dollars in subsidies?
"The nub of the problem is dealing with the transitional period," said Reed E. Hundt, the chairman of the F.C.C. "My No. 1 goal is to make sure the cost of residential dial tone does not go up."
Mr. Neel of the telephone association and other experts acknowledge that it would be politically untenable for the F.C.C. to put through a direct increase on basic phone rates.
Mr. Hundt said the commission might instead try to increase monthly flat rates for people who install second phone lines in their homes -- even at the risk of curbing Internet and other on-line computer access, which is what many second lines are used for these days. The F.C.C. might also raise the line charges on phone lines used by small businesses or for lines used in vacation homes.
Under the regulatory system in place for decades, Americans have been assured of affordable access to basic phone service, through the policy known as universal service. As a result, while the average monthly bill for residential long-distance service was around $31 in 1995, the average household paid only about $19 a month for local phone service.
But those low local rates are made possible only by a complex web of Federal and state subsidies, which are coming under attack as the Telecommunications Act opens up the telephone market to competition.
The first subsidy to go under the knife will be access charges, the per-minute fees that long-distance carriers like the AT&T Corporation pay to Nynex and other local phone companies to complete their calls. These charges are purposely set above cost, and they generate roughly $14 billion a year, which the local companies use to subsidize local service.
The MCI Communications Corporation says the F.C.C. should cut access charges to $3 billion, which it contends is the local companies' actual cost of providing access. The company is currently running a multimillion-dollar print and television advertising campaign to persuade consumers that they are victims of rampant overcharging by their local companies.
The BellSouth Corporation, however, said that if access charges were cut that much, it would no longer be able to pay for improvements in its phone network. The company, which offers local service in nine Southern states, said these charges ought to be allowed to decline on their own as new competitors lured customers away from the Bell companies.
In a filing last year with the F.C.C., the GTE Corporation estimated that it would have to raise local rates by $7.20 a month to make up the money lost from access charges and other subsidies.
Despite such potential increases, some analysts said consumers would benefit from reduced access charges because the long-distance carriers would pass along the money they saved in lower long-distance bills. But consumer advocates noted that access charges have already declined 15 percent since 1990, while long-distance rates have risen 15 percent. Moreover, they noted that access charges are only one of the subsidies that keep local rates down.
Another way that local phone companies are able to keep rates down is through the monthly "subscriber line charge," an additional flat fee, long mandated by the F.C.C. and currently $3.50 for residential lines and $6 for businesses, which local phone companies levy to help defray the overall costs of operating the local network. This fee, which is waived for low-income subscribers who qualify for bare-bones "lifeline" service, amounts to a subsidy underwritten by the more affluent.
Other subsidies include the inflated prices for extra calling features, like call waiting, caller identification and voice mail, which regulators have let phone companies charge as a way of underwriting basic telephone service.
All told, Federal and state subsidies generate about $23 billion a year for the local phone companies. Under the terms of the Telecommunications Act, these subsidies are to be replaced with a new universal service fund, to be collected from any company -- old or new -- offering local phone service. This fund would be administered by the Federal Government to insure affordable phone service for all.
How best to make this transition is the subject of intense debate between local and long-distance companies and the regulators. If access charges are cut too abruptly, or if the universal service fund is not large enough, the local companies said, they will have to raise their local rates in order to continue offering ubiquitous and reliable service.
These companies have not hesitated to battle the F.C.C. in court on these kinds of issues. GTE and several of the Bell companies are suing the commission in Federal court in St. Louis over the F.C.C.'s rules for opening their networks to new competitors.
Still, few experts expect the local phone companies to be compensated dollar-for-dollar for the subsidies they lose. Wall Street analysts said the stock prices of the seven Bell companies and GTE already reflected an expectation that they would give up $1 billion to $2 billion in revenue.
Executives at BellSouth said the Federal universal service fund would have to be between $9 billion and $10 billion, including the cost of wiring schools, in order for local companies to keep a lid on rates. In his recent speeches, however, Mr. Hundt has hinted that he would prefer to keep the Federal fund at between $5 billion and $7 billion.
Unless it wants local phone companies to absorb the difference, the F.C.C. must find several billion dollars. Among the proposals being considered is a plan to increase the monthly $3.50 subscriber line charge.
In separate filings with the F.C.C., AT&T recommended doubling the charge to $7 a month, while BellSouth suggested increasing it to $5.43 a month. The F.C.C., however, seems more likely to increase charges on second lines or on lines in second homes.
"This game is called 'shift and shaft,' " Sharon L. Nelson, the chairwoman of the Washington Utilities and Transportation Commission, said. "You shift the costs to the states and shaft the consumer."

GRAPHIC: Chart: "The Price of Competition"
In the transition from a regulated telephone industry to free-market competition, the F.C.C. may need to raise local phone rates to eliminate long-standing subsidies. Here are the various parts of a sample monthly phone bill that could be affected.
 
Subscriber Line Charge*
The F.C.C. is weighing proposals to increase this $3.50 charge by $1.50 to $3.75 per month, most likely on second phone lines and on lines in second homes. It may also raise the $6.00 monthly charge to businesses for primary phone lines.
 
Usage fees*
If the F.C.C. does not raise rates at the Federal level, state regulators may have no choice but to let local phone companies raise per-minute or per-call rates.
 
Long-distance access
The F.C.C. may impose a flat rate on long-distance carriers for access to networks of local companies. The long-distance carriers will probably pass some of the cost along to customers.

HEADLINE: Use Democratic India as Counterweight to China

 BODY:

To the Editor:
A. M. Rosenthal's columns on India (March 21 and 25) are heartening. India is the world's biggest democracy, and we have a common bond, for we were both British colonies. We also imported India's method of peaceful dissent, which fueled our civil rights movement in the 1960's. And the tremendous number of immigrants from India contribute so much to this country. 
 We would be wise to cultivate this bond for several reasons. Economically, India is an enormous unclaimed Asian market timed to emerge just as China's market matures, much in the way China's market emerged as Japan's was maturing. Culturally, standing by a solid agrarian democracy as it grows on the world scene would go a long way toward advancing American ideas globally. Politically, a confident, democratic India allied with the United States can provide leverage against a tyrannical China.
Many fear this century will be America's last great hurrah as a superpower, and that the next century will belong to the Chinese. But if we stick to our tradition of being the advocate of democracy worldwide, our way of life will continue to reign supreme. Leaning toward India is the next logical step down that path.
 
DAVID FRIEDMAN
New York, March 25, 1997

HEADLINE: U.S. SURVEYS FIND FARM WORKER PAY DOWN FOR 20 YEARS

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

DATELINE: SALINAS, Calif.

 BODY:
Despite decades of efforts to improve the lot of American farm workers, wages for the nation's more than two million farm laborers have trailed stubbornly behind inflation for the past 20 years, making it hard for many of them to afford adequate housing and other necessities.
Agricultural economists and some industry surveys found that farm workers' wages have fallen 20 percent or more over the past two decades after accounting for inflation, while a United States Department of Agriculture study found a 7 percent drop, to $6.17 an hour in current dollars, over that period. 
 Here in Salinas, a lush farm center on California's Central Coast, the piece rate for picking broccoli remains 20 percent below levels of a decade ago, even before considering inflation. In nearby Watsonville, the average wage for strawberry workers has dropped from $6.55 an hour in 1985 to $6.25 today -- and that is without taking inflation into account.
"In terms of constant dollars, there's been a significant decline in wages, we think by over 25 percent, over the last 20 years," said Don Villarejo, executive director of the California Institute for Rural Studies. "You have a situation where there is a substantial surplus of workers, almost all of them immigrants, and labor unions have lost their importance in terms of helping to maintain wage levels for farm workers."
Making the economic squeeze worse, many farm laborers work fewer hours a year mainly because farmers can choose from a large pool of immigrant workers, most of them Mexican, to get their harvesting done more quickly. As a result, many farm workers -- whose jobs often involve bending over for hours at a time under a searing sun -- complain that their earnings have slid to $8,000 a year from $9,000 a few years ago.
Despite all the surveys, many farmers insist that wages are keeping up with inflation. They also assert that living conditions for farm laborers have improved, noting that a far lower percentage are migrants who follow the harvest from state to state each year.
To be sure, the United States has made some sizable strides in lifting living conditions for farm workers since the 1960 CBS documentary "Harvest of Shame" trained a spotlight on the deplorable conditions that migrant workers endured.
Nowadays most farm workers are offered unemployment insurance, Social Security and workers' compensation. Safety laws have been tightened, and farmers generally provide toilets and drinking water to their workers, as required by law.
But farm workers say that in some important ways their lives have deteriorated, often in ways related to the slide in after-inflation wages.
Effraim Lara, an earnest, muscular vegetable worker, says that because rents are so high and wages so modest, he, his wife and three daughters have to share a four-bedroom house in Salinas with two other farm worker families.
"In 1986, we were paid $3.70 for each box of broccoli rape we picked, but now we're paid $2.50 a box," Mr. Lara said in the Spanish of his native Guadalajara. "In the 1980's, I often earned $450 or $500 in a good week; now it's more like $300. I can assure you housing prices haven't gone down. What used to rent for $300, 10 years ago, now rents for $600."
His employer has stopped providing dental coverage, and his health insurance plan has grown skimpier -- now he has to pay 40 percent of each medical bill, up from 20 percent a decade ago.
In the 1960's, farmers usually provided free, though sometimes substandard, housing to their workers. But Federal laws that were enacted a decade ago to set standards for those farmers who provide housing have instead caused many growers to stop providing lodging, concluding it is too difficult and costly to meet the regulations. As a result, farm workers must pay for their own shelter, a cost that often claims at least one-third of their paychecks. That change has also forced many farm workers to find their own transportation to work, since they no longer live on the farm. Frequently they pay $15 to $25 a week for rides to work.
"Just by moving off the farm their disposable income has been cut enormously," said Philip Martin, an agricultural economist at the University of California at Davis.
Seizing on the surveys that show the smallest decline in farm wages, agricultural growers maintain that the wages of farm workers have done better, at least in the 1990's, than American workers not employed on the farm.
"Real wages in agriculture have declined slightly over the last 10 years, but they have declined less than the real wages of nonagricultural workers," said James Holt, an economist who serves as a consultant to the National Council of Agricultural Employers.
For all nonsupervisory American workers, real wages have slid about 3 percent since 1989, while some surveys show that farm wages have slid 2 percent since 1989 and others show a 10 percent drop. Farm wage studies often reach different conclusions because some are based on surveying farmers, some on farm workers, and some on census data.
To farm workers, it is scant comfort to hear that their after-inflation wages have slightly outpaced nonfarm earnings in recent years since agricultural wages have traditionally been just half that of nonfarm wages. What is more, only a small fraction of farm workers have benefits like health insurance and pension programs, which are common among nonfarm workers. Farm workers also complain that in many states they are not paid time-and-a-half for working more than eight hours a day.
According to the Department of Agriculture, wages for field workers rose from $2.57 in 1977 to $6.17 last year in current dollars. After adjusting for inflation, that survey found a 7 percent drop.
Many economists fault the Department of Agriculture's survey, saying that because it is based on questioning farmers, it overstates how much workers are paid and understates how much wages have fallen.
Some economists also fault that survey for not factoring in the many field hands who work for farm labor contractors, a fast-growing group that hires and supervises workers for farmers. Contractors, who employ about one-third of California's 700,000 farm workers, normally pay about 20 percent less than farmers who hire their workers directly.
An important factor behind the fall in wages has been the sharp decline in the power of the United Farm Workers of America. Its membership plunged from 80,000 in 1970 to 21,000 in 1993, the year its founder, Cesar Chavez, died.
Two decades ago it had contracts for all California table grape workers. Today, it has none, the result of growers getting tougher toward the union.
Mr. Lara, the Salinas vegetable worker, said that when the union had a contract with his farm 15 years ago, the farm paid $7.05 an hour at a minimum. Today, the workers have no contract, and the farm has a $6 an hour minimum.
"As long as there is no threat of unionization, growers have no reason to raise wages," said Arturo Rodriguez, president of the United Farm Workers. "Unless we have a real presence to force the industry to do what it should do there will be no change."
If California farm wages had kept up with inflation since 1985, he said, workers would be earning $9.40 an hour, instead of $5.50 or $6. To raise wages, the union is mounting one of the nation's largest organizing drives, an effort to unionize 20,000 strawberry workers.
Edward Angstadt, president of the Grower-Shipper Vegetable Association of Central California, said it would not be so simple for farmers to raise wages. "The past couple of years we haven't been in the best position from a sales standpoint," he said. "There are some people who just can't afford to bring up wages."
To farm workers and many economists, it is a truism that another major reason for falling wages is what they see as a huge surplus of immigrant labor. Sometimes immigrants show up at a field and begin working without even being hired, hoping the crew leaders will pay them at the end of the day.
Leo Polopolus, an agricultural economist at the University of Florida in Gainesville, said that 1986 legislation has not slowed the inflow of immigrants, as it intended.
"This continuous flow of legal and illegal immigrants into the work force has kept farm wages from rising faster than the cost of living," he said. He notes that the piece rate for picking oranges has remained flat in much of Florida since 1990.
Showing that farm workers and growers often have starkly different views of reality, employer groups deny that there is a surplus of labor and also deny farm worker allegations that they have manipulated Federal law to insure there are excess workers to keep wages low.
"If there is a surplus of labor, I don't know of any farmer who knows where it is," said Bryan Little, chief lobbyist for the American Farm Bureau Federation.
Economists say giving farm workers a substantial raise would in theory raise the costs of fruits and vegetables just a few cents a pound. For example, only about eight cents of the cost of a lettuce that retails for $1 goes to farm workers. That means a 25 percent increase in farm wages would raise the labor cost for each head of lettuce to 10 cents from 8 cents, in theory raising the supermarket cost of a head to $1.02 from $1.
"Most Americans don't want to do this work," Mr. Villarejo said. "Yet we want our fruits and vegetables. We love our fruits and vegetables. When it comes to this work, we're just as happy to have someone come in illegally and do that work for low wages. It's a serious question that we as a nation haven't faced up to."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Many farm workers, like these in Gonzales, Calif., say their annual earnings have slipped in recent years to $8,000 from $9,000, making it difficult for them to pay for housing and other necessities for their families. (Audrey Shehyn for The New York Times) (pg. A13)

2 Networking Companies Plan a $3.7 Billion Merger
Continuing the wave of consolidation in the computer networking industry, Ascend Communications said that it had agreed to acquire Cascade Communications in a stock swap valued at about $3.7 billion. 
 The deal is intended to broaden Ascend's arsenal in the battle with Cisco Systems, the market leader in the fast-growing networking business, and comes on the heels of a similar alliance, the planned acquisition of U.S. Robotics by 3Com. [Page D4.]
 
Gold Search Ends in Distress
The curious death of a Filipino prospector is only one of many mysteries in the tale of gold pursued and dreams shattered that has Bre-X Minerals, a tiny mining company in Calgary, Alberta, scrambling to retain credibility. The company's stock soared after Bre-X claimed to find a reserve of as much as 200 million ounces of gold in Indonesia. But drilling samples taken by a prospective American partner shattered confidence in the company, and its stock dropped more than $3 billion in just minutes on Thursday. [A1.]
 
U.S. to Cut Supercomputer Support
The National Science Foundation plans to cut in half the number of supercomputer research centers it finances, to two from four. [D5.]
 
The Smoking Guns on E-mail
Billions of electronic mail, draft documents and phone-mail messages are hiding on hard drives and lurking on archival tapes. The result, according to lawyers and others who work on commercial cases, is that electronic discovery in lawsuits has become a mini-industry. [D5.]
 
Paychecks Shrink for Farm Workers
Despite decades of efforts to improve the lot of American farm workers, studies show that wages for the nation's more than 2 million farm laborers have stubbornly trailed inflation for the last 20 years. But many farmers insist that wages are keeping up with inflation. [A1.]

The Roman Catholic Church has plunged into the debate about the impact of advertising on society, discussing a host of familiar themes like truthfulness in advertising and the manipulation of children and citizens of developing countries. Some ad executives ask whether the church should be involved in the debate. Advertising. [D9.]

Fox, the broadcast network owned by Rupert Murdoch's News Corporation, has thrown a bolt of lightning into the television business, surpassing CBS last year in prime-time advertising revenue. Now, it may push past ABC as well this year, at least in terms of ad revenue per program, and even make inroads on the dominant financial position held by NBC. Bill Carter: Broadcasting. [D7.]
 
Disney Attempting On-Line Fees
When Walt Disney opens its new Web site for children next week, it hopes to do what few others have managed: charge a fee. Disney Online plans to bill $4.95 a month to play games, do puzzles and read stories featuring Mickey and his friends. [D5.]
 
Patenting the Jurassic Age?
Two molecular biologists have been issued a patent that appears to cover the concept of recovering not just ancient bacteria, but an array of other organisms found in amber or other resins. Teresa Riordan: Patents. [D2.]

HEADLINE: U.S. to Support Fewer Supercomputer Centers

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, March 30

 BODY:
The National Science Foundation announced Friday that it planned to cut in half the number of supercomputer research centers it finances -- going from four to two -- another reminder, if one was needed, that the time when supercomputers were hailed as symbols of the nation's technological prowess has passed.
New awards were given to the National Center for Supercomputer Applications, or N.C.S.A., based at the University of Illinois, and the San Diego Supercomputer Center, two of the original four centers that helped pioneer the networking technology during the mid-1980's that was to become the modern day Internet. Researchers at N.C.S.A. also created the Mosaic browser that led directly to the commercial explosion of the World Wide Web. 
 "There has never been a Federal technological program that has come close to showing the social and economic benefit of the National Science Foundation supercomputer center funding program," said Michael Roberts, a vice president of Educom, a consortium that promotes computing in higher education.
Officials said that financing levels for the supercomputer center program would not change significantly, despite the decline in the number of centers. Total spending for each center is planned to be $170 million over five years, with a budget of $29 million the first year.
While the most advanced supercomputers are still designed and installed first at the national weapons laboratories, the supercomputer centers have, during the last 12 years, provided a ready source of high-performance computing power for the nation's scientists.
However, the increasing power of the microprocessor chip has meant that near-supercomputer speeds are attainable inexpensively on the desktop.
The explosion of computer networking has reduced the need for supercomputing research centers, according to National Science Foundation officials. Increasingly, supercomputing power which was once available only to researchers at the weapons laboratories is now accessible to thousands of scientists via high-speed computer networks.
"When the program began in the mid-1980's we had four machines on top of four mountains," said Robert Borchers, director of the foundation's advanced scientific computing division. "If you could scale the mountain you could use a supercomputer."
He said the new program is based on the new realities of scientific computing in the Internet era in which it does not matter where computers are located.
One of the new programs, named the National Partnership for Advanced Computational Infrastructure, will be led by the University of California at San Diego, and will focus on building a generation of computers and networks that are more than a hundred times as fast as today's technology.
"The opportunity for making enormous strides is absolutely there," said Sid Karin, director of the partnership program, which will involve 37 other institutions.
The goals of the project include building a new class of supercomputer-based scientific instrumentation that will be accessible to researchers around the nation via high-speed networks, Mr. Karin said. The new program will also focus on making supercomputers and data bases available to social scientists.
The second program will be named the National Computational Science Alliance. Led by the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign it will also focus on making advanced computing available to a broad range of researchers like cosmologists and biologists.
"What's appropriate for the next 10 years starts with the assumption that we have a networked nation," said Larry Smarr, director of the N.C.S.A.
The new programs have the potential to match the impact of the original program, Mr. Smarr said.
The National Science Foundation supercomputer centers program began in 1985. In December 1995, after a task force review of the program, the National Science Board recommended reorganizing support for a new program. A summary panel met in late 1996 to make final recommendations to the foundation.
The process was not without conflict. The Cornell Theory Center at Cornell University, one of the losing centers, has filed a protest over the award process with the Government, Mr. Borchers said. Funding for the Cornell center, and for the Pittsburgh Supercomputing Center at the University of Pittsburgh, will be phased out over two years.

HEADLINE: DEATHS AT RALLY RATTLE CAMBODIA

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: PHNOM PENH, Cambodia, March 30

 BODY:
Cambodia's hard-won but increasingly tenuous democracy was shaken today by a grenade attack that killed or wounded scores of people at an opposition political rally in the center of the capital.
At least 10 people were killed and more than half the 200 people at the rally in Phnom Penh were wounded by the rapid series of explosions. It was the worst political violence since a democratic Government was established here in 1993. 
 The apparent target of the attack, the opposition leader Sam Rainsy, escaped with only minor injuries, though he was covered with the blood of a bodyguard who he said leaped to protect him. He immediately blamed one of the country's two feuding co-Prime Ministers, Hun Sen, who has frequently threatened violence against his opponents.
A leading member of Mr. Hun Sen's Cambodian People's Party, Khie Kanharith, denied that the party was involved. "The C.P.P. has long experience with politics and does not commit acts of violence against innocent people," he said.
The country's fragile democracy has degenerated in recent months into a cold war between Mr. Hun Sen and the other co-Prime Minister, Prince Norodom Ranariddh, that has occasionally burst into exchanges of gunfire between soldiers loyal to each of the two government factions.
The confrontation has eroded the legacy of a $2 billion international effort sponsored by the United Nations in 1992 and 1993 to hold elections and establish a democratic Government in this nation wounded by many years of civil war.
But political analysts said today that they were stunned that large-scale political violence had erupted so far in advance of elections scheduled for November 1998.
"This does seem ominous," said David Chandler, a scholar of Cambodian history and politics. "This much violence so soon is a surprise. It was a perilous, perilous thing to do."
Western diplomats whose countries continue to support Cambodia's corrupt and threadbare economy with large amounts of aid expressed fears that this international experiment in nurturing democracy could collapse.
"It is imperative that all in Cambodia do everything possible to avoid any future violence which could put at risk the significant progress Cambodia has made in recent years," the United States Embassy said after the killings in what seemed an almost pleading statement.
As rivalries have grown here, political interests have become increasingly complex and fragmented. Although Mr. Hun Sen appears to have been behind previous smaller attacks on opposition politicians and journalists, one Western political analyst cautioned that it was not clear who had ordered today's attack.
"I don't know whose interests are served by this," said the analyst, who spoke on condition of anonymity. "It's a murky situation. This would be exactly the kind of thing Hun Sen's advisers have been advising him not to do."
Political alliances have been in flux in recent weeks, with Mr. Rainsy entering into a prickly alliance with Mr. Ranariddh in opposition to Mr. Hun Sen with both sides courting the support of a new political player, members of the previously outlawed Khmer Rouge.
That radical Communist group, which is blamed for the deaths of more than a million Cambodians during its four years in power from 1975 to 1979, has been collapsing as a guerrilla force since last summer. Large numbers of defectors have joined the Government side and are expected to be involved in the coming election.
Outside the National Assembly building, where Mr. Rainsy had staged his rally to criticize Mr. Hun Sen and his party, even the details of the attack itself seemed unclear.
In the stunned silence that followed the explosions, nobody was quite sure whether there had been three or four grenades, whether they had come from a white car that rolled slowly past, from "two beefy men" on a motorcycle or from over the wall of the National Assembly building.
Casualty estimates varied, with Mr. Rainsy offering the highest numbers: 15 dead and 120 hurt. Hospitals said that as many as 20 of the injured were in serious condition.
The dead and injured included many of Mr. Rainsy's supporters, several journalists, passing motorists and vendors selling sugar cane and other refreshments.
At least one local journalist, Chhet Daravudh, of New Liberty News, was among those killed.
Among those seriously injured were an American, Ron Abney, 55, of Cochran, Ga., a political adviser who works for the International Republican Institute, and Zhu Changdu, the Phnom Penh correspondent for the New China News Agency.
As police officers stood by helplessly, witnesses said, journalists and other bystanders rushed the dead and wounded to hospitals.
The ground was littered with shoes and bits of clothing and blood-spattered protest placards. Severed arms and legs were collected in a bin.
"This is a terrible setback for the democratic process in Cambodia," Mr. Rainsy said, his glasses cracked and his suit covered with blood. "It bodes ill for the national elections.
"Hun Sen, the bloody guy -- he should be sentenced and arrested."
For months, Mr. Rainsy has been taunting and provoking Mr. Hun Sen, and Mr. Rainsy's supporters have in turn come under harassment.
In a radio broadcast later, Mr. Hun Sen said the police should find and arrest the attackers -- as well as the leaders of the rally, who he said shared responsibility for the bloodshed.
He also said he had ordered the Interior Ministry to prevent Cambodians with dual nationality from leaving the country.
He did not specifically refer to Mr. Rainsy, but like many Cambodians who fled abroad after the collapse of the Khmer Rouge, Mr. Rainsy holds both Cambodian and French passports.
The political rivalries that are paralyzing Cambodia's Government have long and tangled histories. When men like Mr. Rainsy fled Cambodia after a Vietnamese invasion ousted the Khmer Rouge in 1979, the Vietnamese installed a compliant Government headed by Mr. Hun Sen, a former Khmer Rouge soldier.
Mr. Hun Sen governed Cambodia through the 1980's, until the elections sponsored by the United Nations. When Mr. Ranariddh's royalist party won the elections, Mr. Hun Sen threatened civil war and a contentious power-sharing arrangement was put in place, with the two men heading what amount to parallel governments.
Mr. Hun Sen's rivalry with Mr. Rainsy, bloody as it has become, is only a sideshow to his growing confrontation with Mr. Ranariddh.

HEADLINE: Gingrich Warns China That U.S. Would Step In to Defend Taiwan

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: SHANGHAI, March 30

 BODY:
Speaking with startling bluntness on an issue so delicate that diplomats have tiptoed around it for years, Newt Gingrich said today that he had warned China's top leaders that the United States would intervene militarily if Taiwan was attacked.
As he left for Tokyo after a three-day trip to China, Mr. Gingrich said he had made it absolutely clear how the United States would respond if such a military conflict arose. 
 Referring to his meetings with China's leaders, Mr. Gingrich said: "I said firmly, 'We want you to understand, we will defend Taiwan. Period.' "
He also said, "I think that they are more aware now that we would defend Taiwan if it were militarily attacked."
Mr. Gingrich, the Speaker of the House, delivered his message, among the most forceful ever given about Taiwan by a visiting United States official, to Wang Daohan, China's chief representative in talks with Taiwan. Mr. Gingrich said he had given the same message to President Jiang Zemin and Prime Minister Li Peng in Beijing last week.
Chinese leaders offered no public response to Mr. Gingrich today. But on Friday, Mr. Jiang urged him to treat the Taiwan issue with care.
Although Chinese leaders can hardly be pleased by his remarks, just as Taiwan's leaders are likely to be thrilled by them, Mr. Gingrich said the response he had received in Beijing and Shanghai had been calm.
"We never got into an argument," he said. "They never said, 'Well you can't have that right -- that's interference.' They said, 'O.K., noted.' And then they basically would say: 'Since we don't intend to attack, you won't have to defend. Let's go on and talk about how we're going to get this thing solved.' And I think that's very healthy."
[Asked about Mr. Gingrich's statements, a Clinton Administration official in Washington said Mr. Gingrich had received briefings about American policy toward China, but that Mr. Gingrich "was speaking for himself" in his conversations with Chinese leaders.
[The White House issued a statement saying that the policy of the United States was to "meet its obligation under the Taiwan Relations Act, including the maintenance of an adequate self-defense for Taiwan," and that the Administration would maintain its "one-China policy, the fundamental bedrock of which is that both parties peacefully address the Taiwan issue."]
As a Republican leader, Mr. Gingrich is often at odds with President Clinton, yet Mr. Gingrich and Vice President Al Gore said during their visits to China in the last week that they were coordinating their messages to Beijing.
Mr. Gore stayed away from the subject of Taiwan in his public comments. After the Vice President met with Prime Minister Li, Mr. Gore's national security adviser, Leon Fuerth, said the two men had not discussed Taiwan.
In an interview on Friday, Mr. Gingrich said he had spoken with Mr. Clinton, and with Mr. Gore on several occasions, to make sure that their messages to Beijing dovetailed. At the time, he did not mention his message on Taiwan.
After visiting Japan, Mr. Gingrich and the dozen members of Congress with him will stop in Taiwan on Tuesday.
Mr. Gingrich said he agreed with the Administration's support for a one-China policy that opposes formal independence for Taiwan and preserves the hope that the island will be reunited peacefully with the mainland someday. But he insisted that the American willingness to defend Taiwan must be unmistakable.
The Clinton Administration's position on Taiwan is intentionally vague. It leaves open the possibility of American intervention, yet never spells out a firm position for fear of angering Beijing, which considers any discussion of Taiwan to be unacceptable interference in China's internal affairs.
After losing a civil war to the Communists in 1949, the Nationalist Government of China retreated to Taiwan, where it has remained in power -- maintaining a capitalist economic system -- with American suppport.
A year ago, when China test-fired missiles off the coast of Taiwan to intimidate voters in the island's first democractic presidential election, the United States sent an aircraft carrier to waters nearby. No public stand was made, but the unspoken message was clear.
Rejecting an indirect approach, Mr. Gingrich effectively said that it was best to bang a drum loudly, and that to remain vague could be dangerous.
"The worst thing that could happen would be an absence of clarity, the Dean Acheson problem of 1950," Mr. Gingrich said. In outlining the American defense perimeter in the Pacific in a 1950 speech, Secretary of State Acheson did not mention South Korea, an omission that may have encouraged the North Korean attack that began the Korean War later that year.
China has said consistently that it might use force to take back Taiwan, if necessary, much as it favors a peaceful reunification with the island. Mr. Gingrich said today that since China's position is that it might have to use force, the United States position should be that it would use force in response.
For the last 25 years, ever since the policy was spelled out in the Shanghai Communique when President Nixon visited China in 1972, the United States position has been that China and Taiwan should work toward a peaceful solution of their conflict. That effectively allowed Washington and Beijing to put off the Taiwan issue indefinitely and proceed with diplomatic and economic cooperation.
Yet China remains highly sensitive about Taiwan. Even though the possiblity of reunification seems remote to the people of Taiwan, where the standard of living and the political system are starkly different from the mainland's, Beijing has not relented in its determination. After China resumes sovereignty over Hong Kong this summer, Chinese leaders have said repeatedly, recovering Taiwan will be their next goal.
Despite the seeming intractablility of the Taiwan question, and the bluntness of his own remarks on Taiwan, Mr. Gingrich said the overall atmosphere of his talks had been warm and friendly.
"I didn't flinch, but they didn't flinch either, because I wasn't hostile," he said. "If you can be respectful but firm, you can get a long way talking with the Chinese. I think that's a key part of this. We are who we are. We believe passionately in freedom. To deal with us is to deal with a free people.
"Now having said that, they are a great nation. The more we honor their pride and we honor their national achievement, the easier it is to then get them talking openly about democracy."
Mr. Gingrich, like many other foreign visitors, was clearly taken with experiments in village-level democracy. Today he extolled Shanghai's local legislature, where he described the use of new technology as "a video-computer legislator's dream."
Mr. Gingrich arrived in Shanghai this morning in time to attend an Easter service in a Chinese church built by Americans in the 1920's.
"It was a wonderful thing to be in Shanghai celebrating the Resurrection of Christ and to have a chance to worship with Chinese and Australians and others," Mr. Gingrich said, referring to the Australian Prime Minister, John Howard, who was here too. "I think that's a good sign. We want to encourage that kind of openness."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
The Roman Catholic Church gets mixed reviews on its foray into advertising's big issues.

BYLINE: By Tamar Charry 

 BODY:
THE Pope's getting into advertising?" said one ad executive with a laugh.
In a sense, the answer is yes. Forget, for a moment, about separation of church and state, for that issue has a new twist: separation of church and capitalism, or, more specifically, separation of the Vatican and Madison Avenue. 
 What took that executive by surprise was a pamphlet put out recently by the Roman Catholic Church that plunges into the debate about the impact of advertising on society.
The 35-page pamphlet touches on a host of familiar themes: truthfulness in advertising, the preservation of human dignity, the manipulation of children and citizens of developing countries, and the influence of advertising on politics and the media. It comes down hard on familiar targets like vulgar or violent ads.
Valid issues all, but the larger issue is whether the church should even sit in on this particular debate.
"We get into a very dangerous place when the Catholic Church or any other religious organization tries to dictate content," said Donny Deutsch, chief executive of Deutsch Inc. in New York. "Let the consumer decide. The free enterprise system provides its own policing."
But others react differently. "We are in a powerful position, and we need to be careful not to abuse that privilege," said Seth Werner, co-chief executive of Publicis/Bloom in Dallas, owned by Publicis S.A.. "The way I look at it, the Vatican can say anything they want, about anything they want."
Not that the report has exactly shaken the advertising world since it was released in late February. Few advertising professionals have even heard of it, and when they were told of it, their response was often one of surprise. But it is getting around, with an article and excerpts of the statement published in Advertising Age earlier this month.
The pamphlet, "Ethics in Advertising," makes no blanket condemnation of the industry. "Many women and men professionally engaged in advertising do have sensitive consciences, high ethical standards and a strong sense of responsibility," it states. "But even for them external pressures -- from their clients who commission their work as well as from the competitive internal dynamics of their profession -- can create powerful inducements to unethical behavior. That underlines the need for external structures and systems to support and encourage responsible advertising and discourage the irresponsible."
The report was written by the Pontifical Council for Social Communications, the Vatican's communications department. It was released in six languages, and was distributed among bishops worldwide. It is just one of many documents on social issues, like a 1992 report on changes in communication technology and policy and a 1989 report on pornography and violence in the media.
The impetus for this one came from Catholic bishops and cardinals, as well as advertising executives, said Archbishop John P. Foley, an American and president of the council. It was intended as a balanced assessment, and does mention several useful aspects of advertising.
"Advertising has a profound impact on how people understand life, the world, and themselves, especially in regard to their values and their ways of choosing and behaving," the report states. "These are matters about which the church is and must be deeply and sincerely concerned."
Many business ethicists agree that this is fair game for the Vatican.
"The Vatican not only has every right to do this, but it is fundamentally what religion is about," said Gene Laczniak, a professor of marketing at Marquette, a Jesuit university, and author of a book on marketing ethics. "All religious organizations are about values. Espousing those values in a way that has implications for society is to be expected, and in some ways commended."
Others, though, think the church statement is more likely to sour the industry to issues of social responsibility.
"The first thing the advertising community will say is, 'Who is the Vatican to make statements about capitalism?' " said Tim C. Mazur, vice president of the Council for Ethics in Economics, based in Columbus, Ohio. He said the argument that advertising decisions should be based on moral standards is irrational to the capitalist business audience. "To that extent, it may turn off this audience more than they were before," he said.
To a degree, he is right. Some executives do go on the defensive when faced with the statement.
"They're playing in the wrong ballfield," said O. Burtch Drake, president of the American Association of Advertising Agencies in New York. "There are very strong governmental regulations in the United States, particularly on the basis of honesty. You can't say anything deceptive without the F.T.C. getting on your case."
While that may be true in the United States, this document was intended for many countries, including those that do not have such strict regulatory systems. And in addition, pointed out Professor Laczniak, government intervention in this country does not necessarily insure moral conduct at all times.
"Unfortunately, I think there are a lot of business people in this country who on Saturday or Sunday profess the values of their religious community," he said, "but when they go to work on Monday, it's the dog who snaps fastest who gets the bone."

INTERNATIONAL A3-9
 
Gingrich Issues Warning To Chinese Over Taiwan
Newt Gingrich, the Speaker of the House, said he had warned China's top leaders that the United States would intervene militarily if Taiwan were attacked. The issue is so sensitive that diplomats have tiptoed around it for years, but at the end of a three-day trip to China, Mr. Gingrich said he had bluntly stated how the United States would respond. Chinese leaders offered no public comment, and a Clinton Administration official said Mr. Gingrich "was speaking for himself." A1
 
Grenade Attack in Cambodia
A grenade attack killed at least 10 people and wounded more than 200 at an opposition political rally in the Cambodian capital, Phnom Penh. The apparent target, the opposition leader Sam Rainsy, escaped with only minor injuries. He immediately blamed one of the country's two feuding co-Prime Ministers, Hun Sen, who has frequently threatened opponents. It was the worst political violence since a democratic Government was established in 1993. A1 
 
Palestinian Protest Contained
Palestinian police maintained a tight grip on Palestinians who rioted for an 11th day across the West Bank, preventing a dangerous increase in violence on an annual day of protest by Arabs. Both the Palestinians and Israelis continued their tough talk, and Arab officials meeting in Cairo threatened to revive economic and political isolation of Israel. The United States special envoy is expected to prepare a new plan to break an impasse in the peace talks. A1
 
Italy Blamed in Refugee Deaths
An Albanian man who survived the sinking of a boat carrying refugees wept in Brindisi, Italy. Senior Albanian officials blamed Italy for the deaths of as many as 79 Albanians, accusing one of its navy vessels of deliberately ramming and sinking the boat. The episode is expected to complicate Italy's effort to assemble a multinational force to deliver food, medicine and other aid to Albania. A3
 
India Coalition Nears Collapse
Prime Minister H. D. Deve Gowda's nine-month-old minority Government was on the verge of collapse after the Congress Party abruptly withdrew its support, thus depriving his 13-party coalition of a parliamentary majority. The move appeared to set the stage for a new period of political uncertainty in India, and possibly even for a new general election less than a year after the inconclusive election that brought Mr. Deve Gowda to power. A9
 
School Shooting in Yemen
A man with an assault rifle killed a school headmistress, a teacher and six children in an attack on hundreds of pupils at two schools in the Yemeni capital, Sanaa, a security official said. Eleven other pupils from two neighboring schools and three other people were wounded. The gunman was arrested after a shootout with security forces. Witnesses described him as a disgruntled bus driver who had been dismissed. (Reuters)
 
NATIONAL A10-13
 
Farm Workers Poorer Despite Years of Action
Wages for farm laborers have fallen as much as 20 percent over the past two decades after accounting for inflation. Despite years of organizing, protests and other actions, many of the workers still cannot afford adequate housing and other necessities. Experts attribute the problem to immigration and the weakness of labor unions. In some areas, piece rates and wages have fallen even without considering inflation. A1
 
The Inexplicable on Day of Faith
On a day when the Christian world celebrated a Resurrection, priests and ministers across the country dealt with the suicide of 39 members of a cult who expected to be brought back to life aboard a U.F.O. they thought was hidden behind the Hale-Bopp comet. The adherents of the Heaven's Gate cult were remembered directly and indirectly in Easter services throughout the country. Attending church in Cincinnati was Donald McCurdy, the brother of one of the victims, Yvonne McCurdy-Hill. A12

Winds Hurt 40 at Space Center
With little warning, a fierce storm whipped through the Kennedy Space Center in Florida, injuring about 40 tourists and causing minor damage to the launching site, a NASA spokesman said. Winds gusting up to 52 miles per hour hurled rocks and debris against three double-decker tourist buses, shattering windows and showering glass on the occupants, said Joel Wells, a spokesman for the National Aeronautics and Space Administration. Robbers Shoot Bank Teller
Two bank robbers shot a teller and got away with $20,000 at a Bank of America branch in Danville, Calif., the police said. The robbers fired a warning shot before ordering customers to lie on the floor, a police sergeant said.They then ordered two clerks to take them into the vault to try to open the safe and deposit boxes. While one robber was in the vault, the other grabbed bills from cash drawers, the sergeant said. When the clerk could not get the safe and deposit boxes open fast enough, one of the thieves shot the teller in the arm, but did not seriously hurt him. The pair got away in a brown BMW. (AP)

Officers said they were looking for a third suspect in the beating of Mr. Yee, who died on Friday. Mr. Yee was waiting for a train when a man in his early 30's began encouraging two boys to attack, the authorities said. Mr. Yee was hit, kicked and stomped. (AP)

The woman, Joycelyn Bennett, 27, is accused of killing her son, Daytwon, who along with his four siblings had been in and out of foster care for several years. The other children were found unharmed. Domestic Danger for Women
More women in New York City are killed by their husbands or boyfriends than in robberies, sexual assaults, drug violence, random attacks or any other crime in cases for which the motive is known. That conclusion, long suspected by experts on family violence and criminal justice, has been confirmed by a study of every woman killed in the city over five years, one of the first studies of its kind in the country. The stock market has fallen into a dizzying stupor, experiencing its biggest declines since last summer, after the Dow Jones industrials rose to more than 7,000 points earlier this year. A steep selloff at the end of last week raised the question of whether stocks might be in for a more extended, significant slump, or whether one of the greatest bull markets in history would continue after only a relatively minor interruption. Troubled U.P.I. Shifts Focus
The latest owners of United Press International, the news wire service that has been through three owners, two bankruptcies and one court-ordered liquidation since 1986, hope to find better success by satisfying the country's appetite for bite-sized bits of news. U.P.I. is strengthening its radio broadcast arm while developing a kind of niche journalism, selling fragments of news to customers ranging from a San Francisco paging service that puts headlines on pager screens to a Kentucky enterprise that wants to flash headlines in small streaming lights installed in bars to religious broadcasters who have begun adding news broadcasts as a way of keeping their listeners tuned in. HEADLINE: Guiding Journeys Through Ancient Text

BYLINE: By SARA RIMER 

DATELINE: LEXINGTON, Mass., March 26

 BODY:
The rabbi was heading east on the Massachusetts Turnpike, a stack of Torahs in his back seat, the full moon out his window. It was three weeks before Passover, and he was talking about the Passover story: the meaning of the exodus from Egypt, the four questions, the importance of joy.
"The Hebrew word mitzraim is translated into English as Egypt," the rabbi, Alan Ullman, was saying as the signposts of the Boston suburbs beckoned, Home Depot, Ski Market, McDonald's. "The root of mitzraim is the Hebrew word, tsar, which means narrow." Ancient Egypt, he said, was for the Israelites a spiritually narrow place, and by extension, mitzraim today can mean any spiritually narrow place. "In Egypt, one can't even find God. One is worshiping a false God. Passover is about leaving Egypt." 
 Rabbi Ullman, who lives in Worcester, Mass., has 48,000 miles on his blue 1995 Saturn. He does not have a traditional congregation. For seven years, he has been a rarity: a wandering rabbi, traveling around New England, leading Torah study groups in synagogues, Unitarian churches and people's homes. Together the rabbi and his students, who are non-Jews as well as Jews, wrestle with the ancient text, interpreting it as a metaphorical map for the journey through contemporary life.
In one group, an examination of Exodus, Chapter 5, where Moses tells Pharaoh to let the people go, led to a conversation about power. "Moses knows Pharaoh has the economic, political and military power," Rabbi Ullman said, recalling the discussion. "But Moses knows that there is another kind of power, the power of the spirit."
Rabbi Ullman, whose schedule allows him plenty of time with his wife, Melanie, and children Lara, 12, and Noah, 8, recalled that one woman in the group talked about how her husband, a corporate executive, had spent two weeks away from home on a project with 18 other men from his company. With two days off before the next project, the company made it clear that it preferred that the men not fly home for the weekend.
"All but one of them were afraid to go home," Rabbi Ullmansaid. "Here were all these high-level corporate executives who one would think had power, and in fact they're acting like Israelites who are making bricks in Egypt. We talked about what does it mean to have the spiritual wherewithal to say, 'I'm going home.'?"
On this night, the rabbi, in jeans, a flannel shirt and running shoes, was on his way to Lexington, where 12 people were meeting; the night before he had been in a Wellesley synagogue, with 14 people.
The Lexington meeting was at the home of George Foote, a lawyer, and his wife, Susan, a high school librarian. "The Torah has messages that can guide us here and now," Mrs. Foote said. "But you have to take the time to stop and know what's in it."
The group, which includes a surgeon, a pediatrician, a psychiatrist, a social worker and an engineer, talked about Passover. Leaving Egypt, the rabbi said, is about spiritual freedom. In order to leave, he said, one has to recognize that Egypt is spiritually dead.
One woman in the group related the story of Passover to her marriage, and subsequent divorce. "I had to see what was dead," she said.
Everyone has an "Egypt," Rabbi Ullman said afterward. For him, in his 20's, it was working as a senior auditor in a bank in California. "It was not just the bank," he said. "It was where I was in the bank. I knew people when I was at the bank who were very spiritual people and very good people. Of course, the bank has its regimen. I was a person who was giving that too much power."
He found satisfaction in his work outside the bank, teaching in the religious school of a congregation in San Bernardino, Calif. Eventually, he left the bank, and enrolled at Hebrew Union College in Cincinnati, the Reform movement's seminary.
He became an assistant rabbi at a synagogue in Worcester. For him, the pulpit meant too many administrative demands, and he left nine years ago to open a religious school in his house, which he still runs. Teaching gives him joy, he said.
The discussion had turned to joy the night before in Wellesley. The group had been talking about the four questions. The rabbi suggested that the fourth question, about why people recline at the Passover meal, relates to the capacity to "rest in the blessing," to "rescue joy."
One woman in the group, a lawyer, volunteered: "Joy is very hard. If you ask me the last time I felt joy I'd have to really think about it."
In his car, reflecting upon the discussion, Rabbi Ullman recalled a summer night in Jerusalem 12 years ago. He was sitting on the rooftop of a youth hostel with friends. "It was a gorgeous Jerusalem night," he said. "The scent of the city was in the air. You could feel the air on your skin. We were good friends who hadn't seen each other in a year, we had this exquisite orange vodka from Russia, and we were talking Torah." That, he said, was joy.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Rabbi Alan Ullman leads discussions on the Torah for Jewish and non-Jewish people in synagogues, Unitarian churches and people's homes. (Keith Meyers/The New York Times)
