HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
The Bell Tolls for Oslo

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
The Arab-Israeli peace process is in such dire straits today because for the first time it is threatened not by extremists, but by the very leaders who are supposed to be doing the negotiating. The enemies are all inside the tent, and they are Benjamin Netanyahu, Yasir Arafat, Bill Clinton and just about every Arab leader.
Thanks to these gentlemen, the core bargain struck at Oslo is being trashed. That bargain stipulated that at the end of the Oslo road the Palestinians would get a homeland in most of the West Bank and Gaza, and Israelis would get the security and normalcy they have craved. That bargain also implied that despite the huge advantage in power Israel holds over Palestinians, Israelis would not undertake unilateral acts on issues, like Jerusalem or settlements, that are reserved for the final-status negotiations. Unfortunately, today Israelis no longer feel they're getting security and Palestinians have lost all confidence that they'll get anything near a state. 
 Why? First of all, Mr. Netanyahu thinks he can have a politically painless peace process, in which he makes a concession in Hebron one day but then, so that there will be no political pain, starts building in a disputed area of Jerusalem the next day, to appease some right-wing followers. Yitzhak Rabin was no less committed than Mr. Netanyahu to Israel's right to build in East Jerusalem. But Mr. Rabin tried to avoid doing so in provocative cases so as not to disrupt his higher goal of bringing the Arab-Israeli conflict to an end. He knew Israel controlled Jerusalem. He didn't need to prove it over and over. Not only does Mr. Netanyahu engage in provocative building, but he does so without even saying to Mr. Arafat first: "Look Yasir, we're partners. I have to do this to shore up my political base. But what can I give you to get us through this?" Instead, Mr. Netanyahu embarrassed Mr. Arafat, and then had the nerve to complain that Mr. Arafat wouldn't take his phone calls. That's flat-out dishonest.
Still, however maladroitly Mr. Netanyahu handled the Jerusalem case, he did withdraw from Hebron a month earlier. That was a hard thing and the Arab leaders (except King Hussein) have done nothing -- zero -- hard in return to give Mr. Netanyahu some credit. That's flat-out pathetic.
As for Mr. Arafat, he responded to Mr. Netanyahu's provocation in Jerusalem with his usual one-two punch -- terrorism, followed by a trip far, far away to Bangladesh. There can be no place for terrorism in this process. None. Israelis will not tolerate a situation in which every bump in the road is greeted with a suicide bomb. But let's not forget one thing: The reason Israel's security chiefs warned Mr. Netanyahu that his actions in Jerusalem could trigger violence was because they understood that the Palestinians, having no other means to stop Israeli bulldozers, would resort to terrorism.
I still believe that a majority of Israelis feel Oslo brought many benefits. It has extracted Israel from a demoralizing occupation in Gaza. It has established cooperation between Israeli and Palestinian security services, albeit rocky at times. It has delivered a peace treaty with Jordan and trade with many countries that once shunned Israel, contributing to Israel's economic boom.
But none of this will count if the core bargain is not restored -- if Israelis don't get security and the Palestinians a homeland. And that core bargain certainly can't be restored if Mr. Netanyahu thinks he can play peacemaker one day and provocateur the next. And it certainly can't be restored if the Palestinians behave as if bulldozers and suicide bombers are moral equivalents.
The Oslo bargain also can't be restored unless Mr. Clinton and Secretary of State Albright start acting as the reality principle for the parties, speaking out loudly and clearly -- not with the mumbo-jumbo they've been using to avoid offending anyone.
Mr. President, you had it easy the first four years. You could just walk behind Mr. Rabin, let him take all the political flak while you provided the catering and got the benefits. Well, those days are over. Yitzhak Rabin was your friend. He died for this process and so far you haven't been willing to take even an ounce of political heat to keep it alive. If Oslo goes down, Mr. President, your name will also be on the death certificate. Be sure of that. If you want to be remembered as a peacemaker, then you too have to pay. Everybody pays, or this is the end.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Ban Expiring, Haitians Fear Return of the Tontons Macoute

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti

 BODY:
Ten years ago, Haiti approved a new Constitution prohibiting officials of the recently deposed Duvalier family dictatorship from seeking public office. But that ban is expiring, and the prospect that former leaders of the Tontons Macoute may soon re-enter public life has the Haitian Government scrambling to take countermeasures.
An old lawsuit against members of the Duvalier family and their entourage has been revived, and President Rene Preval has warned of a renewed threat from the Tontons Macoute, the Duvaliers' dreaded private security force, even as his aides seek to reassure the population that they have nothing to fear. 
 The Duvalier family first took power here in 1957, when Francois Duvalier, known as Papa Doc, was elected President and initiated a period of brutal and corrupt rule that lasted three decades. Early in 1986, Jean-Claude Duvalier, the dictator's son, known as Baby Doc, was overthrown in a popular rebellion and fled to France with a large chunk of the state treasury.
On March 29, 1987, a Constituent Assembly approved a new Constitution denying political rights for a decade to "any person well-known for having been by his excessive zeal one of the architects of the dictatorship." The 10-year ban also applied to all those "denounced by public outcry for having inflicted torture on political prisoners" or "for having committed political assassinations."
For several weeks now, the country has been awash with nervous rumors that Jean-Claude Duvalier himself plans to come back to Haiti. Diplomats here say that is extremely unlikely: he still faces criminal charges here.
Nevertheless, Mr. Duvalier's former father-in-law, Ernest Bennett, returned recently. At a restaurant in the upper-class suburb of Petionville, Mr. Bennett, who used his ties to the Duvaliers to reap a fortune, even led a group of diners in cheers of "Long Live Duvalier!"
Mr. Bennett is reported to have left Haiti in late February, after the Government filed a suit against Mr. Bennett, his daughter Michele (now estranged from the former dictator), and 36 other former officials and sympathizers of the Jean-Claude Duvalier dictatorship, charging that they had misappropriated public funds.
"This came up because these guys started coming back, which I thought was pretty brazen," said Ira Kurzban, an American lawyer who represents the Haitian Government in efforts to recover assets the Duvaliers sent abroad. "There was no sense of anyone being brought to justice who was a figure in any of this, which is why the Bennett suit is important."
The Haitian Government, which estimates that the Duvaliers and their associates pilfered more than $500 million from the treasury during their last five years in power, has already won judgments of $11 million against the Duvalier family in American courts. They have been able to retrieve little of that, though Mr. Kurzban said the Government expected to take possession soon of an apartment of Michele Bennett's on the East Side of Manhattan.
Whether pro-Duvalier forces still represent a threat to public security and political stability here, however, is a subject of considerable debate. In a recent speech, Mr. Preval evoked the specter of their return to rally support for his Government and its unpopular policies.
"We have a police force that is young, short of equipment and beleaguered," Mr. Preval said. "If you don't help them, the Tontons Macoute, who are watching from the sidelines, will stage a coup d'etat, just as they did in 1991, which will force us to go into hiding."
But some of Mr. Preval's supporters argue that it is important not to overstate the possibility of a resurgence of the Tontons Macoute.
"As a political force, they are completely finished," said Gerard Pierre-Charles, leader of the Lavalas Political Organization, the center-left political party that has the largest bloc in the Haitian Parliament. "The Duvalierists have no leadership, no ideology and no army."
The director of the United Nations mission here, Enrique ter Horst, described the neo-Duvalierists as "a spent force" politically. But it does not necessarily follow, he cautioned, that they no longer constitute a security threat.
"There are still people from that part of the political spectrum who do meet and have dreams of organizing a coup d'etat, which only shows how gravely they are misinterpreting the new political situation," Mr. ter Horst said.
Still, some public officials said that they had begun to detect a certain nostalgia for the Duvalier era among groups dismayed by the lack of economic growth since democracy was restored in 1994.
"The population is so disappointed by the current rulers of the country that some of them say, 'It wasn't so bad when we had a dictatorship,' " said Jean-Robert Sabalat, a Foreign Minister under President Jean-Bertrand Aristide who is now a member of the Senate.
"After the Macoutes were defeated, they dispersed throughout the country," he said. "But when they see misery growing and disagreement among the people in power, they get more confidence in themselves and say, 'The people who used to criticize us are doing worse, so why don't we come up again?' "

HEADLINE: National News Briefs;
Muslim Wins $3 Million In Suit Over Dismissal

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: FREMONT, Calif., March 30

 BODY:
A former United Airlines worker who claimed he was discriminated against because of his Muslim beliefs and then dismissed for complaining has been awarded nearly $3 million in a wrongful termination suit.
An Alameda County jury ruled for Ahmad Abu-Aziz on Friday after a two-week trial. United said it would appeal. Mr. Abu-Aziz, a 29-year-old Jordanian who is a permanent resident of the United States, won $2.67 million in punitive damages, $246,000 in economic damages and $50,000 for emotional distress. 
 He began working for United as a customer service agent at Oakland International Airport in 1994. He said fellow workers made anti-Muslim and anti-Arab comments and that he got unfair work assignments.
After he complained to his supervisors, he was dismissed because co-workers accused him of drinking and stealing company property, both of which he denied. United officials said they had five witnesses.

HEADLINE: CUNY Critics Should Blame Themselves

 BODY:

To the Editor:
The spate of New York politicians imputing slow progress and scant success to City University of New York students smacks of "blame the victim" (news article, March 27). The carpers should blame themselves for raising tuition while slashing financial aid: a double whammy that forces more students to work longer at outside jobs with less time for study. 
 The politicians have also been slashing away at teachers and books, making it harder for students to complete their education in a timely and adequate manner. Faced with such obstacles, the students are to be commended for their commitment to excellence. Two from my own department have just won full graduate fellowships to Harvard. Others are finishing graduate study at Columbia and Yale.
In spite of political fashion, disinvestment in education is shortsighted. Educated citizens and producers will be essential for New York to keep pace in the new century as an economic and cultural player. Nor will a narrow technological training suffice. I have been hearing from business executives that new recruits must be versed in analytical reading, critical thinking and effective communication -- the meat of liberal arts.
Politicians who complain of falling standards should ask whether they themselves meet the standard of support for the liberal education our future requires.
 
JOHN B. VAN SICKLE
New York, March 27, 1997
 
The writer is a professor of classics at Brooklyn College, CUNY.

HEADLINE: Women's Killers Are Very Often Their Partners, A Study Finds

BYLINE: By PAM BELLUCK 

 BODY:
More women in New York City are killed by their husbands or boyfriends than in robberies, disputes, sexual assaults, drug violence, random attacks or any other crime in cases where the relationship between the murderer and victim is known.
That conclusion, long suspected by family violence and criminal justice experts, has been confirmed by a study of every woman killed in New York City over five years, one of the first studies of its kind in the country. 
 To piece together a portrait of these women, researchers from the New York City Department of Health spent nine months teasing clues out of the dispassionate documents that are the residue of murder: autopsy findings, crime scene reports, witness statements, toxicological and sexual assault tests, ballistics reports, and descriptions of stab wounds and strangulation marks.
For a study that is yet to be released, researchers looked at files on each of the 1,156 women age 16 and over killed in New York City from 1990 through 1994. In only 484 murders could investigators determine the killer's relationship to the victim -- friend, stranger, acquaintance or relative. From that narrower pool, researchers learned that nearly half of the women were killed by current or former husbands or boyfriends, a much higher proportion than expected. Nationally, the figure is 40 percent, compared with 6 percent of men who were killed by wives or girlfriends.
The portrait derived from the homicide files is telling in its details of how the women lived and graphic in the specifics of how they died. More than half the women killed in the city during those years died in private homes, usually their own. In the majority of the cases in which the killer was identified, the murderers were people they knew.
When they were killed by their husbands, one-third of the time the women appeared to be trying to end the relationships. In one-quarter of the cases where husbands or boyfriends were the killers, children were also killed or injured, or they watched the murders or found their mothers' bodies.
And, unlike men, who are killed most often by guns, women "are very likely to be punched and hit and burned and thrown out of windows," said the leader of the study, Dr. Susan A. Wilt, the director of epidemiology and surveillance for the Health Department's injury and prevention program.
"We were surprised by the degree to which some of these murders just spoke of enormous rage. Some women were stabbed and also strangled," she said. "Some women were beaten, and they weren't dead yet, so they were thrown out of the window."
"What's very clear is that female homicides are so different from male homicides," Dr. Wilt added, noting that her research staff attended monthly support group sessions and deliberately avoided looking at gruesome autopsy photographs while studying these deaths.
Understanding the women's deaths, city health officials say, will help map a plan for hospitals, law enforcement officials, clinics and community organizations to identify and protect women at risk.
The study also provides concrete information at a time when domestic violence policy is a heated issue, with victims' advocates pressuring city and state officials for more help for battered women and more serious penalties for their batterers.
"This study shows that to protect women from the threat of homicide we have to develop different strategies," said Dr. Margaret A. Hamburg, the New York City Health Commissioner.
"On-the-street police strength is not effective when the majority of women are killed in an individual's home," Dr. Hamburg said. "Health care providers should be trying to identify women who are at risk for domestic violence because often women do have early contact with the medical system. And we need to reexamine laws that apply to domestic violence."
Two clear patterns that emerged were particularly worrisome to the researchers.
Two-thirds of the domestic violence killings were in the poorest boroughs, the Bronx and Brooklyn, and three-quarters of the women killed by husbands and boyfriends were black or Hispanic. These findings run counter to the long-held public impression that "domestic violence knows no class and color boundaries," said Dr. Jeff Fagan, director of the Center for Violence, Research and Prevention at Columbia University's School of Public Health.
"The myth of the classlessness of domestic violence is one that has persisted since the 1960's," Dr. Fagan said. "The truth is, it is a problem of poverty, associated with other characteristics like low marriage rates, high unemployment and social problems. Whatever we've done to prevent domestic violence has been more effective for white women and we have to figure out how to make it apply to poor women in poor areas."
Also surprising was that in nearly one-third of the cases where husbands and boyfriends killed women, the men also tried to kill themselves.
"Now, when a man comes into a psych clinic and acts like he's suicidal, we should know to screen him to see if he has a family and what kind of relationship it is," Dr. Wilt said. "And women who come into an emergency room because they get hurt by their intimate partners should be made aware of the risk to children in the house."
The fact that one-third of the women killed by their husbands were still married but not living with them suggested to Dr. Wilt that these women were trying to leave the relationships at the time of their deaths.
"At a certain moment when a relationship unravels, it really gets very volatile and dangerous," said Dr. Richard Gelles, director of the Family Violence Research Program at the University of Rhode Island.
Over all, many fewer women are killed than men -- during the five years in the study, the proportion was nearly seven to one. As total crime decreased in those years, murders of women decreased also, but not as sharply as those of men. At the same time, the number of domestic violence murders increased slightly.
Public health officials worry that the incidence of domestic violence has not dropped as precipitously as the overall crime rate in the past few years following the five-year period of study. And they are finding that domestic violence rates are not responding to the most common crime fighting techniques.
"Intimate violence is not amenable to community policing or the death penalty," Dr. Gelles said. "If you crack down on crack cocaine, you don't affect the domestic homicide rate."
Out of the files and forms and documents used in the study sprang vivid pictures of how some of the women met their deaths.
A 35-year-old woman in Brooklyn was stabbed 12 times with a hunting knife and bludgeoned with a baseball bat by her boyfriend in a case described as "very typical" by Dr. Wilt, who said confidentiality concerns kept her from providing names or identifying details of the victims. The Brooklyn women's boyfriend killed her on a night he had come home drinking and accused her of having an affair. He also stabbed the woman's sister, who survived.
In another typical domestic violence case, Dr. Wilt said, a 28-year-old woman in the Bronx was killed by her live-in boyfriend after they argued about her leaving him. Neighbors said that the boyfriend had abused the woman before and that there was a protection order barring him from seeing her. The boyfriend hit the woman in the face, shot her with a .38 caliber handgun and then shot himself in the head. When the police arrived, they found two children, 5 and 7, in the home with the bodies.
The researchers also looked, of course, at the highly publicized murder cases of those five years, but said that many of them were not typical. Those included the deaths of 87 clubgoers (31 women, 56 men) in the Happy Land Social Club in the Bronx in 1990, which was set on fire by the ex-boyfriend of an employee. And the 1993 murder of Danielle Almonor, a Federal probation officer shot in the back of her head by her estranged husband at the Brooklyn Family Courthouse while she was waiting for their case to be called.
Other unusual cases were the deaths of a woman whose doctor was accused of malpractice and a 16-year-old girl who died of AIDS after being infected through childhood sexual abuse.
Of the 1,156 women killed in New York over the five-year period, more than half were black, even though only one-quarter of the city's female population is black. Sixteen percent of the victims were white, although they make up nearly half the city's female population. And 29 percent of the victims were Hispanic, about the same as their proportion of all women in the city.
Black women were likely to be younger, in their 20's or 30's, and to live in poorer neighborhoods. White women were likely to be older (nearly half were over 50).
Almost one-third of all the murders of women were in the Bronx, even though only 16 percent of the city's women live in there.
Sixty-eight women were killed by their children, grandchildren or other family members who were not husbands or boyfriends. Those murders tended to be more brutal than "intimate partner" murders and other killings because women were more likely to be stabbed -- and stabbed more than 10 times.
Domestic violence victims were much less likely to be using cocaine, marijuana or other illegal drugs than other women who were killed. But they were just as likely to be drinking. Dr. Gelles said if a woman in a troubled relationship used alcohol to help her cope, she would be "less able to perceive a threat, more vulnerable to a lethal assault."
Still to be studied, Dr. Wilt said, was whether these victims had sought help from hospitals, the police or other places.
"We're very interested to know," she said, "if women didn't avail themselves of the system, or if the system doesn't work, or if the system can't save them at all."

HEADLINE: In Albany, Budget Lateness Is a Form of Realism

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO 

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 30

 BODY:
This Tuesday, barring a miracle, New York State will begin a new fiscal year without a budget in place for the 13th consecutive year and for the 19th time in 23 years -- a record unrivaled in the nation and quite possibly the world.
To any close watcher of the Albany budget game, this year's failure no doubt seems strikingly, perhaps depressingly familiar, with its bickering over projected revenues and costs, its partisan warfare, its personal animosity among negotiators. 
 Yet in its very sameness, many fiscal monitors point out, this year's impasse is in its own way extraordinary, for this reason: Like Dorian Gray, it has managed to resemble impasses through the decades, even though the world around it has undergone major political and economic changes.
A Democratic governor was unseated by a Republican. Years of dark recession have given way to years of Wall Street prosperity. Perennial deficits have been replaced by annual surpluses. (This year's surplus is $1.7 billion.) Welfare caseloads are consistently falling, not rising.
The political ground has rumbled, rattled and shifted beneath Albany, yet the budget is once again late. It is as if the local political culture dictates a hard and fast rule that no matter what the economic circumstances or the political climate, the budget must forever be enacted after the legal deadline of April 1.
"New York is in a category all by itself," said Arturo Perez, a specialist on state budgets for the National Conference of State Legislatures in Denver. "Other states have late budgets typically only in recession years. In good times and bad times, New York is late."
So, the question inevitably arises: Why? What makes New York's Legislature constitutionally unable to accomplish its most basic function in a timely manner? A few reasons stand out:
*The negative consequences of the late budget are not noticed by most people. School districts, state contractors and local governments often take out interest-bearing loans to make up for late state payments when the budget is overdue, driving up overall costs by tens, even hundreds of millions of dollars a year. But most people do not feel such costs because they are dispersed throughout a $66 billion budget.
As a result, many legislators contend that holding out for improvements to the budget plays better back home than getting it done on time. "There is a track record that nothing truly bad happens from a late budget," said Donald J. Boyd, director of the Center for the Study of the States at the Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany. "So why would you agree to a budget that you think has the wrong policies?"
*Voters rarely hold legislators or governors responsible for late budgets. Last year, when the budget was enacted a record 104 days late, all but 3 of the more than 145 incumbents who sought re-election won their races -- a re-election rate of better than 97 percent.
"The average voter doesn't make the late budget an issue," said State Comptroller H. Carl McCall, a Democrat. "The public has simply come to accept this."
*Though being a legislator is theoretically a part-time job, more legislators than ever treat it as full-time work. That means they don't have other jobs tugging at them to get home to by May or June. So if the budget fight drags into July, well, they're just doing their jobs.
"Because members are full-time they don't feel pressure to leave," said the Senate majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, a Rensselaer County Republican. "You can sense it around the place."
*As the power and size of state government have expanded over the years, the budget has become a much more complicated document than it was 25, 15 or even 10 years ago. Total state spending now stands at $66 billion, compared with $11 billion in 1977. There is more policy tied into the budget than ever, and programs for welfare, education, health care and prisons have grown and diversified through the years.
The people most responsible for negotiating the budget -- the Governor and his budget director, the Senate majority leader and Speaker of the Assembly -- offer few solutions or apologies for the problem. As much as anything, they sound resigned to it.
"It has become a cultural thing," said Patricia A. Woodworth, the Governor's budget director. "I don't know why it started 13 or 14 years ago. But once you cross that threshold, it's very hard to go back."
Mr. Silver unapologetically acknowledges that he thinks it serves his members' political needs to wait for the best deal possible. "While I would prefer things in a timely fashion, I think fairness is of equal importance," he said. "I am not going to sell out the working-class people or children or elderly of this state, just because the media or some part of the public says you have an obligation to have a budget on April 1."
The most repentant of the group is Mr. Bruno, who contends Senate Republicans tend to represent more conservative voters who care more deeply about budget timeliness. He has repeatedly pushed for legislation that would bring greater pressure on the Legislature to enact budgets on time, but to no avail.
"April 1 doesn't mean a thing to anybody," he said. "You might as well as say Christmas. It's just an accepted kind of thing. It gets to be almost humorous. I talked about an April 1 budget to one group, and everybody started laughing."
But even if the lateness has become accepted in Albany, many political analysts warn that it has a long-term, corrosive affect on the public's image of state government.
"It's delegitimizing to have late budgets," said Gerald Benjamin, a professor of political science at the State University at New Paltz. "All this government bashing today is based on the fact that government doesn't work. That we're not only paying a lot, but that when we pay a lot, we don't get what we pay for. Government as an institution has lost support in the culture for these very reasons."

HEADLINE: In His Daughter's Memory;
Grieving Father's 14-Year Crusade Helps Air Crash Victims

BYLINE: By JAN HOFFMAN 

 BODY:
The 75-year-old retired businessman with a horseshoe of white hair climbed into the witness stand in a Long Island courtroom this month, to face Korean Air Lines in a lawsuit over his daughter's death. It had taken him 14 years to get there.
In 1983, Korean Air Lines Flight 007 strayed into Soviet airspace and was shot down by a Soviet fighter pilot. All 269 aboard died, among them Alice Ephraimson-Abt, a 23-year-old graduate student in Asian studies from Saddle River, N.J.
Her father, Hans Ephraimson-Abt, endured the legal marathon that follows international aviation crashes, but not just for his benefit. As chairman of the American K.A.L. 007 association, the first such large-scale crash victims' group, he took on Congress and the airline industry, helping to win victories to insure that families of future victims will be compensated swiftly and fairly, and that their voices will be heard in debates over aviation policy.
Mr. Ephraimson, as he is called, pursued three Presidential administrations for information about one of the most notorious Cold War attacks. Families of passengers who had died on Pan Am 103, Valujet 592, and T.W.A. 800 considered his group a model for their own organizations.
Two victories have come in the last six months. In November, Congress ordered the National Transportation Safety Board to communicate with and care for families of victims. In the past, an airline assumed that role. Then, in February, United States airlines agreed to increase the base compensation customarily made to families of victims and to lower the barriers preventing speedy resolution of lawsuits.
Mr. Ephraimson's legal battle was protracted because his daughter had been on an international flight. A longstanding international aviation treaty limits a carrier's damages to $75,000 per passenger, unless the family proves that the airline's misconduct was willful. That criminal-like standard has been overcome less than a dozen times.
Under the February agreement that Mr. Ephraimson helped to broker, the base payment by airlines involved in future crashes will increase to $139,000. Families seeking more money will no longer have to prove willful misconduct. Instead, the burden will be on the airline to show it was not negligent.
Department of Transportation officials and industry leaders have publicly thanked Mr. Ephraimson. "Because he has tenacity and unbelievable patience, he has become terrifically effective," said Jeffrey N. Shane, a former assistant secretary of transportation. "And he made us better public servants. What a monument to a daughter."

A Gentle, Cheerful Man, A Relentless Fighter
For the first six years after Flight 007 went down, lawyers in the United States for 108 victims' families worked to show that Korean Air Lines had willfully erred because the plane, en route to Seoul, had gone off course three minutes after leaving Anchorage.
Information about the disaster was scant, as East and West traded accusations of espionage. No government raised the plane, which lies in 574 feet of international water off the coast of Sakhalin Island. Bodies were never recovered.
Forgotten in the furious political posturing were the victims' survivors. Gradually, the American families stumbled toward one another. If their cause was to remain alive in courts and diplomatic circles, "we would have to do it ourselves," said Mr. Ephraimson, who became the group's chairman in 1985.
He was a logical choice, because he had the temperament and financial means for a siege. Although his family home and fortune in Berlin had been plundered by the Nazis, he re-established himself here as an adviser to the West German Consulate General, developing a cheerful but gently relentless style of diplomacy. Later, he became an international business consultant. Divorced since the 1970's, he settled in Saddle River, where he raised two daughters and a son, and cared for his parents.
Accustomed to fussing over others, he became the Flight 007 families' one-man research librarian, therapist, cheerleader and unofficial grandfather. Every month, he would send typewritten newsletters as long as 50 pages to those who could not travel to the meetings in one family's East Side Manhattan apartment.
There, a core group of 20 would peruse the agenda Mr. Ephraimson had prepared. Which lawmakers could they write? How could they get documents declassified?
He encouraged members to talk about grief, money woes, legal problems. "Men in the legal profession tended to intimidate the widows and patronize them," recalled Renay Bevins, whose husband was on the plane. "So Hans would say to us: 'Why don't you ask your attorney this or that?' as if to say, 'We're really going to blow their socks off!' "
Always dressed in three-piece suits, Mr. Ephraimson, who is fluent in three languages and retains such Old World mannerisms as hand-kissing, proved a master at keeping the group on track, its members said.
Since 1983, he has traveled to Washington more than 250 times. Scarcely 5 foot 3, with a shiny pate, bright blue eyes and a German accent, he charmed his way into offices by memorizing the birthdays of dozens of receptionists, eventually meeting with 149 officials in the State Department alone.
"Hans would often open meetings with either a small but symbolic gift for someone or an announcement that the day happened to be an anniversary of a benchmark in his group's litigation," said Anne McNamara, general counsel to American Airlines. "Or that we should dedicate our work today to one family, 'so that for them we will have a good result.' "
By 1989, a Federal jury in Washington, before which 108 cases had been consolidated, found Korean Air Lines liable for willful misconduct; two years later, a Federal appeals court agreed. It was now eight years after the plane went down. No American plaintiffs had yet received any money. Finally, it seemed as if the plaintiffs would be able to be compensated, through settlements and trials, for more than $75,000.

A Listener Who Can Calm
Over the years, Mr. Ephraimson advised other families afflicted by air disasters about forming groups. But he found his legislative soul mate in M. Victoria Cummock, whose husband died in the explosion of Pan Am Flight 103 over Lockerbie, Scotland.
Ms. Cummock, tall, brash and given to all-American hyperbole, towered over Mr. Ephraimson, the epitome of quiet, behind-the-scenes diplomacy.
They wanted to expand carrier liability and to make sure that in the aftermath of an air disaster, families would be quickly informed. The two loped through the halls of Congress, testified before subcommittees, worked on national aviation commissions, and popped up, often uninvited, at industry conferences.
Some airline executives would sneer at them; many lawyers who sue airlines considered them naive meddlers.
"Hans has somehow ingratiated himself with the Government so they accept him as a representative of victims' families," said Lee Kreindler, who represented many victims of Pan Am 103 and K.A.L. 007. "But he's not qualified: he's not a lawyer and he doesn't really understand the cases."
This July, at the invitation of the National Transportation Safety Board, which investigates accidents, the pair spent two weeks on Long Island helping families of passengers who had been on T.W.A. 800. Over the summer, his phone bill would include more than a thousand calls he made on behalf of the T.W.A. families.
"Hans was a very calming influence for the European families," said Peter Goelz, a top official at the safety board, who relied on Mr. Ephraimson's fluent French. "He would just sit down at a table and introduce himself. He's a great listener and the most unruffled guy: he has an amazing capacity to listen to anger and handle it."

A President Lends a Hand
Meanwhile, Flight 007 trials started and stopped, as the airline appealed jury verdicts around the country. Eventually, the United States Supreme Court wrote two opinions on the litigation.
Changing political winds also affected the cases. With the end of the Cold War, thousands of Soviet archives were made public. Buried in the vaults were Flight 007's data recorders. Unknown to the world, they had been retrieved by Soviet divers six weeks after the plane crashed.
Mr. Ephraimson began plucking at a hundred diplomatic elbows: Four Senators wrote to Mikhail S. Gorbachev, the Soviet President. In October 1992, Mr. Ephraimson led a delegation of families and State Department officials to Moscow, where the Russian President, Boris N. Yeltsin, handed him transcripts from the plane's last half-hour.
In 1993, the International Civil Aviation Organization, a United Nations agency, concluded that the downing had been caused by mechanical and human error by the Soviet Union and Korean Air Lines. The airline tried unsuccessfully to undo the willful misconduct verdict. Trials were halted for two years.
One struggle has been over the type of damages survivors can seek. In 1996, the United States Supreme Court said that because the plane crashed in international waters, admiralty law , which governs shipping, applied, not aviation law. And so compensation was limited to a victim's economic value, and claims for grief were precluded. Children and students like Alice Ephraimson-Abt are worth less than wage-earning spouses, because they had not yet started working. According to George N. Tompkins Jr., a lawyer for Korean Air Lines, Flight 007 settlements range from $75,000 to $10 million, for the family of a Korean businessman.

One Victory, But a Continuing Battle
About 16 families have still not been compensated. This month, Mr. Ephraimson's case finally went to trial. Years of rulings had left him clinging to a sliver of legal turf. He could not, for example, ask a jury to punish Korean Air Lines for the actions of its pilot or the years of delay.
All he could seek was the economic worth of Alice, his first-born daughter. When the plane crashed, she was traveling to China to study Mandarin, her career undecided. Mr. Ephraimson's lawyer since 1983, Gerald H. Baker, could ask the jury only for home care services that Alice might have rendered her elderly father.
Several months earlier, the airline had offered Mr. Ephraimson about $575,000, the amount offered by Pan Am for college students who died in the Lockerbie crash. Knowing that one student's family had won $1.2 million at trial, he refused.
He wanted to gamble on an untested legal theory. Judge Thomas C. Platt of Federal District Court in Uniondale, L.I., permitted him to raise a second question, unaddressed by the Supreme Court. The jury, the judge ruled, could decide if Alice had been conscious as the plane went down, and award damages for her suffering.
The trial lasted five days. After the verdict, Mr. Ephraimson rushed over to the jurors.
Because Mr. Ephraimson's mother died at 97 and he appeared vigorous, the jurors found that he would not require home care services until he was 82. They awarded him $135,000. Gravely, he thanked them for their optimism.
But they also said they believed that as Flight 007 spiraled down, Alice Ephraimson would have been conscious of her impending death for at least eight minutes. They awarded him an additional $2 million.
Crowding around him, jurors expressed their sorrow at his loss, and their fervent hope that with their verdict, his 14-year battle would be over.
Because of appeals and the unresolved question of pain-and-suffering compensation, he explained gently, the end is still years away.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Hans Ephraimson-Abt, whose 23-year-old daughter was killed in the downing of Korean Air Lines Flight 007 in 1983, took on Congress and the airline industry to help win fair and swift compensation for families of crash victims. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times); Families of victims are taken to the waters near where Flight 007 was shot down in 1983. (Associated Press) (pg. B1); Hans Ephraimson-Abt pursued three Presidential administrations for information about the downing of Korean Air Lines Flight 007. Above, a meeting with President Reagan and First Lady Nancy Reagan. (pg. B4)
 
 
Chart: "K.A.L. Flight 007: Litigation Continues"
 
Sept. 1, 1983 -- Korean Air Lines Flight 007, flying from Anchorage to Seoul, strays into Soviet air space and is shot down by a Soviet pilot. All 269 people aboard die.
 
September 1983 -- American families of Flight 007 victims, attending a memorial service in Seoul, decide to band together. In 1985, Hans Ephraimson-Abt becomes chairman of the group, now calling itself the American Association for Families of KAL 007 Victims.
 
April 1989 -- The United States Supreme Court rules that each plaintiff suing Korean Air Lines would be limited to $75,000 in damages, even though the airline had technically violated an international treaty by printing disclaimers on its tickets in smaller type than the treaty required.
 
August 1989 -- A Federal jury in Washington, hearing 108 consolidated Flight 007 cases, finds the airline liable because the pilot had shown "willful misconduct" in straying off course. This makes plaintiffs eligible to collect damages beyond $75,000. The jury also awards plaintiffs $50 million in punitive damages.
 
November 1989 -- Mr. Ephraimson testifies on international airline liability before the Senate Foreign Relations Committee.
 
May 1991 -- A Federal appeals court upholds the willful misconduct verdict, but tosses out the $50 million award, saying the treaty does not permit it.
 
October 1992 -- President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia hands Hans Ephraimson-Abt translations of tapes from the downed plane's black boxes.
 
December 1993 -- Korean Air Lines files suit in Federal court in Washington asking for a new trial on the willful misconduct finding. Judges temporarily halt individual trials, pending resolution of the issue, which is eventually dismissed.
 
January 1996 -- The Supreme Court rules that because the airplane went down in international waters, damages should be governed by admiralty law, which permits compensation only for the economic worth of the victim.
 
September 1996 -- Mr. Ephraimson testifies before a Senate committee hearing on how families of victims should be treated after airline accidents.
 
February 1997 -- The Department of Transportation and the Air Transport Association, a consortium of American carriers, agree to waive limitations on damages for families of victims in future international air disasters.
 
March 1997 -- Ephraimson-Abt v. Korean Air Lines opens in Federal District Court in Uniondale, L.I.
